A LOVER’S SENTIMENT.

FOR SISTER M. I. HORNE ON HER EIGHTIETH BIRTH -
DAY, NOV. 20, 1898.

’Tis not “too much” that reverence should be
	shown,
Where such integrity as thine is known,
Love, the “fulfilling of the law” is here.
That “perfect love” which “casteth out all fear.”
The love which faith and confidence inspire,
Which knows no guile and no unjust desire.
Lovers and sweethearts in the balmy days
Of young, sweet love, repeat each other’s prai[-]e;
And so will we; there is no higher theme
Than holy love, pure as an infant’s dream
Our “Sweetheart” of today has proven true
[-]hrough eighty years; in Kirtland and Nauvoo
She with the prophet and his people fared,
And all the trials of the Saints has shared.
So speak her worthiness whoever may,
To high a tribute none of us can pay.
Sweethearts and lovers, with rich thoughts sub-
	lime,
Oft set dear names to music or to rhyme;
And so will we in joyous measures tell
Of our great love for Mary Isabelle
’Tis not “too much,” that honor should be done,
To loyalty like thine, dear honored one
			L. L. GREENE RICHARDS.


[Column 1]
PRESS CLUB ENTERTAINMENT.

	The Utah Woman’s Press Club were de-
lightfully entertained at Mrs. Arthur
Brown’s commodious residence on Friday
afternoon, Dec. 9. The literary feature of
this charming social function was the toasts
given at table, though the luncheon was a
delicious affair in itself. The hostess gave
the subject and a quotation applicable from
some well known author. Prayer by Mrs.
Doolan. Song “America” by the club,
Annie M. Bradley accompanist.
TOASTS.
	“Benjamin Franklin.” A concise and
omprehensive sketch of Franklin was given
by L. M. Rhodes.
	“Utah Woman’s Press Club, Its Origin,
Its Present, Its Future,” Emmeline B.
Wells.
	“Utah’s Pioneer Women,” Phebe C.
Young.
	“The Freedom of the Press,” Dr. R. B.
Pratt.
	“The freedom of the press is essential to
civic and religious liberty and in the out-
come of freedom of speech and thought.
	It was said by high authority that the

[Column 2]
late grand victory in our war with Spain
was due to American individualism more
than to anything else. Freedom of the
press does not mean license. It does not
mean that a knight of the quill has a right
to vent his spleen on private individuals, or
give him the right to invade the sanctity of
the home. Public people who have charge
of public affairs are proper subjects of press
praise or criticism and the press acts as
guards on the tower, to watch over the wel-
fare of the people and the nation. Kept
within its own sphere the press is the ex-
ponent of right, the champion of liberty and
the promulgator of intelligence, and its
freedom should be maintained by every in-
telligent being, society and nation.”
	“Women as Bread Winners.” Dr. Ellis
R. Shipp.
	“Since time immemorial when Ruth went
into the fields to follow the reapers and pick
up the heads of golden grain, have some
women been bread winners. Today we
find many notable instances, women who
win bread not only for themselves, but fam-
lies, etc., and various are the occupations.
In early times, before suffrage, that bright
star of civilization shed its beneficent rays
upon the world, women were obliged in
order to gain a livelihood to resort to the
most menial toil. Hood’s “Song of the
Shirt,” and Miss Alcott’s “Song of the
Tub,” give vivid pictures. But how
changed! and this change has been wrought
by noble women! Through the heroic
efforts of such workers as our own Susan B.
Anthony and others like unto her, woman
is found in the professions and in places of
trust with competent salaries. Would that
this reformation were universal.”
	“The Club Woman,” J. A. Farnsworth.
	“Sarah E. Carmichael, Utah’s Immortal
Poetess,” Lydia Alder.
	“Fanny Burney Mad. D’Arblay,” Ella
W. Hyde.
	“Women Engaged in Journalism in Utah”
Ruth M. Fox.
	“The Woman Reporter,” Annie M.
Bradley.
	“In tracing the evolution of the newspaper
we find the development of the woman re
porter has kept pace with the strides of wo-
men in every other department of life.
“Once I lived apart, now I live with all.”
Only a few years since and the “sphere” of
the woman reporter was “Woman’s World,”
“Fads and Fancies of Women,” “Essential-
ly Feminine,” “Society,” and “My Ladies
Toilet,” Long and various the report of
foolish gossip we have read. But so nar-
row a sphere could not long satisfy, those
whose energies were undaunted. She is
now a thief and a pirate of the universe
with many fashions and addresses, who
writes the adventures her eye has seen and
her ear has heard in many styles as bright
and keen as were ever penned by her worthy
brother in the profession. We find her with,
	“An energy that searches through
	From Chaos to the dawning morning,
	Into all our human plight,
	The soul’s pilgrimage of flight;

[Column 3]
	Without halting; without rest,
	Sifting Better up to Best;
	Planting seeds of knowledge pure, 
	Through earth to ripen through heaven
		endure.”
	The natural instinct of woman, which is
much keener than that possessed by man,
seems to peculiarly fit her for the work, and
the public have found the woman reporter
as
	“Inquisitive, fierce and fasting,
	Restless, predatory, hasting,
as any one could possibly be. Let us
spread her a welcome wherever she goes.”
	Reading of Sarah E. Carmichael’s poem
“The Origin of Gold,” by Mrs. Alder. 
Singing by the club “Auld Lang Syne.”


POLITICS.

	Politics! Politics!! Party! Party!! Party
politics, till one’s soul is sick of the words
and all their paltry meanings! The parti-
sans of every party plow the whole field of
history, past, present and to come, for roots
of laudation of their party. According
to them every blessing which ever did,
does now, or ever will, descend like rain on
the just and the unjust, from a famine in
India or the sinking of a hostile fleet, to six-
teen chickens at one hatch, is directly trac-
able to the wisdom, forethought and phil-
anthropy of Our Party; and all the ills of
life from a cyclone or a labor strike to old
Brindle dead in the lucern patch, or a sliver
under your thumb nail, was just as surely 
caused by the ignorance, greed and general
mismanagement of Your Party.
	When one party puts a name at its head
that bristles with as many serpents of men-
ace as the head of Medusa, and you talk of
scratching it off, what an outcry is raised
about “going against the party.”
	When two equally good and efficient men
are named on different tickets for one office,
and you choose to take into consideration
some difference—we will say in physical
strength or financial circumctances, to
favor the less fortunate, you raise another
hullabaloo about “damaging the party!”
I say “To Blazes with party” if it makes
slaves of the voters.
	In the present campaign scarcely a wo-
man’s name appears on any ticket. Since
woman’s citizenship is part of the State
Constitution, and since women are human,
they ought to participate in the privileges
as well as penalties of humanity. Canvas-
sers have been very active in calling at the
homes of the voters to inquire if they were
duly registered, and to recommend either
self of self’s party to your kind considera-
tion.
	You perhaps take the liberty to ask if
there was no woman in that or any other
party who could be trusted to fill that or
any other office. Some professed to be as
much at a loss as the questioner, and some
indulged in wild guesses.
	After carefully sifting the bushel of chaff
you find at the bottom a single grain of—
smut, which says “A woman’s name weak-


[Column 1]
ens the party ticket, because—because—the
women voters will not vote for a woman.”
	Fellow women, is this contemptible thing
true? or is it only a dingy veil used to
screen the faces of the men voters who will
not vote for a woman?
	If it is true let us begin down at a b c,
and organize little Civil Government classes
and study the Declaration of Independence,
the Constitution of the United States, and
the condition of society and of the country
at the time these documents were framed.
As we advance we might brush up on the
Salic Law the Common Law and give spec-
ial attention to such points, as Marriage
Laws, Guardianship of Minors, Inheritance,
The Widow’s Incumbrance, and many
shining spots where light is to be obtained.
As side studies we might look into some of
the procedures of the Courts of Common
Pleas and learn by eye and ear witness how
the laws materialize in practice; and if, after
completing the course in these branches of
education, we still decline to vote for wo-
men, our case is hopeless; and our masters
should pierce our ears with awls against the
doorposts, that we may remain their ser-
vants forever.
	If it is not true that we prefer in all
cases to vote for men, if the clean sweep of
women’s names from all the tickets is only
a selfish grab of our brothers at the “hon-
ors and emoluments” of public duty, we
should express our displeasure in plain, un-
equivocal terms, and see that it does not
happen another time.
	One canvasser whom I asked concerning
this matter said, “All those men offered
themselves for those places and women did
not. I think that is the way it happened.
People must ask for what they want in this
world.”
	“Is that not branding us a nation of
office seekers?” I asked. “Well, yes, that’s
about the size of it,” he replied.
	Fellow citizens, is this true? Are we a
nation of office seekers? Is there no other
and better way of distributing public trusts
than placing them in the keeping of men or
women who importune for them? Is our
political maxim “To the victor belong the
spoils?” Where then is patriotism? Dead
or only sleeping?
	Oh, that we could be patriots without be-
ing partisans, and that principles of pure
right, not time-serving policy, could be the
mainspring of our actions, private and pub-
lic! Would we could hear more about the
general weal of our whole country, and less
about the East and the West; more about
developing the grand resources, and less
about “Corners and Giant Trusts;” more
about prosperous rural communities, and
less about millionaires.
	Would that the poor might receive such
aid and favor as would lift them out of their
misery, rather than be given additional bur-
dens of taxation and the necessity of paying
“protected” prices for the necessaries of
life. Would that “protection” would pro-
tect the weak as well as the strong, the con-
sumer as well as the producer.
	If protection be the great beneficence we
are told, then give us plenty of it, that our
crying needs may be satisfied. Why should
not timber, water, coal etc., as well as land
belong to the general government as trustee
in trust for the public? They are all mat-
ters of general and urgent necessity, and
monopoly by the few against the many
should be made impossible.
	Why should not railroads, banks and all

[Column 2]
other public service, as well as postal ser-
vice, be taken in charge by general and
municipal government, for the benefit of
the public? Such protection would pro-
tect alike the great and the small.
	Until some such desirable ends have been
accomplished let no man boast overmuch of
his party; and as for one small unit of the
body politic, Yours Truly will belong to
no party. Thus I close the door against
the temptation to become an office seeker;
and if I honor and choose to vote for Frank
J. Cannon because he had the courage to
draw back from that protection which pro
tects “only the favored few,” no party
lash can be laid on me for doing so. If I
choose to scratch the name of some candi-
date whose private character is immoral, no
one can say me nay. If I choose to vote
for a woman, I reserve the right to do so.
	Rather than be a party hack, I will be
written down to the end of the chapter,
					A MUGWUMP.


WOMANS EXPONENT


[Column 1]
AN INCIDENT.

	Mrs. S: “Will you perform an errand
for me?”
	Mrs. H: “With pleasure, anything that
lies in my power.”
	Mrs. S: “Well then, I wish you
while in the city to call on Mrs. Wells and
ask her to please discontinue the “Ex-
ponent” to me.”
	I looked at the invalid (for she had been
one for many months) in surprise, as I
knew her to be an earnest worker in the
woman’s cause, and a staunch advocate of
the “Exponent.”
	“I see you are surprised,” she said, “it
is true I will greatly miss the little wo-
man’s friend, but I cannot meet the pay-
ment since my illness and I don’t wish to
add to the great burden the editor already
carries. I fully realize she has much to
contend with.”
	As I gazed upon her pale and much
wasted face I thought with regret how
careless on this little matter many of us
were that are well and able to do. As I
reflected an incident that came under my
notice a few years back came to my mind.
	After having left the “Exponent” office I
met a lady who asked to be directed there,
and then asked if she would be able to find
Mrs. Wells in as she was anxious to get a
copy of the paper, for she dearly loved to
read it.
	In much surprise I ventured to ask her
why she did not become a regular subscrib-
er, for it was easy to be seen she was a lady
of means.
	“I was a subscriber at one time,” she re-
plied, “but now the children are growing
up it seems they must have other reading.
Such as the “Exponent” and the Juvenile
are too dry for them, they must take
the New York Ledger.” She then men-
tioned other periodicals equally as trashy,
ending by saying with a heavy sigh‘ “You
see my expenses are very heavy.”
	I replied I thought they were, but in my
own mind I communed thus: “Horrors!
can it be possible that there are many moth-
ers in Israel in this dire condition?” Since
then, I regret to say, I have found many
just such cases.
	Years may come and go, I don’t think I
shall ever completely efface from my mem-
ory the vision of the sad faced mother as
she stood talking to me that day. And it

[Column 2]
is not always we mothers that make these
grievous mistakes.
	Another incident is recalled in which a
father figures and a teacher (without the
least disrespect to the office of a teacher.)
One evening while sitting reading an inter-
esting article in the Juvenile, the gentleman
in question called for my husband to assist
him in his rounds as a teacher. While
waiting for him to make ready he spoke of
the Juvenile, as he noticed I had been read-
ing in it.
	“Yes,” he said, “it is a nice little maga-
zine, but I am not taking it now, can’t af-
ford it. My children are reading a nice
continued story in an Eastern paper we are
taking. They can hardly wait for the time
for it to come.”
	Oh! let us as mothers in Israel choose
good reading for our children, that we may
do nothing that will in any way tarnish
their characters; and remember the beauti-
ful poem which says,
“Build well the temple ye mothers of men;
For know ye are builders for glory or shame,
Know ye are building for weakness or might,
Know ye are building for darkness or light.”
IDA.


R. S. REPORT.
		PUEBLO, COLORADO.
Editor Exponent:
	Thinking you would like to hear how we
are progressing here in Pueblo, I thought I
would send you a few lines. Since we
were organized in July by Apostle John W.
Taylor and Sister Susa Young Gates we
have had splendid meetings. Our meet-
ings are held every first Thursday in the
month and the number has never fallen be-
low the first meeting, but we are constant-
ly adding one or two to our number. The
sisters have been liberal with their dona-
tions and the teachers say it is a pleasure to
visit among them. We gave the Sunday
School, numbering fifty, a Twenty Fourth
of July celebration and sent to Utah for all
the books we needed, and still we have a
nice sum in the treasury.
	I am sorry to say that our beloved presi-
dent, Elizabeth Hart, has been very sick,
and for some four weeks we did not know
whether she would ever come to another
meeting or not. Her husband did not
leave her night or day for nearly five weeks,
thinking that she might die at any time;
but she is now recovering, which we are all
thankful for.
	We had a very nice visit from Sister
Susa Young Gates in July, and she gave
us some good counsel. We have just start-
ed to make quilts. With this letter the
president is sending for the "Exponent."
		Your sister in the Gospel,
	ELLA BRANCH, Sec.


At the state convention of the Daughters
of the American Revolution at Norfolk, Va.,
Mrs. Herbert Doyle Brackett of Alex-
andria, reported that $41,000 of the amount
required for the building which the Daugh-
ters propose to erect in Washington was
now in hand. It was decided that further
contribution to the building fund would be
asked at each chapter. The building is to
be the national headquarters for the society,
and will be known as Continental Hall.


WOMAN'S EXPONENT.


OLD FATHER TIME.

Old Time is a good o[-]d man,
	What though his step be not gay,
He trud[-]es along as well as [-]e can,
He trudges along still with equal span,
		With his scythe in his hand,
		And his time-piece of sand,
	And his single lock glossy and gray.

Full many joys he bears,
	Full many griefs he brings,
Yet thinketh he naught of the load of cares,
Contained in his wallet, nor wots who shares,
		But indifferent smiles
		On the world and its wiles,
	On the beggar’s lot or the fate of kings.

The years in their flight he measures,
	As round his dial they climb;
But we, alas ! scarce value his treasures,
We’re thinking now of the seasons pleasures
		When cares we lay by,
		When we banish each sigh
	For the song and dance of Christmas time.

Hail, then, December, th ugh old and hoary!
	Fresh fagots pile on the bright fire,
And listen awhile to the comical story,
[-]he year’s departu[-]e let us crown with glory,
		By the embers bright glow,
		We’ll defy frost and snow,
	While the whistling wind joins in the choir.
SELECTED.


[Column 1]
	A LOVING TRIBUTE.

TO MY MOTHER ON REACHING HER 
EIGHTIETH YEAR NOV. 1898.

Dear Mother

	Through the blessings of a kind, loving
Father, whose children we are, you have
been permitted to remain in mortality to
the ripe age of four score years. Eighty
years of probation in this world of care,
sorrow and trouble have proven your integ-
rity and womanhood to be equal to all
emergencies.
	Born in a foreign land, fostered under an
alien government, but brought under the
royal emblems of liberty and peace in early
womanhood, you have proven your fidelity
to your country and its constitutions under
the most trying ordeals of life. True to
your convictions of right you have not
swerved from the truth as it came to your
heart through the light of the Gospel of the
Lord Jesus Christ.
	Drivings, mobbings, the trials and suffer-
ings incident to exile and pioneer life have
failed to weaken your trust in a divine
work, but have caused you to remain firm
to the last.
	We hail you as our mother, and sincere-
ly hope that the remaining years of your
stay with us will be your happiest, best
ones on earth.
	As one to whom you have given birth in
the flesh. I must respectfully bow in hom-
age to my mother, and tender a son’s filial
love and gratitude. To you and your kind,
motherly affection and interest in me in my
young days I owe much of the little good
that is within me.
	Ever solicitous for the welfare of those
whom God has intrusted to you, and wish-
ing all your children to follow paths of
honor and righteousness, you have careful-
ly guarded us against the wiles and snares
of the ungodly.
	Admonished and advised by you when
the tempter’s wiles and fascinations were
wrapped about me, I was saved from fol- 

[Column 2]
lies and sins so common to youth, and to
which I might have yielded. By your kind
hints and counsels I was taught that respect
which is due to seniors and superiors.
	Respect for the afflicted, decrepit and in-
firm and sorrow-stricken was awakened
early in my mind by your worthy example,
as well as your wise instructions.
	If aught in my life has caused you regret
or suffering, I trust you will extend pardon
therefore; that you will ever remain solici-
tous for my welfare, and still advise, coun-
sel and admonish, as your mother-love and
wisdom may dictate.
	May the choicest blessings of our Heaven-
ly Father be liberally showered upon you;
may His Holy Spirit be your constant com-
panion;may your earthly joys daily increase;
may your happiness be greatly multiplied
in the love and loyal homage of your chil-
dren, grandchildren and great grandchil-
dren;may your favor with the Lord and His
Holy Priesthood and Saints prove an anchor
of hope to your soul in all your remaining
days on the earth; may a happy reunion be-
yond the vail with loved ones gone before,
and chosen ones of our Father’s household
be your reward when you have done with
this mortal life, is the earnest desire of
Your loving son,
	RICHARD S. HORNE.


U. W. P. C.
	The regular meeting of the U. W. P. C.
was held in the “Exponent” office Nov. 30,
1898, the new president, Ella W. Hyde, in
the chair. Mrs. E. B. Wells offered prayer.
Roll call was ahswered by miscellaneous
sentiments. Minutes of annual meeting
read and approved.
	The new president then made a happy
little speech, thanking the ladies for the
honor conferred and expressing a desire for
the future advancement of the club. Under
unfinished business the matter of incorpor-
ating came up and was again laid over.
Under head of new business a note was
read from the secretary of the General
Federation asking members to subscribe for
the Biennial report of the General Federa-
tion for 1898. Ruth M. Fox was nomina-
ted by E. B. Wells and seconded by Lucy
A. Clark for director to the State Federa-
tion; carried.
	Miss Julia Farnsworth then gave a very
interesting and instructive paper on the
Phillipines, followed by discussion. A con-
cise and pithy paper on Current Events was
given by Mrs. L. M. Rhodes. A pretty
original poem entitled “Life” by Phebe C.
Young finished the program.
	Mrs. I. Cameron Brown kindly invited
every member of the club to meet at her
home, 201, E. South Temple St., at 2
o’clock on December 9.
	The program for next meeting was read,
and after benediction by Dr. E. R. Shipp
the club adjourned to meet at the rooms of
Dr. E. R. Shipp, Constitution Building,
for the December meeting.
	RUTH M. FOX, Asst. Sec.


THE LIFE AND WORK OF SUSAN B.
			ANTHONY.

		BY IDA HUSTED HARPER.

	The life and work of Susan B. Anthony
is far more than the recital of one woman’s
experience, even though that woman be the
most interesting personality of the  century.


It is a history of the great evolution of the
status of woman, in which Miss Anthony
has been the central figure. It pictures the
struggle to obtain the right for woman to
speak in public; first in behalf of the slave,
then in the interest of temperance, and at
last for her own freedom,for laws to secure to
herself the control of her own property, her
wages, her person and her children. Then
follows the long continued battle for equal
educational advantages and equal industrial
opportunities, and last and longest of all for
the citizen’s right to the franchise.
	The work consists of two large volumes,
copiously illustrated with over fifty portraits
of Miss Anthony and her colaborers, en-
gravings of her different homes, old Quaker
meeting house, and a large number of let-
ters and autographs of distinguished men
and women. The facts and dates of each
chapter have received Miss Anthony’s per-
sonal approval; and this is the only authen-
tic biography of her that ever can be written,
as the letters and documents will not be ac-
cessible to other historians. This work fol-
lows consecutively the history of one of the
greatest woman reformers. The early
chapters describe her ancestry, home, child-
hood and life in a Quaker boarding school,
the last told in her own quaint letters and
diaries. Later chapters depict her experi-
ence as a teacher related in her own charact-
eristic letters, the moving to Rochester by
canal in 1845; evolution from schoolroom
into pupil life,then follow:—Early Laws for
Women; History of the Bloomer Costume;
Canvass of the State of NewYork in Sleigh;
Anti-Slavery Work with Letters from Gar-
rison, Greeley, May, Gerritt, Smith, etc.;
Campaigning with the Garrisonians; The
Mobs; Letters from Phillips, Curtis, Pills-
bury, Lucretia Mott, Elizabeth Cady Stan-
ton, the Fosters and Others; The Marriage
and Divorce Question; The War and the
Woman’s Loyal League;Reconstruction and
Fight over Fourteenth Amendment; Kan-
sas and George Francis Train; Publishing
the Revolution; Fiftieth Birthday Celebra-
tion and Poem by Phoebe Cary; The Mc-
Farland Richardson Case; Victoria Wood-
hull;Arrest and Trial for Voting Under the
Fourteenth Amendment; Beecher-Tilton
Trial; The Woman’s Fourth of July in 1876;
Ten Years on the Lyceum Platform; A
Year in Europe; Appealing to Congress
for Thirty-Three Years; Suffrage Amend-
ment Campaign in Eight States; Practical
vs. Theoretical Religion; Pronounced Opin-
ons on Vital Questions: Thirty Years' Ex-
perience with Political Conv ntions and
Pa ties; Gathering of the Anthony Clans in
1897; Final Triumphs, Honors, Tributes,
etc.


We should always keep a corner of our
minds open and free, that we may make
room for the opinions of our friends. Let
us have heart and mind hospitality.

	Miss Olive Schreiner positively refuses to
see an interviewer. To a young reporter
who approached her on her African farm
she said:
	I heartily condemn the modern interview.
A person is ensnared into a light and
superficial colloquy upon a subject which
demands deep thought and mature reflec-
tion. If a man or a woman has a message
to issue it cannot be uttered forcefully in
one of these "interviews." ‘‘Interviews’’
are abominations which accentuate the per-
 onality at the expense of the principle.

second-class matter. 

	SALT LAKE CITY DECEMBER 15, 1898.


A CHRISTMAS THOUGHT.
   
	The agents of the “Exponent” in all the
several wards and Stakes of Zion, and also
individual subscribers indebted for the
paper are urgently requested to settle all old
accounts at the earliest date possible. This
becomes an absolute necessity on account
of the change that takes place in the man-
agement of the Deseret Evening News.
The “Exponent” will not be sent to any
subscriber after January 1899 unless pre-
paid. All delinquents will as soon as pos-
sible receive written notices of bills due in
addition to the printed matter.

	In consideration of the value of the“Ex-
ponent” to the Relief Society, and because
of the work it has done in the cause of suf-
frage as well as the general educational
help it has given the women of this inter-
mountain country,it seems scarcely possible
that women should be so careless of their
own interest as to let the paper languish for
want of support, yet the fact remains, and
it is impossible to say what the women will
do in the matter.
	With the Relief Society of 30,000 women
if one tenth of the number would take the
paper and pay cash down it could be carried
safely, yet that has been looked for, hoped
for, and waited for in vain What the “Ex-
ponent” has done to keep the society in
various Stakes and in different parts of the
world in touch with each other is of more
value than the few hundred dollars it would
take to maintain it independently. Appeal
after appeal has been made through the
columns of the paper and by those who are
its faithful supporters in public and in
private, and yet the failure to come forward
with financial aid. At one dollar per year
it seems incredible that sisters who have the
cause of Zion at heart and who know as
many hundreds do know, the generous
help the “Exponent” has given in ways al-
most innumerable, should not immediately
raise the necessary funds, through large
numbers of subscription. Beside the great
need the Relief Society has for an organ, of
its own the silk industry needs it quite as
much and both should have its support
through the liberal patronage of the women
of Zion.


There are two things in which we should
thoroughly train onrselves—to be slow in
aking offence, and to be slowed in giving it.


THOUGHTS. 

	Having been asked “Does the Woman’s
ballot purify politics?” in responding my
inclination would be to say “Yes, conditions
being fair,” but the experience I have had
proves it will take years to accomplish what
the best women desire. However, the de-
cidedly moral and spiritual element with
which woman is naturally endowed must
produce a salutary effect in the realm of
politics.
	If we go back to history we find no na-
tion was ever wrecked so long as women
wielded a powerful influence. Woman is
to be redeemed from the curse, and that is
the true interpretation of the phrase “The
nineteenth century is woman’s era”—the
work has begun and is fast going forward.
One of the most potent forces in this revo-
lution of things is the ballot. Women have
not usurped this honor—behind them is
the sentiment and the accumulation of
force, if we may be permitted the use of the
term, that the age has brought with it
which comes from a source unknown to any
except the wise, who see as poets and proph-
ets do, things that are not perceptible to the
natural eye, and hear voices that are not
heard by the multitude. The plan of re-
demption is as real and as true as was the
creation.
	Woman is one of the strongest forces in
the spiritual world of thought and of action.
Who does not know that when awakened
by trials accidents of fortune or misfortune
woman turns to God, and seeks strength
from the heavenly fountain of inspiration
through prayer and devotion, and necessa
rily by the sincerity of her purpose in all she
undertakes for the betterment of humanity
her very purity of motive and exalted views
bring about an uplifting wherever her in-
fluence spreads. It is the soul-type in wo-
man, that sacrifice of self, that obedience to
the Savior’s golden rule, “Do to others as
ye would they should do to you,” that is
ever helpful. It is that element which made
her last at the cross and first at the sepul-
chre.
	When the franchise has been extended
over more space, and embraces more people
and peoples, or more nations, then we may
be able to see what a powerful factor for the
amelioration and purification of society it
will become; and, therefore, in conclusion
let me exhort every woman in the states
that have equal suffrage, not to grow indif-
ferent to the power she has in her own pos-
session, the ballot; and because two years
or more have not worked a miracle toss
aside the weapon of defense and of author-
ity given her; but with an effort worthy the
most profound patriotism use her utmost
endeavors to induce Congress through its
legislation to carry the sixteenth amend-
ment, which has been so long pending be-
fore that Honorable Body.
	There is no other vitalizing force that can
permeate every home and appeal to every
individual citizen as the ballot can, there-
fore it must be the mightiest lever to uplift
and to regenerate the world.


[Column 1]
CHRISTMAS AGAIN.

	Here comes Christmas again with all its
delightful accompaniments and memories.
No day in all the year for the children like
Christmas with its wonders and its legends,
fairy tales and above all Santa Claus, for 
whom they long and wait, and even pray,

[Column 2]
so enthusiastic are they about his coming.
Dear old Santa Claus! What gladness he
brings to many thousands of homes, and
alas for those bereft of children or where
none ever came to bless and kindle new
life in human hearts. But the children,
Santa Claus belongs especially to them,
they watch for him carefully, continuously,
getting up at unseasonable hours to try and
surprise him and to see which way he
comes; whether over the roof with his rein-
deers and sled and down the chimney, or
through the window, or stealing in softly
when the door is open and hiding until all
are asleep in the house to make his raid as
the Brownies do. What fantastic visions
the little folks conjure up of the visits of
Santa Claus. Christmas is the day of all
days for the little folks, no other day in the
year is so attractive, not even the 4th of
July with its flags, banners, music, parades
and booming of cannon; nor yet the child’s
own birthday when he is “cock of the
walk,” so to speak. Is it because of the
delusion of Santa Claus that fires the child’s
imagination? partly so it must be. And
those of older growth, they, too, are fasci-
nated with delusions and fancies. And it
this only happened one day in the year one
might be more excusable.
	This is the day of love in every house-
hold, of peace on earth and good will to all
mankind, and every one who feels the im-
portance of the sweet charity the Savior
taught and practised should himself exer-
cise that sweet spirit of sublime and heaven-
ly grace which reflects as much upon the
giver as the receiver. The scripture says
“It is more blessed to give than to receive,”
and it is a divine truth,as many can testify.
	To know how to give acceptably is an
art in itself and one which many people do
not understand; to give ostentatiously is al-
ways an offense, even though the giver
may have the best of motives. It is true
that Christmas opens a wide door for gifts
to loved ones as well as for charity, but
nevertheless, careless giving especially of
charity, sometimes makes the recipient feel
he would rather starve. We are all, or al-
most all, too neglectful of the little courte-
sies and proprieties of life; we excuse our-
selves because there is so much to be done,
and we say this is an age of hurry and bus-
tle. But when one looks around and finds
those who do things of this sort beautifully,
in such a way that there is no possible
chance of criticism, then we wonder what is
the reason that others could not do as well.
A gift is not so much in itself as in the
manner of giving.
	Children should be taught to give in the
spirit of love and sweetness, and then there
would be greater pleasure and more satis-
faction in small things. As a people we
have grown so selfish ourselves with the
prosperity the Lord has blessed us with,
that we feel unless we can give largely we
must not give at all. Yet when we look
back and recall the early days in this valley
and the few little comforts we had for the
children at Christmas time and compare it
with the present, its loads of toys and beau-
tiful as well as useful things it seems in-
credible. And are we happier or better, or
are the children any more satisfied ? We
think not. The molasses candy, ginger
bread and dough-nuts Santa Claus brought
then were as acceptable as the best sweet-
meats and Hiler’s candy and wax dolls that
can open and shut their eyes instead of the
rag babies made by their own mamma’s


deft fingers, into which she put so many
loving stitches.
	Yes we have a different Christmas now,
and this year we have much to be thankful
for and to rejoice over. Many little ones
whose father went to battle have him home
with them for the jolly Christmas. And
some whose homes are not so blest, yet
know the war is over and look forward to
the home coming with joyful anticipation.
	There has been a bountiful harvest, the
Lord has blest His people wonderfully and
the children should be made to realize this,
and to comprehend how the Lord has pro-
vided for the needs of His people and given
them an abundance and to spare, so that
on this Christmas day in all the valleys of
this Western land, there should be no
scarcity in any home and certainly not
where there are those, blessed messengers of
relief, Bishops and sisters of the Relief
Society. The dear, old people who are so
much alone in the world, more even than
the children should be richly remembered
at Christmas. They are as fond of dainties
as the children, though they cannot keep
up the delusion of Santa Claus, and if the
children were taught to divide with the
aged people of their own locality, those who
have no grandchildren of their own to come
and bring them delicacies, it would be a
greater blessing to the children than to
those to whom they minister in innocence
and love. So let us learn the Christ lesson.
If we only give to those who can give to us
in return, we are not the true followers of
the lowly Nazarene whose birth we cele-
brate, (even though we have long since
learned that it was not the day of his birth.)
Christmas has so long been kept on this
particular day that a change all over the
world could scarcely be effected.
	This year, 1898, Christmas comes on the
Sabbath day and therefore festivities will
be observed upon Saturday or Monday, or
both as the case may be, but the children
will probably receive fruitful lessons at the
Christmas Sunday School.
	Already the city has put on its holiday
appearance, and never was there more dis-
play. Stores, shops and business houses of
all kinds are in gala dress, and the brighter,
gayer colors that have been the rage the
last year or more add to the brilliancy of
the decorations. In the midst of all this
display perhaps there is as much delusion
as attaches to Santa Claus. The home and
the fireside is always connected with Christ-
mas so closely that one invariably thinks of
the yule log, or the huge pine knots and
the joys of social or even jovial family inter-
course. “The holly round the Christmas
hearth” has become proverbial, and

	‘‘At Christmas time the open hand,
	Scatters bounty o’er sea and land.”

	It is good that we have the holidays,
what would life be without these seasons of
renewal? It makes us almost young again
to participate in such rejoicings.


WOMAN'S EXPONENT.


[Column 1]
PRESIDENT SARAH M. KIMBALL. 
	Sister Kimball whose departure from this
life took place on Thursday evening, Dec.
1, 1898, at ten minutes to six, was a prom-
inent woman in the history of the Church.
She was connected with it as a member
when a very young girl and figured in its
history to the time of her death. She was
born in Phelps, Ontario Co., New York
State, Dec. 29, 1818, but her parents moved

[Column 2]
to Kirtland, Ohio, soon after the Church
was established there, and were associated
with the Prophet Joseph intimately. Sarah
M., whose maiden name was Granger, at-
tended the Hebrew school in Kirtland, with
Joseph Smith and others who were among
the foremost leaders of the Church in early
days. As a young girl she had excellent
opportunities for advancement and cultiva-
tion, and the knowledge acquired was a
good foundation for her subsequent career
as a teacher and as a leader among women.
Her father and mother were Oliver and
Lydia Dibble Granger and well known in
Church history. ( Of their family only three
grew to man and womanhood. Fayette
Granger, the eldest, was well known in
Utah in the early days as a sort of pioneer,
and spent most of his time on the frontiers.
Farley went to California and settled and is
still residing there.)
	Oliver Granger, the father, is mentioned
in the Book of Doctrine and Covenants,
section 117, in a revelation given to the
Prophet Joseph in Far West, Missouri,
July 8, 1838, (Brother Granger was still in
Kirtland,) and there were many precious
promises made to him at that time. Not
only to Brother Granger but to Sarah were
promises made by the Prophet that were
very sacred and very comforting to her
throughout her life.
	After the Saints gathered to Nauvoo
Miss Granger became acquainted with
Hiram Kimball, who was a resident and
land owner of the town of Commerce, as it
was then called. They were married in
Kirtland, Ohio, but came to dwell in Nau-
voo where they had a lovely home, and
Mr. Kimball figured conspicuously in busi-
ness and was very prosperous.
	Sister Kimball was ambitious in good
works and especially anxious to contribute
towards the Temple which was to be erect-
ed in that city, and her zeal in this respect
was one of the factors in bringing about the
organization of the Relief Society, which
has since become such a large and flourish-
ing institution. She was present at its or-
ganization in the Masonic Hall in Nauvoo
on the 17th day of March, 1842, and in fact
the preliminary meeting was held in Sister
Kimball's own house. There are few wo-
men living who were members of the first
organization and only one, Bathsheba W.
Smith, known to the writer, who was pres-
ent when the organization was effected.
Zina D. H. Young and M. Isabella Horne
were afterwards members, and are the only
ones we can recall that are now living.
	Sister Kimball came with her husband
and family to the valley in 1850 and settled
in this city, where her residence has been
ever since. When the Relief Society was
organized in Utah Sister Kimball was chos-
en to preside in her own ward, the Fifteenth,
this was on the 9th of February, 1857, and
she has been from that time one of the most
able presidents and indefatigable workers in
the cause throughout all Zion. Perhaps
there was no more judicious economizer of
means, yet withal liberal in bestowing to
the needy and unfortunate, than she has
been. Her own ward can testify to her
good works, her unbounded charity and
motherly care of the sick and afflicted, bet-
ter than we who knew her in a wider sense.
Everywhere her works testify of her, and
the younger generation should not pass by
such notable examples as her life affords.
Her name and life should be perpetuated in
the memory of every young man and wo-
man in Zion.

[Column 3]
	Notwithstanding the many public duties
Sister Kimball performed and the positions
she filled her home was never neglected;
she was a model housekeeper and knew the
art of making home attractive; yet there
was no stronger-minded woman in all
Israel than Sister Kimball. She has main-
tained the principle of equality of the sexes
and contended for it with all the advantages
pertaining thereto. She did not believe in
half measures. She stood solidly for the
lar[-]est freedom of opinion and of the press,
without waiting to see what other people
thought. As a public speaker she was con-
cise and to the point, never made long
speeches, but said what she felt forcibly
and always with effect. It was invariably
something to remember. She was specially
gifted in conversation, was well read, had
traveled much and met many celebrated
people; was at one time quite familiar in
Washington society.
	Sister Kimball taught school in this city
for several years, and under very trying 
circumstances, and while thus engaged in
teaching she became even more than ever
convinced of the need of changed conditions
for women engaged in work that came in
competition with men, and determined to
push the matter to the utmost. And she
knew there was no other method that could
be so effectual as the elective franchise.
	She was an ardent admirer and champion
of Susan B. Anthony and since her active
work as president of the Utah Suffrage
Association, her name has been on the Roll
of Honor of the National American Suffrage
Association as Honorary vice-president. 
Sister Kimball was a member of the Utah
Constitutional Convention of 1882, and was 
for some years a member of the Territorial
Committee of the People’s Party. It is im- 
possible to enumerate all this remarkable
woman has done, but suffice it to say she
has been one of the foremost in all pro-
gressive work of elevating, uplifting and
advancing society and humanity.
	After the Relief Society had been organ-
ized throughout Zion in Stakes and branches
a central organization was effected and Sarah
M. Kimball was chosen by Sister Eliza R.
Snow, the president, as secretary, June 19,
1880. October 10, 1892, the society was
incorporated and she was elected one of the
vice presidents, which position she occupied
at the time of her death. Her loss will be
deeply felt by all her co-workers, she was
wise in counsel, and her words were al-
ways fraught with meaning; her judgment
on all subjects was excellent, and she never
failed to give a word in due season and to
sound the rising note.
	We shall miss her in all our assemblies,
her presence was always inspiring if she 
was not able to say but a few words. At
our last General Conference of the Relief
Society in the Assembly Hall, October 5, 
she was not present being afraid of the ex-
posure, but she had been with us in the
meeting of the Board of Directors in the
“Exponent” office the day previous and
spoke impressively upon the subjects under
consideration. She also attended the officers'
meeting in the lecture hall of the Latter-
day Saints Business College on Saturday,
October 8, and spoke twice, a most unusual
thing for her. But we shall hear her voice
no more among us, she has passed on to
a higher life for which she was eminently
fitted, to join the innumerable company of
Saints who had preceded her, her husband,
father, mother and many relatives, whom


she officiated for in the Temples of Zion.
	Sister Kimball left three sons and one
daughter, her sons all have families but
they were just as tenderly attached to their
mother. There are quite a number of grand-
children.
	The relatives and friends of Sister Kim-
ball gathered about nine on Sunday morn-
ing at her residence to take a last look, as
she lay peacefully sleeping in her beautiful
white robes within the white casket and a
holy calm upon her face as if in vision.
When the farewells were over the casket
covered with loving mementoes of floral
offerings was borne to the chapel, followed
by the family and friends and the Relief
Society of which she was the honored and
beloved president. The Fifteenth ward
hall where the funeral services were con-
ducted was exquisitely draped in white and
flowers, and potted plants were artistically
arranged, (her express wish was that there
should be no black.) Bishop John Morgan
presided at the funeral services, the musical
exercises were by the ward choir, which in-
cludes Sister Maggie Hull, one of the
sweetest singers in all Israel.
	The speakers were first Sister Zina D.
H. Young, who had known Sister Kimball
since the days of Kirtland; she was much
affected while speaking, but paid an elo-
quent tribute of love and tenderness to her
departed friend and companion.
	Bishop R. T. Burton spoke of her many
virtues and of her magnificent capabilities,
her motherly attention to all who needed
assistance, whether of the household of faith
or not, and of the great blessing she had
been to all in the ward.
	Apostle H. J. Grant and President An-
gus M. Cannon each spoke in glowing
terms of praise of her lifelong labors. Presi-
dent M. Isabella Horne made the closing
speech, which was a loving tribute to her
memory and eulogistic of her long life
and work among the Saints. Apostle John
Henry Smith offered the benediction.
	A long train of carriages followed the re-
mains to the cemetery, where the body was
deposited beside her mother. Sister Lydia
D. Granger, her husband, Elder Hiram
Kimball, having been buried at sea on his
way as a missionary to the Sandwich Is-
lands, more than thirty years ago. Coun-
selor Rodney Badger dedicated the grave,
and the last sad rites were over when the
coffin was covered with the fresh earth and
also profusely with beautiful flowers.


WOMAN'S EXPONENT.


"MUSINGS AND MEMORIES."

	“Musings and Memories” is a volume of
poems entirely of home production, and is
very suitable as a gift for the holidays. The
book is sufficiently elegant in appearance for
any drawing room or library table. It con-
tains three hundred pages and a steel en-
graving of the author. It is for sale at the
office of the “Woman’s Exponent ” Tem-
pleton Builiding, opposite Deseret News
office. Cloth binding, $1.25; dainty cover,
$1.50; morocco, $2.00. Those living out-
side Salt Lake City who might wish to pur-
chase the book should address,
		Mrs. E. B. Wells,
	Templeton Building,
		Salt Lake City, Utah.


EDITORIAL NOTES.

	The "Exponent" acknowledges the gift of
a picture group of beautiful women, the


General Board of the Primary Associations
of Zion. Not only were we delighted with
the gift in itself, but knowing each one
personally and the individual grace of soul
that shines out from the eyes, it is doubly
dear, and as we gaze and admire we recall
the many lovely traits in each one’s char-
acter, and incidents in each one’s life that
are admirable. The likeness, too, is very
good and the picture altogether does credit
to the association. They are certainly faces
that denote intelligence and intense spiritual
power. Every Stake board in Zion who
can afford one should purchase one of them
to hang where the children can become ac-
quainted and familiar with the founders of
the Primary work.
	Among the handsome displays for Christ-
mas the new book store of George Q. Can-
non Sons and Company has certainly one of
the very finest, and perhaps equal in quality
of goods in their line to any establishment
in the city, if it does not excel. The goods
are all in style and the prices are on an
average with the quality. In fact they do
keep an excellent line of stationery and
materials in that line. This year they have
fancy china ware, rare vases and such orna-
ments as are suitable for Xmas gifts and a
fine assortment of pictures, principally
medallion, subjects new in style. The “Ex-
ponent’’ can safely recommend their goods
and also the attention one receives in being
served. The new store itself is very
attractive and fitted up in excellent taste,
and everything seems in keeping within
and without.


[Column 1]
		GENERAL CONFERENCE.

NATIONAL WOMAN'S RELIEF SOCIETY.

	Afternoon session, President Zina D. H.
Young presiding, Prof. C. J. Thomas lead-
ing the singing by the Temple choir.
Meeting opened with “Now let us rejoice.”
Prayer by Elder C. J. Thomas. Singing
“Oh Golden Day,” very beautifully rend-
ered by Maggie C. Hull and members of
the Temple choir.
	President Artemissia Lugmidler reported
Kanab Stake, referred to the lack of mem-
bers attending meetings and said that in
listening to other reports she found it to be
a general complaint. In regard to laying
up wheat said theirs was not a grain pro-
ducing locality, but she had urged the sis-
ters inasmuch as there was an appearance
of a bountiful harvest this year to lay up
what wheat they could; felt although they
were a small Stake they were diligent and
proving themselves faithful over a few
things.
	President Ann C. Woodbury of the St.
George Stake, told the long distance they
had to travel by team to visit the branches
of the society in their Stake, some places
were four hundred miles away and it
was quite an arduous undertaking to go all
the way around. “We are just getting a
good start in the silk industry. We were
to have had timber for a mill and the boys
were going to start off with their teams to
haul it when we received word the mill
was burned. Still we have every prospect
of success this year, all have plenty to do.
Our children have not applied themselves
to industrial pursuits in the past as they
should, we must insist upon industrious
habits in our children and the silk industry
is one pursuit that will be helpful in this
respect, and there is a good prospect of suc-
cess.”

[Column 2]
	Counselor C. C. Bartlett reported Uintah
Stake, they have a good wheat country,
five branches of the society have each a
good granary and the wheat stored is of a
good quality and in good condition. They
hold conferences twice a year, they try to
impress upon their children that they must
make themselves useful in life. “It is three
years since we were visited in our Stake,
although last year we had Sister Gates, who
came in the interest of the Y. L. M. I. A.
	President Annie R. Duke of Wasatch
Stake said she had only been elected a short
time, their former president, Sister Emma
Brown, having passed away she had been
called to the position of president and hoped
to fill the place with credit, but felt she
knew very little as yet, but would try to do
all in her power. She had visited around
the Stake since her appointment.
	Cassia Stake was reported by Sister Rol-
lins; the president was a very capable wo-
man. In traveling around they had to go
over a mountain to get to the other side
where one part of the sisters lived. Their
president had never yet missed attending
one conference since they were organized.
They were trying to do their part well.
	President Emma J. Bennett said, “We
make our visit among the sisters a pleasure
trip. We have twenty branches, we travel
with the authorities of the Stake and we al-
ways have a good time, our sisters are dili-
gent and united as well as energetic. We
want to do our share and assist the General
Society, we have been talking of a Stake
granary.”
	Star Valley was reported by Sister Sarah
Ann Burton. “We have a good band of
sisters, we do not have the best of grain,
but we have a mill now, which is a great
blessing to the people. We are just begin-
ning in our Stake to save grain, we have
many early frosts but we intend to perse-
vere and do our part in whatever is under-
taken by the General Society. We are
blest in having an abundance of strawber-
ries and small fruits. We have nine wards,
it takes three days to visit those fartherest
off.
	Sister Maggie Hull sang “Glory to God
on High,” the Temple choir joining.
	President Julia P. Lindsay of Bear Lake
Stake made an excellent report of the work
done in the society in that locality, and the
good spirit prevailing in their midst. She
felt the importance of the organization and
the great blessing it had been to the wo-
men of Zion, earnestly urged the sisters to
train their sons and daughters in the ways
of the Lord and exhorted all to good works.
	President Elizabeth Fox of Oneida Stake
was very much pleased with the instruction
given by the leading sisters, spoke par-
ticularly of their efforts in storing grain, in
Franklin they had over a thousand bushels
and a small granary but had concluded it
was best to put their wheat into the grist
mill and the mill pays them interest on the
grain. Sister Fox spoke of the change
made in their Stake so that it had not so
many branches, some wards having been
taken into the new Stake recently organized.
She also urged the sisters to take the “Ex-
ponent’’ and to read it, expressed her own
appreciation of its helpfulness; thought it
wrong not to support our own paper.
	President Mary Pitchforth of Juab Stake
referred to the good singing and realized
how much it added to the spirit of the
meeting. In their Stake they always had
good singing and interspersed it between
the speeches at their conferences. Referred


with sympathetic feeling to the death of
President Paxman, he had truly been a
father as well as a leader to them all in
Juab Stake. Sister Pitchforth spoke in a
very interesting manner of the good work
of the society, felt proud of their labors, re-
joiced in the Gospel and felt to press on- 
ward.
	Sister Maria Crosby spoke for Sevier
Stake, thought all were trying to do what
they knew to be right and ministering to
the needy. They had a good, energetic,
capable president.
	President Zina D. H. Young spoke of
the mission given her by President Young
to establish silk culture, and entreated the
sisters to put out mulberry trees and raise
cocoons. She thought we were too indiffer-
ent about these things and did not half con-
sider what the Lord had placed on the
daughters of Zion. Realized the brethren
carried heavy loads and so did the sisters.
Sister Zina remembered how Brother Joseph
looked when he plead with the sisters to be
faithful to the society and not commit the
sin of ingratitude, thought his words were
sweeter than honey in the honey comb.
“Do teach the daughters of Zion to do what
is right, to have confidence in their fathers,
and teach them how to get strength in the
hour of trial, and to draw near to the Lord,
it is their only safety.”
	Brother Huff of St. George, read an arti
cle on silk which he had prepared for publi-
cation, after which President Young spoke
upon silk again, her great desire to see it
established and for young women to take
hold of it, and closed her remarks by say-
ing she was thoroughly committed to the
silk industry.
	President Jane S. Richards spoke of the
young women, how glad she was to see
some in the conference, to hear Wasatch
reported by Sister Annie Duke who said
she only knew about the Young Ladies
Association when she was called to preside
over the Relief Society of the Stake. She
felt that young mothers needed just the in-
struction they could get in the Relief Soci-
ety, knew it would be good for them to
come and hear the experienced mothers
like Sister Zin[-] and such women as they
had heard today. She rejoiced in days
gone by when she could listen to women
who were wiser and loved to hear them in
their meetings, Sister Eliza and Mother
Whitney and those who were older, there
should be younger women in the society,
we need their help and they need us. Sis
ter Richards spoke of the house for confer-
ences and use of the General Society. Said
if a little was given in from all the settle-
ments it would soon accumulate, we could
have a place and have it dedicated, and it
was just what was needed.
	President M. I Horne spoke about the-
nursing class taught by Dr. Roberts free of
expense, and said that there was still op-
portunity for other students; spoke of the
growth of the society, new wards had been
organized, we had need of a three days
meeting, it would not be too long for all to
have an opportunity to report.
	Singing “Lord dismiss us with Thy bless-
ing.” Conference adjourned until April
1899. Dismissed with prayer by President
Susan Grant.
				E. B. WELLS, Secretary.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


No one who is not a good listener can
possibly become a good conversationalist.


IN MEMORIAM.

EDNA SPRAGUE.
	Another of our dear sisters has been called to
leave us, Sister Edna Sprague of this place,
(Junction.) She died Oct. 13, 1898, after an ill-
ness of two weeks.
	Sister Edna was born in the 15th ward, Salt
Lake City, June 2, 1871. Her parents were David
and Esther La Baron. Her mother dying when
she was about four years old Edna came to live
with an older sister, Sister Ella Merrill, who is
the president of our Relief Society, and whose
husband, Brother John Merrill, is our Bishop. 
	Under the loving care of Brother and Sister
Merrill she grew to womanhood, and on Sep. 17,
1891, was married to Edwin E. Sprague. She
leaves four children, two boys and two girls, the
youngest a baby two weeks old.
	While we miss the dear sister who has “gone
before” and sympathize with those who are be-
reft, yet we can but bow in humble, silent sub-
mission to our Father’s decree, and pray for His
grace to strengthen us in all we are called to pass
through.
					ALICE HAYES.
Junction, Nov. 17, 1898.
ELIZABETH EDMONDS GAILEY.
	It is with sorrow we record the death of anoth-
er faithful sister, Mrs. Elizabeth Edmonds Gailey.
She was born July 24, 1854, at Lowstaff, Suffolk
Co., England.
	She was afflicted for a long time with consump-
tion. Her whole life was spent in the work of
God. Before her marriage much of her time was
taken up with the Y. L. M. I. A. in Salt Lake
City. She was a member of the ward choir and
a very useful member in the Relief Society.
	She was married to John Gailey May 21, 1886,
and since that time has been a teacher in the
Kaysville Relief Society and faithfully performed
that duty whenever her health would permit, al-
ways being cheerfnl and happy.
	She leaves a husband and three living children
to mourn her loss. It was hard for her to part
with her loved ones, she clung to life to the very
last. She has gone to rest until resurrection shall
unite them no more to part. ’Tis hard to part
with those we love, but parting comes to all.
CELIA HALL RAYMOND.
	Celia Hall Raymond died at her home October
22, 1898, of dropsy after an illness of nearly three
months.
	Celia, only daughter of Bradley and Elizabeth
Hall, was born at Liberty, Sullivan Co, New
York, April 20, 1825, and was reared in a quiet
home in a country place by a kind and gentle
mother, having lost her father at an early age.
	She was married to Grandison Raymond of the
same place, August 12, 1849. Shortly thereafter
she and her husband were influenced to go West
to investigate the principles of the Gospel. While
at St. Louis they became satisfied of the truth
and were baptized January, 1850. In the spring
that year they moved to Council Bluffs, Iowa, and
a year f[-]om that time went back to their old
home on a visit to settle up their affairs prepara-
tory to gathering with the Saints.
	In the summer of 1852, they removed to Salt
Lake valley with their two small children, the
youngest being but ten days old when the journey
was commenced. They landed in Salt Lake City
and after remaining here for some time moved to
Bountiful where they made their permanent
home. She was the mother of ten children,
seven of whom survive her.
	Sister Raymond was a faithful worker in the
Relief Society as a teacher, in which capacity she
labored for many years. She was most generous
in giving to the poor in a private as well as a pub-
lic way, and in her home circle her kind, forgiving
and self-denying character cannot be too highly
praised
	Funeral services were held on Monday at 1
o’clock in the meeting house. The speakers
were Brothers John Ellison, William [-]eezl[-]y,
Ebenezer Williams T. F. Rouche, John R.
Barnes and John Thornley.
					SARAH B LAYTON


OBITUARY.

		CAROLINE HARRIS POULTON.

	Died at her residence on Sunday, November 6,
1898, Caroline Harris Poulton,wife of James Poul-
ton, after a severe a d lingering illness, which
she bore with patience and fortitude. Deceased


was born at Straford on Avon, February 4, 1829.
	She received the Gospel in early childhood,
being baptized without the knowledge of her
father, her mother being a faithful Latter-day 
Saint She was married to Elder James Poulton
October 14, 1852. She with her husband set sail
on the vessel Amazon for Salt Lake City, June 4,
1863. They crossed the plains with ox teams and
arrived in Salt Lake City, October 4, of the same
year. They have resided in the 6th ward from
that time, her husband being the leader of that
choir since the fall of 1864, and Sister Poulton
being an active singer for many years in the same.
	She was a faithful worker in the Relief Society,
being a teacher which labor she performed for 
many years, as long as her health would permit.
She held this position unti the day of her depart-
ure from this life. She was also one of the
wheat committee.
	She ever proved a faithful wife and loving
mother. She was blessed with twelve children,
seven sons a[-]d five daughters, four of whom have
passed away She leaves six sons and two
daughters and thirty-four living grandchildren to
mourn her loss. One of her sons has recently re-
turned from filling a fai[-]hful mission.
	Her life was an eventful one, she enduring all
the hardships incident to early days. Her health
had been failing for some time. After undergo-
ing an operation twelve months ago she seemed
to rally, but three months previous to her demise
she was taken with another severe attack, but her
faith was strong in the Lor[-] and His ordinances, 
she being desirous of receiving with her husband
all the blessings that can be obtained in the
House of the Lord. The brethren, and the sis-
ters of the Relief Society rallied around her and
exercised such faith and unremitting kindness,
and through t[-]e administration of the Gospel she
was once more restored, the physicians having
given her up. [-]hrough the mercy of the Lord
she was able to go to the Salt Lake Temple and
there with [-]er husband received all the blessings
that can be obtained in the house of the Lord.
Her work being completed it pleased the Lord to
call her home, and she passed peacefully away.
	The funeral services were held in the 6th ward
assem[-]l  hall on the 8th inst. Many timely re-
marks were made by President Joseph E. Taylor,
Bishop James C. Watson, Brother Green and
others The coffin was beautifully covered with
flowers from loving friends,and to those who were
there it seemed that Sister Poulton was not dead 
but sleeping.

		RESOLUTIONS OF RESPECT.

	Resolved, That as our heavenly Father in His
wisdom has seen fit to call home our sister to a
better life, w  the officers and members of the 6th
ward Relief Society express our sincere sympathy
to the bereaved husband and family in their loss,
feeling, also, that they have been greatly blessed
with her wise and faithfu[-] counsel and example. 
Be it further
	Resolved, That these resolutions be placed up- 
on our society records, also a copy sent to the
“Woman’s Exponent” for publication
				ISABELLA WEST, Pres.
				ELIZABETH W.CONDIE,Sec.


LADIES’ SEMI-MONTHLY MEETING. 

	Minutes of Oct. 14, 1898, Counselor E.
J. Stevenson presiding. Singing “Come
hither all ye weary souls.” Prayer by Sis-
ter J. C. Howe. Singing “Glorious things
are sung of Zion.” Minutes read and ap-
proved.
	Sister Stevenson made opening remarks,
exhorted the sisters not to follow the fash-
ions of the world and gave good counsel.
	Sisters H. M. Wright, Ellen Barton and
another sister, also Sister Black, each bore 
good testimonies of the truth and regarding
experiences in their own lives.
	Sister Mary A Freeze spoke of the good
instruction she had received from Sister
Eliza, Sister Zina and others. Sister Eliza
had told the sisters not to pray to the Lord
to have their trials removed, but ask Him
to give them faith to live above them.
	Sister Brixen had just returned from a
trip to the old country, her native land. 
When she was away she thought of her sis-
ters here at home in Zion. She visited two


[Column 1]
branches of the Relief Society over there
and told them of our meetings and the
unity among the women here. Many of
them were anxious to emigrate. Sister
Brixen felt these meetings had prepared her
to understand the Gospel that she might be
able to instruct her dear sisters in foreign
lands, she thought every sister should be
interested in Relief Society work.
	President Elmina S. Taylor felt thankful
for our aged sisters, and that she had been
privileged to associate with them. Spoke
of the trials she passed through because of
the opposition of her family in joining the
Church, but there was nothing on earth to
tempt her to deny her religion, wondered
how many would fall by the wayside, she
could see that many were departing from
the truth but prayed that all present would
be preserved faithful.
	Sister M. A. Hardy expressed her grati-
tude, was glad she remained faithful, had a
strong testimony to bear.
	Sister Annie T. Hyde had a testimony of
the work of God, had always been associated
with Sister Young and other good and
noble sisters, it had been a trial to her to
assist in the work of the society, but she
was thankful she had accepted the position
she now held and knew it had benefited her.
	Sister M. O. Brown thought it was easier
for some to remain faithful than others.
The Gospel principles seemed familiar
when she first heard them, felt it was beau-
tiful, every family ought to be interested.
	Another sister gave her experience and
related incidents of a visit to relatives in
Canada.
	Counselor Stevenson said the Lord had
opened the way and she had got some
genealogy of her family. Gave good ad-
vice to all present, exhorting them to be
faithful.
	Singing, Doxology. Adjourned for two
weeks. Benediction by Sister Alder.

	Minutes of Oct. 29, 1898, President M.
I. Horne presiding. Singing “O, say what
is truth.” Prayer by Counselor Webb.
Singing “Who are these arrayed in white?”
	The president alluded to the organization
that led to these meetings and believed
President Young was inspired of the Lord,
realized that He preserved His children and
those who trusted in Him from the world
and its turmoil. We must acknowledge
His hand in all things and seek for wisdom,
knowledge and discernment. These are
the days of proving and days of temptation.
Let us set an example worthy of imitation,
be firm, steadfast and watchful that Satan
may not deceive us. Those around us can-
not take the spirit of God from us if we are
in possession of it by obedience. Now that
winter is coming she wanted the presidents
to look after the poor and see what they
need.
	Counselor B. W. Smith spoke upon the
necessity of being true and faithful, and to
teach our children to be honest and virtu-
ous so they can resist temptation. Sister
Smith had selected an article to read in the
meeting, Sister Hyde read it, it was a de-
scription of the deplorable condition of the
morals of the world, and how incompetent
religious denominations were to cope with
the existing evils. A prophecy was next
read of the Prophet Joseph Smith’s in re-
gard to war.
	Counselor Webb said, “We cannot be too
humble and charitable, we must set a good
example and be careful of little things,
visit and comfort the sick and exercise faith

[Column 2]
and it will be a blessing to ourselves and
others to whom we minister.”
	Sisters Rawlins, Nuttall and Morris each
bore excellent testimonies and made excel-
lent remarks on interesting subjects.
	Counselor Stevenson said it is one of the
commandments given to the Saints to meet
together often, and we should teach our
children the principles of the Gospel and
prepare them for life; also spoke urgently
of the work in the Temple, which was a
blessing to the living and the dead.
	Sisters Leaker and Wright, Nellie Taylor
and Julia Woolley spoke of the goodness of
God and the blessings of the Gospel and
testimony of the truth, and President Horne
read a letter from Brother Bassett about
teaching singing to the Relief Society and
asked for the vote of those who would join
a class in singing; but the sisters did not
feel they could at present. She also wished
some of the sisters to visit Dr. Robert’s
nursing class.
	Meeting adjourned for two weeks by
singing “We thank Thee O, God for a
prophet.” Benediction by Sister L. D. Alder.
		LYDIA D. ALDER, Act. Sec.

	Minutes of Nov. 12, 1898, Counselor
Stevenson presiding in the absence of Presi-
dent Horne. Singing “Lord we come be-
fore Thee now. ” Prayer by Sister Clara
Cannon. Singing “O, ye mountains high.”
	Sister Cannon said she wished each Re-
lief Society to give in a sentiment, they
were to be recorded in a book and given to
Sister Horne for a birthday present and
that a meeting would be held in the Assem-
bly hall next Saturday in honor of her
birthday.
	Minutes read and approved. Counselor
Stevenson said she thought we were blest
beyond those of our sisters who are living
in our surrounding cities and towns, we
were privileged to hear the apostles and
people of God almost every time we went
to meeting.
	President Horne arrive. Sister Jane
James find another sister bore faithful testi-
monies. 
	Sister Zina H. Bull sent in her resigna-
tion, stating that it was impossible for her
to continue her labors. A vote of thanks
was tendered her and Sister Julia S. Wool-
ley was sustained as secretary to fill the
place made vacant by her resignation
	Sister Horne said she felt that this peo-
ple were her people, and where they go she
wanted to go. She knew Joseph Smith was a
prophet of God, the greatest that ever lived
except Jesus Christ. Said there were so
many false spirits abroad that each one
must have a testimony in her own heart to
resist their power, we must watch that we
be not led astray. Said our Stake secretary
of the Relief Society had resigned and Sis-
ter Augusta W. Grant had been selected to
take her place. She was unanimously sus-
tained in that office.
	Sister Julia Howe said we had received
the priesthood in connection with our hus-
bands and had had the Holy Ghost confer-
red upon us. She thought economy should
be a part of our religion and use what
God blesses us with for building up the
kingdom of God. Exhorted all to sustain
each other, redeem our dead and increase in
faith, and not do as those who are rich and
grow faithless. Let us pray for each other,
especially those who are placed in responsi-
ble positions.
	Sister Morris spoke beautifully to Sister
Jane James. Sister Georgie Young was

[Column 3]
present to announce an entertainment to be
given in the Assembly Hall on Friday night
the 18th for the benefit of the Kindergarten.
	Several other sisters bore strong testi-
monies of the truth of the Gospel and the
goodness of God.
	Sister Derrick related instances of the
fulfillment of prophecy in her behalf. Sis-
ter Paul said she had been blessed in the
Temple and had many promises fulfilled.
	Sister Lydia D. Alder said the trials of
life are hard to pass through but God will
bear us up. Several other sisters bore testi-
monies of God’s goodness to them and the
power that had been made manifest in heal-
ing them, as well as giving them spiritual
blessings. 
	President Horne said she would advise
the sisters to all rise on their feet and testi-
fy of God, said Jesus Christ said He would
testify to our Heavenly Father of those who
would testify of Him before man. Said she
always had attended her meetings for she 
needed spiritual food.
	Meeting adjourned for two weeks. Sing-
ing “Lord dismiss us with Thy blessing.”
Benediction by Counselor Webb.
		JULIA S. WOOLLEY, Sec.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


It is quite possible to go through an end-
less variety of experiences, to suffer deeply
and enjoy keenly, to see all the countries of
the world, to hear what great men have to
say and to live to a good old age with a
past full of stirring occurrences, and yet
have very little to add to the wisdom, or
knowledge, or interest of mankind. It is
not a man’s actual experience, but that
which he has gained from it that determines
its value to himself and, through him, to 
society.
