A VOYAGE OF LIFE.

Once on a time in the days that are olden,
	 “The dear, dead days of the beautiful past,”
When our hearts were light, and our visions
		golden
	Then love seem’d to fill the universe vast.

We sent our ships forth over perilous seas,
	With their precious freight bound for havens
		fair,
And the sails unfurl’d in the tremulous breeze,
	Signals of triumph, to do, and to dare.

Many proud vessels came back heavy laden,
	With cargo of precious jewels full store;
And only a few were with storms o’er take,
	And stranded, or lost on a dang’rous shore.

The pilots were cautious, the vessels built strong
	Storms might rage fierce in midst of t[-]e ocean;
Yet bravely they sailed, tho’ the voyage was long;
	Their trust was in God with faith and devotion

But clouds have since dark’ned the sky that was
		clear,
	And many new dangers threaten the way,
The mariner sailing has much more to fear,
	For treach’rous signals, tempt vessels astray

Yes, the ocean of life has chang’d in its cour[-]e,
	No longer may youth go sailing secure,
They’re met by an enemy, oft with such force
	Their bark must ride fea[-]lessly if they endure!

More beacon lights warn them of drifting ashore,
	More signals, more trumpets sound the alarm
Yet wrecks of life’s voy’gers increase more and
		more,
	How shall we save them from coming to harm?

Can we turn back the tide that’s set in so strong.
	Wrecking the vessels just put out to sea?
O have we been guiltless of error and wrong?
	How shall we answer in worlds yet to be?

O, let us by Faith light a beacon so bright,
	That its rays may shine forth from shore to
		shore,

And the wand’rers bark in a perilous night,
	May find its way safe, tho’ the wild waves roar.

Let Love be the Shibboleth now evermore,
	“Pass the word down the line, ’afar off and near
Truth be our armor, till Life’s voyage is o’er;
	And white wings of Peace, our vessel shall
		steer.
							E. B. W.


Women are to the fore in educational
matters in Colorado. Out of the fifty-nine
counties in the state, twenty seven have
women superintendents of schools and
every school board has one or two women
members.


Snow-flakes are falling, so softly so white,
I fancy they’re whispering to my heart to-night;
So pure and so sweet through the darkness of
	even,
They fall at my feet [-]ike a message from heaven.
I fancy them letters the angels have thrown
From the windows of heaven and watched them
	come down.
And then as they spread their pure folds o’er the
	sod,
They seem like a letter—a message from God.
Put over its surface in sparkles of gold,
His unfailing promise to mortals is told.
That under the fold of this soft, sunny glean
He guards the life-buds of the beautiful Spring.


MISS ANTHONY’S LETTER.

My Dear Mrs. Wells:
	I have before me your good letters of
Nov. 2nd and 14th, also several others from
women in Utah, all relative to the election
of Mr Roberts. The deed is done—the
women of Utah, as well as the men, voted
their party ticket regardless of the right
action of its Congressional nominee on the
woman suffrage question. Congress will
not reject him because of his disloyalty
to woman’s rights, and it is doubtful if it
will on account of his family relations;
but we shall see what we shall see!!
	If his influence in Washington is against
woman suffrage you must expose him right
along, just hold him to woman suffrage.
	Governor Wells’ word was splendid, give
him my kind regards, and also to each and
all of the Wells family. They are made of
good sterling stuff, and that is the kind of
people this nation most needs. The point
of Mrs. A’s letter was that the Utah Suf-
frage Association ought to publish an earn-
est protest against Mr. Roberts; that you
surely can do. Mrs. S. tells of securing an
expression from Mr. Roberts that he would
not oppose suffrage. Both seem very earn-
est for the honor of the women of the State.
I do hope all women, Mormon or Gentile,
will banish sectarianism or partisanshipism,
and talk and work and vote only for good
officials, regardless of church or party!
There are good and bad men and women in
all churches and all parties, and it is
only the good that should be selected for
official positions.
	Your women must learn from this elec-
tion the vital importance of their presence
in the very first nominations, for after the
nominations are made it is so very difficult
to persuade voters to stand there for any
save their own party nominees. But it will
take time to teach women. Why men have
not learned the lesson with a hundred and
more years of practice, then let us be patient
with women’s mistakes at the first and
second time of voting. What you need in
Salt Lake is a local council of all the differ-
ent societies and clubs; and then for the
officers of the council to make the move for
or against public measures or men, thus
acting for all the women of the city, Mor-
mon or Gentile, Republican or Democratic,
silver or gold—instead of as now, one
<p>arty protesting and all the others silent.


That morning session in Omaha when
the Local Council reported their work was
very impressive to me, and I am working
to get our Rochester women’s clubs and
societies to organize into a local council.
We cannot help suffrage in so speedy a
manner. We want women on the school
board, women on the board of city physi-
cians, etc., etc. For any one of our clubs
to move for the result would amount to
nothing, but for all of your associations
combined in a local association to make the
move would effect the thing desired. Cer-
tainly with you women who hold the ballot
in your hands!! So to combine the forces
of your women into one voice, one action,
is your great need. But it should be stated
by a call for a meeting signed by the presi-
dents of all of the most popular clubs.
Your suffrage society, like ours, should be
one of these, but not the first or leading
one. Here we have an Ethical club of
four or five hundred women, and I shall try
and get its president to lead off for the
council.
	Do you know I really believe if the suf-
frage clubs would quietly get behind the
scenes and work for such local councils we
would do an immense educational work. I
am going to try what it will do in Roches-
ter, that is sure. * * *
Lovingly and loyally yours,
SUSAN B. ANTHONY.
Dec. 5, 1898


AN OLD, OLD STORY.

	This story has been told but not written,
of that I feel tolerably certain; and as I
could not from experience tell such a fine
story myself, I can only write one told to
me by a school friend a very long time ago.
	She was a famous little tory and all the
rest of us were pure, blue-blood Yankees,
but she told us such wonderful tales that
we looked upon her as a veritable fairy.
We were quite unwilling then to think
these things were all true, and yet since
those days I have many a time and oft
heard stories far more extravagant, and can
easily believe now that Pamela Kingsley
told the truth as she had heard it from her
governess about her maiden Aunt Dilli-
way with whom she lived. She was her
great aunt, the sister of her grandfather
Dilliway, who was a “royalist to the back-
bone,” Pamela told us. And really she
used to sort of queen it over us, in a way,
because she knew all about Xmas and we
barely knew the name. Aunt Dilliway lived
in Boston and her niece, Miss Pamela
Kingsley was sent up in the country to get
fresh air, new milk and the scent of wild
flowers and new-mown hay. That was
how we come to know her, and as we
adored Boston girls we all fell in love with
her before we knew she was a tory.
	Notwithstanding Pamela was from
Boston she didn’t know much more about
Yankees than we did about tories, she had
never been at school before but had a
governess at home, and as her great aunt
Dilliway was not equal to managing a
French governess and an obstinate little


[Column 1]
girl, who was also very delicate; she made
up her mind to send her into the country
for a while to her great uncle on her fath-
er’s side, Judge Kingsley. His wife had
no sympathy with girls who always wore
white muslin, laces and ruffles, and so sent
her to the school with the country girls, and
Pamela was not in the least punished. She
enjoyed it immensely, she knew she was
the best dressed girl in the school and her
city ways were very taking, but most of all
she was fond of telling what she knew in a
fantastic sort of fashion that was ever so
much better than any story book we had
ever read. One Saturday when we were
out in the woods for a ramble and had tired
ourselves climbing, the most romantic girl
in the crowd turned to Pamela and said,
“Do tell us about your Aunt Dilliway when
she was a little girl; you say she was a per-
fect beauty and a great belle in her day.”
	“Well, girls, Aunt Dilliway is so proud
that if she saw me here on the rocks spin-
ning yarns to you girls, she would have me
sent home by the first coach for Boston.
She’s old and grey now but she wears a wig
and paints her cheeks, and there’s a lovely
painting of her upstairs, and she’s even
been written about in books in London
when she was young and beautiful, and she
broke two or three men’s hearts, and then
she got her own broken.”
	“Served her right!” said two or three all
in a breath.”
	“No it didn’t,” said Pamela, “If you
knew what she suffered.”
	“How did you find out about her love
affairs?” inquired the one who had asked
for the story, and who in fact was all her
life getting people to tell her things she
meant some day to put into books.
	“Girls every one of you must promise
never to tell, for Aunt Kingsley wants a pre-
text to send me home, I know it, and if she
could find out about the things I’ve told
you girls, she would bundle me off in
double quick time.”
	Of course we all promised and then we
asked “How about the young men? tell us
about them.”
	“They all married afterwards all but one,
and he got killed, and Aunt Dilliway wears
the ring he gave her—now he’s dead, and
she has her white satin wedding dress all
trimmed with rich lace, hid away in a
cedar chest, and a long lace veil all em-
broidered in a nunnery.”
	“Oh, a nunnery! tell us about the nuns,
if there is anything mysterious we want to
find it out,” said half a dozen at once.
	The whole bevy of girls were wild to
hear about the nuns, but Pamela answered,
“My aunt didn’t know about the nuns, she
isn’t a Catholic, she’s High Church Episco-
pal, and sits every Sunday in King’s chapel
in a great, high pew, and makes me sit by
her, and I daren’t wink but I nearly fall,
asleep, and then she nudges me with her
great feather fan and whispers ‘Miss Pame-
la sit up.” ’
	“Who were your Aunt Dilliway’s beaux,
were they noblemen?”
	“They were gentlemen, one was very
courtly and handsome and brave and a fine
singer, and my aunt played the harp—the
very same one she has now up in the corner
of her boudoir covered with real tapestry.
I asked her about it one day and she said it
was an heirloom.”
	“How did you find out about all these
fine gentlemen and your great aunt’s love
stories, you say she never talks to you?”
	“Why from Belmont, the governess, you

[Column 2]
know; that’s why she was sent home to
France, and I was packed off up here to
cure me of prying into family secrets; my
aunt says she has ruined me every way,
gave me sweetmeats and all sorts of for-
bidden things, and told me the love stories
of the Dilliway family for generations back,
all of which she got hold of herself by spy-
ing, and getting into old letters in the cab-
net, the Dilliway rosewood cabinet, hun-
dreds of years old made in Switzerland.”
	“Miss Pamela tell us what about the let-
ters we don’t care for the cabinet that hid
them,” said Julia, the most curious girl in
the crowd. “What a wonderful part letters
have played in the lives of men and women
ever since the days of Jeremiah the prophet.
Letters do play the mischief, I shall never
write any myself.”
	“You’ll be the first one to do it undoubt-
edly; but Julia, you must have taken to
reading the Bible lately to know so much
about Jeremiah, but remember in those
days everything was written on parchment
and in rolls, and they were not letters car-
ried by post,” broke in Lottie Brooks.
	“No, but they made a lot of trouble, all
the same, and dreadful things came to pass
that had been written about and then hid-
den away.”
	“Girls let’s not go back to Jeremiah, its
quite enough to go back to Pamela’s Aunt
Dilliway, and if we linger much longer
we’ll have to go home without the love
story, and we all dearly love that kind of a
romance. What was in the letters that so
offended your aunt when you found it out?”
interpolated Nellie. 
	“It was a sort of disconnected history
but Belmont put this and that together, and
told me that my great aunt had to run
away to America because some one fought a
duel about her. You see she was engaged
to two or three young men at once, and
after all it wasn’t her fault, she explained
some of it to me and cried and her lips
were as pale as ashes. I never expected to
see Aunt Dilliway shed a tear, she never
cried when bad news came, not even when
any one died that she had known all her
life. She used to tremble a little and her
lips would quiver, and that was all.”
	“Then she is a real born tory is she?
What did she come here for? Was it true
that tale Belmont told about a duel? Did
your aunt own up to it?” cried the roman-
tic one of the girls.
	“It wasn’t quite true, but Harry Severn
was dead when she came over to America,
and she will never set foot on English soil
again, never.”
	“Then she ought to be an American and
not a tory. Harry Severn, was he an offic-
er of rank, brave and accomplished? I
judge so from what you told us about the
harp.”
	“Yes, he was a distinguished officer, but
Aunt Dilliway’s father made another
match for her, and he wanted to separate
her from Lieut. Severn. The family were
too poor, and he thought his daughter was
good enough to marry a duke, so he select-
ed the eldest son of a relative of a duke,
who was the heir (the duke had no children
and he was getting very old) and he was
soon to come into his inheritance. He paid
his addresses to my aunt and sort of fasci-
nated her with his elegant manners and all
that, but he didn’t touch her heart at all,
Belmont said, and then there was another
lover, a real duke in his own right, old and
had grown up daughters but no sons, and
he fairly persecuted Aunt Dilliway.”

[Column 3]
	“For goodness sake, Pamela, what was
your Aunt Dilliway’s name? She couldn’t
have been called Aunt Dilliway when she
was young.”
	“No, but she never will be called any-
thing but Miss Dilliway by people, and all
her letters come addressed to Miss Dilliway,
and I never knew she had another name
till Belmont said to me one day ‘Penelope
Dilliway,’ and I turned on her and said
‘who’s Penelope Dilliway please?’ ‘Penelope
she’s your great aunt herself.’ Before that
I had never thought about her having any
other name.”
	“Now do tell us about the three suitors
and how it all happened and she never
married either one of them. And, besides,
one day you said when your Aunt Dilliway
was a young girl they used to have gay
times at Christmas in England,” chimed in
the romantic young Miss who first asked
for the story.
	“And so they did, and I’ve heard of their
great doings and Belmont has told me
about Xmas in France, too.”
	“Well we haven’t time to go over all the
countries, finish this one story.”
	“So I will if you’ll let me, now keep
quiet. You understand there were three
men all in love with Aunt Dilliway. First
there was Harry Severn, then the young
heir-at-law, then the real duke. Two were
poor and one was rich, but Charles Plymp-
ton had ‘great expectations,’ and the Duke
of Weldmore he wanted a young wife hop-
ing to have a son.”
	“Did your aunt tell you that or did you
read it in the letters?”
	“No, Belmont told me. My aunt would
be wild with rage if she suspected I had
ever heard of it.”
	“It strikes me that your French gover-
ness ought to be sent to Halifax, but it
serves people right who set themselves up
like that, as if their children were so much
better than other peoples. I suppose that’s
toryism, but it isn’t American,” said Julia.
	“On the same principle,” said Gratia, a
very demure, little girl, “we are all sent to
this select school for girls, and simply be-
cause we are not to go with all the other
girls in the villiage, I tell you girls, its a
question of money. I heard my mother
say, ‘I shall send Gratia to a girls’ select
school as we can’t afford a governess.’ ”
	“So you are a little tory, too, are you?”
said Julia.
	“Now girls,” put in Pamela, “if you
are going to dispute about politics I shall
never finish this story and you’ll all dream
about it, and tomorrow will be Sunday, and
my aunt will make me read the Bible and
sing hymns all day, she’s a Puritan, there’s
no denying that, but she can’t take me to
meeting with her, that’s part of the bar-
gain, and uncle never goes any way. Now
where did I leave off, if you say much more
I shall not know whether I’m a tory or a
rebel.”
	“They’re both the same,” echoed two
or three at once.”
	“I am sure I don’t know where I left
off.”
	“You left off about Xmas time,” said
Jane Willis, “and that’s what we all want
to know about, I don’t care which of the three
men your aunt was in love wtth, if you
would only tell us about an English Christ-
mas in one of those great stone castles, or
large old-fashioned mansions, of which we
have so often heard yet know so little.”
	The girls were so inquisitive and inter-
rupted Pamela with questions all the way


[Column 1]
through her narrative that it is quite im-
possible after half a century to be anything
like accurate in the repetition, so I have de-
cided to tell the story myself as she told it
to us as nearly as I can remember it.
	You must imagine a dozen or more half
grown girls in short dresses with low necks
and wearing frilled pantalettes and low slip-
pers clustered around Miss Pamela, eyes
beaming with delight while she told the
love story of her great Aunt Dilliway.
AUNT DILLIWAY’S LOVE STORY.
	Penelope Dilliway was an only daughter,
and very proud of herself and her belong-
ings, had been taught all sorts of accomplish-
ments such as were considered woman’s
legitimate employment in those days, or
perhaps we should say for “fine ladies."
such as embroidery, singing, dancing and
playing the harp, especially dancing. Miss
Dilliway’s father was determined she should
marry well, and his idea of marriage was
wealth and title, one or both. His daugh-
ter, however, fell desperately in love with
a young lieutenant, a man of brilliant
parts, a fine tenor singer, and a general
favorite in society.
	Miss Dilliway resorted to all sorts of
subterfuges to deceive her irate father; and
her younger brother, (the maternal grand-
father of Miss Pamela) was her accomplice
in many well laid plots of the lovers. The
father introduced into the home the young
and fascinating heir-at-law of a dukedom,
Mr. Charles Plympton, who really had
great influence with Miss Penelope, by his
sophistry and polish, and at first Miss Dilli-
way fancied she was yielding to blind her
father to the true state of her love affairs
with the young lieutenant. But her broth-
er more quickly than herself detected the
fascination; and felt in duty bound to re-
monstrate with her saying, “You cannot
possibly be aware of your own inconstancy,
your desire to please Plympton in the mat-
ter of dress, the choice of flowers and per-
fumery, and above all your great desire to
be an expert horsewoman, all of which is
very apparent to a close observer, reveal per-
ceptibly how the matter stands. Father
speaks of it with great satisfaction at the
dinner table after you are gone, and Plymp-
ton chuckles with delight at the success at-
tending his suit. It is downright coquetry
think of it as you will, sister, I believe you
are innocent, I believe you love Harry
truly, but Plympton’s wiles and artifices
are luring you on to your own destruction.”
	When thus reproved Penelope would
weep, (she had tears for occasion then) and
declared she would never try to please
Plympton again, let him pursuade as he
might, she would give up riding much as
she enjoyed it, and remain away from balls
and pleasure parties, where she would be
likely to meet one who sought her against
her will.
	Meantime Harry Severn was away with
his regiment, but rumors reached him of
Penelope riding in the park with Plympton,
and other like rambles together, of their
dancing together more often than even
lovers were wont in those days. Goaded
beyond endurance by his fellow officers he
obtained leave of absence and hurried to
London.
	Arriving at the time of Xmas festivities
he looked up young Dilliway his true friend
who unconscious of the presence of Plymp-
ton at the hall took Harry Severn home
with him, thinking it would be an easy

[Column 2]
matter to come unannounced and surprise
his father, who though perhaps nonplussed
at first was too much the gentleman in his
own house to be rude even to an unwelcome
guest. Unfortunately he had "reckoned
without his host." Penelope was in the
gayest of spirits[-] regardless of her protesta-
tions to her brother, she had dressed her-
self with the greatest care in the colors
Plympton preferred, and even wore his
favorite flowers, sent from his own hot
house, and everything in and about the
mansion bespoke a woman’s desire to please
the guest of the occasion.
‘The mistletoe hung in the castle hal[-],
The ho[-]ly branch shone on the old oak wall.'
	Young Dilliway sprang from the cab and
Harry Severn followed him into the great
wide hall amid a perfect blaze of light and
dazzle of brilliancy; beyond was the library,
and the brother thinking to give his sister
the pleasure of a lover’s greeting hurried to
the library with his young friend while he
went himself to call Penelope. The library
opened on the other side from the drawing
room, and while Harry stood waiting ex-
pecting momentarily to see his sweetheart
the heavy tapestry curtains parted, and
beaming with smiles, and radiant as the
sun, in steps Penelope followed by Charles
Plympton. At sight of her lover in mili-
tary cloak and plumes, she paled like death
and would have fainted had she not burst
into tears; the soldier never stirred, he stood
like a statue, a few seconds and the brother
came to the rescue, he took his sister and
led her from the library through the long
hall up the marble staircase to her own
room and there he left her “alone with her
conscience.”
	He was absent a few seconds but in that
time angry and bitter words had passed be-
tween the two young men[-] and a quarrel
seemed inevitable. The brother succeeded
in pacifying them by reminding them it was
Xmas eve, and that “Peace and good will”
had always prevailed at such times in all re-
spectable English homes; that it would be
an unpardonable offense for two gentlemen
to disturb the host, and what would be
gained by giving way to an insane passion
for supremacy in a young girls affection.
“Besides,” he added, “you believe, doubt-
less in the old saying ‘All is fair in love
and war.’ ”
	While her brother was making peace be-
low stairs, Penelope’s sobs and shrieks had
brought her maid Honore to her assistance,
and with the aid of restoratives and condo-
lences, she finally calmed her nerves, pur-
suaded her to change her apparel, and
make herself presentable at dinner, for there
was to be quite a large party of gentlemen
and a few ladies, among them the old duke
who was so enamored of Penelope, that he
made himself ridiculous in the extreme.
	Penelope had at least two good hours in
which to compose herself and her father
and brother were too much engaged in pre-
parations to pay any attention to other
matters; as for Ned he was maneuvering to
keep peace with the two disappointed lov-
ers, and so Penelope had time for reflection.
Her maid consoled her pride and vanity by
saying “she was sure it was no harm for a
pretty young woman to dress to please
visitors, and of course, honey, you were
perfectly innocent, tho’ if you had known
Mr. Severn was coming home you would
have worn moss roses and violets and pale
blue silk instead of that gay figured satin
with the poppies, and I know the Lieut. is
the favorite by your blushes.”

[Column 3]
	Poor Penelope tried in vain to hush the
voluble maid, who simply adored her young
mistress as she had the mother whom she
had served when Penelope was a child, and
who had been dead now some years; and as
trusted servants will she took great liber-
ities in talking, not only to Miss Penelope
but to Miss Penelope’s lovers, whenever
she had an opportunity making each in
turn believe he was the prime favorite
	Finally the dinner gong rung and Penel-
ope pale and trembling dressed demurely in
Quaker gray satin, emerged from her room
and went almost stealthily to the great,
oaken dining hall. Her brother had come
forward and to disguise the awkward
library scene from his father, said, “My
sister, I have a surprise for you, here is our
young Lieut. home on furlough, and Mr.
Plympton and the Duke of Weldmore, Lady
Eliza and Miss Bessie, his daughters,” and
so on through the entire company. Mr.
Dilliway was in such high spirits that he
did not observe the sedate and studied man-
ner of his daughter, nor the black looks in-
terchanged between the three suitors. He
thought only of the grand dinner and the
grander guests, who had come at his invita-
tion to partake his hospitality; for rich as
he was, favored by fortune, he had no
claim to titles and it was something of a
condescension on their part, to have his
table filled with men and women of rank
and title. He had no idea a storm was
brewing there and then that would end in
sorrow and disgrace.
	Dowager Lady Cushing sat at the table
by Miss Dilliway for in those days it was
not proper for unmarried ladies to enter-
tain at dinner, unless they had reached an
uncertain age; and after much feasting and
gay interchange of witty toasting and rep-
artee the ladies retired to the drawing room
and Penelope breathed more freely. Two
or three times Lady Cushing rallied her for
lack of her usual witticisms, she was an ad-
mirer of Penelope’s beauty and would glad-
ly have secured her as a wife for her young-
est son, but he was sensible enough not to
venture into the field with so many rivals,
even at his mother’s earnest solicitation.
Ned Dilliway and Harry Severn excused
themselves soon after the ladies withdrew,
and went into the open air where they
could speak freely. Ned promised Harry if
he would be discreet, he would secure an in-
terview for him with his sister, whom he
was sure loved him best, notwithstanding
appearances to the contrary, and the Lieut.
went away out of sorts leaving his adieux
for Ned to make. In the dining room the
Duke boasted of his diplomacy in courtship
so openly that he angered Charles Plymp-
ton who bandied words with him until Mr.
Dilliway was obliged to interfere and sue
for peace, at least while under his roof at
Xmas time.
	Lady Cushing and the duke’s daughters
took affectionate leave of Miss Dilliway,
when the hour came for going, declaring
the dinner, the music and the entire
affair a perfect entertainment, and when
Lady Cushing kissed her she said, “Poor,
dear Penelope, what a pity you can’t marry
all your beaux. I’m sure you don’t know
which to choose between so many. I wish
my Dick would enter the contest.”
	Penelope half bewildered stammered out
“O don’t, Lady Cushing, spare him any share
of humiliation I am bringing on my-
self.”
	It was the first out break she had made;
(Continued on page 8[-].)


(Continued from page 87.)
but in a sense it was a sort of relief. She
had neither a mother, aunt or sister in whom
to confide.
	A day or two followed in pouring rain
and sleet and it was almost impos-
sible to go outside, no visitors came though
it was holiday week; and the real Christ-
mas, the day after the party was more for
the servants and people employed by them
than for the family. Penelope’s tears were
almost like the downpour of rain and she
begged Ned to make everything right with
Harry, and so he did and planned a meet-
ing which ended in a reconciliation and a
promise of marriage, as soon as the father’s
consent could be obtained. The Lieut. re-
turned to his regiment and Penelope set
about preparations for the bridal trousseau,
as she had independent means of her own,
and was left much to herself in matters of
expenditure, it was an easy thing to do.
London affords great opportunities for shop-
ping, and Honore knew all the best places
and establishments. The carriage was al-


[Column 1] 
ways at Penelope's disposal and no ques-
tions were asked. Mr Dilliway saw Plymp-
ton come and go often, and thought his
plans were working well; for the Lieut. 
had gone and of the difficulty between the
young men he was totally unaware. Oc-
casionally the old duke came and made
himself officiously familiar and Mr. Dilliway
chaffed Penelope unmercifully about her
old lover; but she kept her own counsel
and took no one into her confidence except
her brother Ned and Honore. And the
winter passed away with only a few house
parties here and there, the Plympton’s and
Dick Cushing would gladly have escorted
Miss Dilliway anywhere, and she had the
entree into the best society, for her beauty
and her voice were almost marvelous;
wherever she went she attracted great at
tention, and the young men in her set were
wild in her praise.
	The old Duke of Osmond died very sud-
denly at last, although his death had been
long talked of, and Charles Plympton the
rightful heir, very soon came into the estate,
assuming the name and title. Mr. Dilli-
way rested secure; to all appearances Charles
was in proud possession of the field, and he
began to urge his daughter to prepare for
marriage settlements, and to purchase
bridal apparel. It was not difficult for Miss
Dilliway to dissemble. Honore thought it
all right and Ned did not object, now that
he felt sure she would be true to Harry,
and Mr. Dilliway spoke of the affair in the
most positive way. The young Duke was
so elated with his newly acquired honors,
and Penelope was so amiable with him,
that he was deceived by appearances into
believing that she was to be his wife though
her manner was always distant and haugh-
ty. He felt he had none of a lover’s privi-
leges, and yet he was so vain and selfish
that he did not take it to heart. There had
really been no courting, but Charles had an
idea it was the proper thing among people
of rank to show no sort of weakness.
	Mr. Dilliway made up his mind the wed-
ding should take place in June and Honore
had given the duke to understand her mis-
tress had consented. And she also added,
“Everything is ready except the legal mat-
ters which can be arranged when the time
comes.”
	Ned was in communication with Harry
and had informed him that his father had
consented to Penelope’s marriage in June
and that he would be ready, and when the
final settlements were to be made Penelope
would be firm in her resolution to marry
none but her own true lover, and his father
tho’ reluctant would certainly consent to
her choice at last and all would be well.
Ned also informed him that Plympton was
sweet on Lady Eliza, eldest daughter of
Duke Weldmore and in fact showed her
marked attention and if he ascertained the
true state of his sisters affection he would
soon console himself by espousing Lady
Eliza, who was really a very proper young
person and had always been accustomed to
wealth and refinement, and would make a
very elegant duchess. 
	Harry Severn was too much in 1ove to
wait until sent for, and hurried up to Lon-
don two or three weeks before he was ex-
pected; when hearing from his old friends
that Penelope Dilliway was engaged to
Plympton became so enraged that he called
upon him at his own place, and proceeded
forthwith to accuse him of winning his
sweetheart’s affection. Both were hot-

[Column 2]
tempered and a challenge was the immedi-
ate result of the interview. The affair was
to be private and each was to choose a sec-
ond. Naturally Harry chose Ned Dilli-
way and the duke one of his own men. It
was to take place the following day and the
night was spent in secret preparations for
the encounter. Ned Dilliway was reluctant
to serve as second but he yielded, believing
Harry to be so good a lance that he would
come off conquerer and all would be well.
However, he would not consent to its tak-
ing place on English ground, and so they
crossed the Channel. It was not an un-
usual thing for Ned to be away from home,
and neither Penelope nor her father
dreamed of any mischief brewing.
	The duel was fought, the duke escaped
without a scratch, but Harry was wounded
and Ned was obliged to carry home the
wounded man and bear the unwelcome
news to his father, who was so overcome
with rage and disappointment that he died
shortly after in a fit of appoplexy. Penelope
suffered untold agonies, she put her bridal
apparel away, determined never to go into
society again, but retire from the world of
pride and fashion. The duke went abroad
until the affair should blow over. Ned was
constantly with Harry Severn until he re-
covered sufficiently to go away on the con-
tinent, he was obliged to leave the service,
and within three years from that Christmas
eve things had undergone a great change in
the Dilliway house. Ned married Miss
Hester Cushing, Dick’s younger sister,
some years later, Harry Severn was
drowned at sea while crossing on the Medit-
teranean, during his convalescence which
had been slow and tedious. During all 
these years Aunt Dilliway had never seen
him, though her heart was constant and
had he not met an untimely death they
would have married. Duke Osmond came
back to England and fairly persecuted Miss
Dilliway but she sternly refused to see him
and they never met except by accident,
when she would be taking exercise in the
parks or suburbs, and then he was persis-
tent in offering her attentions, which she
obstinately refused when possible. She
sent back his letters unopened, refused gifts
sent anonymously and finally made up her
mind that it was impossible to live in Eng-
land.

	Such was Aunt Dilliway’s love story
told to us with all sorts of embellishments.
And no better object lesson could have
been given to a bevy of thoughtless, roman-
tic girls. It was little wonder that Aunt
Dilliway lived the life of a recluse with
such a past behind her. Ned Dilliway was
Pamela’s grandfather and both her grand-
parents and parents were then quietly sleep-
ing in the Dilliway cemetery. Pamela was
left an orphan when only six years old and
had been sent to her aunt in America who
was also appointed her guardian; but when
she became aware of the escapades and
treachery of Pamela’s governess she was
without doubt seriously alarmed. The
governess had been sent from England, in
care of the little girl and had been trusted
as one of the household. Aunt Dilliway
thought the wisest course would be to send
her into the country to her uncle in whom
she had great confidence. Pamela had been
fondly attached to Belmont, and was very
much grieved at parting from her; previous
however, to her going she had given the
girl sound advice, warning her against and

[Column 3]
sort of deception by telling her incidents in
her own experience.
	Shortly after our ramble among the hills
news came of the sudden decease of Penel-
ope Dilliway and Pamela left for Boston
with her uncle. We never saw her after
but heard of her return to England, and
through the Kingsley’s that her aunt had
left a very peculiar will and a letter ad-
dressed to her grand niece warning her
against the temptations of pride, vanity,
and above all coquetry, and the love of
dress and of admiration, setting forth elo-
quently her own mistakes in early life and
dilating upon her long years of suffering,
repentance and prayers for divine mercy
and forgiveness; imploring Pamela never to
practice deceit of any kind, but to be con-
stant, virtuous truthful and upright in all
things, which was the only safe course to
pursue and the sure road to happiness here
and hereafter.
	This love story briefly told should be a
warning to every vain and self-satisfied
young girl, who longs for attention and
admiration from the opposite sex, or from
the world; many innocent ones fall into
snares and are led astray through vanity
and inordinate pride. If girls could only
realize the untold misery and life-long sor-
row they would bring upon themselves and
their loved ones, they would assuredly pause
in their mad career and never allow their
good name to be tarnished by being called
a flirt.
	“Oh what a tangled web we weave
	When first we venture to deceive.”
							E. B. W.

SALT LAKE CITY JANUARY 1, 1898.


[Column 1]
A NEW YEAR'S GREETING.

	To the host of friends here and else-
where we offer our Happy New Year in
love and with best wishes for happiness and
prosperity during the year just entering
upon the threshold of Time. May it bring
with its varied seasons prosperity and peace
to the nation in which we live; may the
dark shadows which still linger in the
horizon of the year just past be quickly
dispersed and war no more like a bird
of ill-omen hoverover our fair land.
	Although the year 1898 opened aus-
piciously and civilization has made great pro-
gress along many lines, yet the war has
made terrible suffering and sorrow in tens of
thousands of homes the country over be-
sides the hardships, wounds and deaths of
the brave volunteers and soldiers them-
selves. Time alone can assauge the devas-
tation the war has occasioned, but even that
will not heal broken-hearted mothers, wives
and sweethearts.
	Utah was more fortunate than most other
states in this respect, and we know that our
boys and men who volunteered went forth
with the prayers and blessings and united
faith of thousands of the Latter-day Saints.
Yet many have returned home weakened
by fever and malaria, still we rejoice that
they are with us yet and hope for their
speedy recovery.
	The women of the Red Cross Society
throughout the land have done bravely and
here in our own state valiant work has been
accomplished; very little has been said or
published of these noble efforts put forth, in
philanthropic charity, bnt we know much
concerning it and how far-reaching it has
been.
	It is a matter of congratulation that
President McKinley mentioned the Red
Cross and its noble President, Clara Barton,
it is the first recognition of woman in the
message of a President of the United States
that we are able to recall. Let us hope that
it is a good omen for woman and that the
day so long looked for and so ardently de-
sired, when woman shall have her rightfnl
place in the annals of the nation, and in all
places and all measures that effect the wel-
fare of the people is not far distant.
	Would it could come to pass in Susan B.
Anthony's time or Elizabeth Cady Stan-
ton's whose courage in advancing human
progress has been so marked for more than
half a century?

[Column 2]
	But women have the courage not only to
act in the fruitful present but to wait with
sublime hope for the coming time, when
all over this free and happy land an era of
progress shall come to pass such as has
never been in any land of which we have a
knowledge. And let us believe it is not
far off. Many wonderful things have trans-
pired in the year to which we now say adieu,
and science and electricity and many new
forces in the material world are pushing
their way to aid mankind materially and
are not moral and spiritual forces doing
battle for the uplifting of humanity. There
is and must necessarily be elements in the
universe, if we but knew how to grasp them
that would aid the triumph of light over
darkness. We as a people know something
of this power, of the faith that can heal the
sick, and through prayer and humility bear
us up when all others means are powerless.
	And now again A Happy New Year to
one and all of the readers of our dear little
“Exponent.” May the year 1899 be a
year of prosperity and success beyond our
expectations in literary work; for the wo-
men of the Church and of this State need
more history written and more home litera-
ture instead of so much that is not worth
the time spent on it and money expended
for it. Our young women do not appreciate
the efforts put forth by the women who
have laid the foundation in this line of
work. They are even inclined, at least the
majority of them to under value pioneer
journalistic efforts, though it has preserved
for the good of the community many
records, histories and sketches of important
trensactions, educational, political, and of
organizations and industries.
	At the present time there are great
deficiencies to be met in closing up old ac-
counts, and starting with the new manage-
ment of the Deseret News Company, even
if it is possible to pull through, but the
Relief Society and those who believe in an
official organ for its advancement should
make strennous efforts to secure sufficient
subrcriptions to tide over the crisis. Other
papers have had similar experiences and
friends have come forward to assist
pecuniarily and given substantial help.
The only thing wanting for the "Ex
ponent to make it financially successful
is a larger circulation. Have the women
of Zion the active interest of the Relief
Society and the silk industry at heart and
if so will they co-operate in raising the
means necessary?
	The "Exponent" has endeared the writer
to very many of the sisters, who have been
its patrons, and the personal friendships and
sisterly relations by interchange of calls,
visits and correspondence have been very
gratifying and beneficial in many waps.
	To tell how dearly we love it and those
who have been its advocates and contribu-
tors would be a difficult task, beyond lan-
guage to express; only when heart speak
to heart can it be understood. May the
new year prove how we all love the "Ex-
ponent" by substantial patronage.


AN APPEAL.
	The agents of the “Exponent” in all the 
several wards and Stakes of Zion, and also 
individual subscribers indebted for the 
paper are urgently requested to settle all 
old accounts at the earliest date possible.
This becomes an absolute necessity on ac-
count of the change that takes place in the


management of the Deseret Evening News. 
The “Exponent’’ will not be sent to any 
subscriber after January 1899 unless pre-
paid. All delinquents will as soon as pos-
sible receive written notices of bills due in 
addition to the printed matter. 
	In consideration of the value of the “Ex-
ponent’’ to the Relief Society, and because 
of the work it has done in the cause of suf-
frage as well as the general educational 
help it has given the women of this inter-
mountain country.it seems scarcely possible
that women should be so careless of their 
own interest as to let the paper languish for 
want of support, yet the fact remains, and 
it is impossible to say what the women will 
do in the matter. 
	With the Relief Society of 30,000 women 
if one tenth of the number would take the 
paper and pay cash down it could be carried 
safely, yet that has been looked for, hoped 
for and waited for in vain. What the “Ex-
ponent has done to keep the society in 
various Stakes and in different parts of the 
world in touch with each other is of more 
value than the few hundred dollars it would 
take to maintain it independently. Appeal
after appeal has been made through the 
columns of the paper and by those who are 
its faithful supporters in public and in pri-
vate, and yet the failure to come forward 
with financial aid. At one dollar per year 
it seems incredible that the sisters who 
have the cause of Zion at heart and know 
as many hundreds do know the generous 
help the “Exponent’’ has given in ways al-
most innumerable, should not immediately 
raise the necessary funds, through large 
numbers of aubscriptions. Besides the 
great need the Relief Society has for an or-
gan of its own, the silk industry needs it
quite as much and both should have its 
support through the liberal patronage of 
the women of Zion.


Lady Warwick among English women is 
an excellent public speaker, fluent, easy 
and pleasing.


DR. ROBERT’S NURSING CLASS. 
	The nurse’s class taught by Dr. Margaret 
C. Roberts in the Templeton for the last 
six months or more, held its graduating ex-
ercises on Tuesday Dec. 20, Counselor An-
nie T. Hyde presiding in the absence of 
President Horne. Dr. Roberts gave the in-
structions free in the interest and under the 
auspices of the Relief Society in this Stake 
of Zion. The members of the class acquit-
ted themselves admirably, and the Dr. her-
self read a very able and highly instructive
paper, (which we would gladly have pub-
lished if space would permit.) Congratula-
tory remarks were made by President B. 
W. Smith, E. B. Wells and L. D. Alder, 
also Counselors Clara C. Cannon and 
Annie T. Hyde; Elder C. W. Penrose oc-
cupied a few moments. Sister Hyde in be-
half of the Stake Board presented to Dr. 
Roberts in a neat and happy speech a hand-
some rocking chair in appreciation of her 
generous labors, and the class through one 
of the students, Sister Jones, presented a 
silver cake dish filled with lovely flowers. 
Other gifts and tokens of love suitable to 
the happy occasion were made and the good 
spirit manifested itself very perceptibly. If 
the graduating nurses will be as conscien-
tious in their duties as the Dr. has been in 
giving them instruction the benefit to the 
society will be productive of great good.


WOMAN'S EXPONENT.


EDITORIAL NOTES. 
	On Sunday July 31, 1898, Apostle J. 
W Taylor, Elders Horace Ensign and Fred
Graham with Susa Y. Gates organized a 
Relief Society in Pueblo, Colorado, Sister
Gates presiding at the meeting. Apostle 
Taylor told the sisters they had the privi-
lege of choosing their own president. They 
selected Mrs. Elizabeth Hart who chose 
for her counselors Amanda Coleman and 
Emma Simmons, Ella Branch, secretary 
and Turner Johnson treasurer. The soci-
ety seems to be flourishing and meetings 
are well attended, the sick, the poor and 
needy are attended to according to their 
needs. They have a little money on hand 
and are making a quilt, so we learn from 
a letter recently received. 
	We have been notified by letter of the 
change of officers made in the Relief Soci-
ety of Sevier Stake. The president, Sister 
Elizabeth Bean, and her first counselor, 
Sister Thurza Thurber, had presided since 
the first organization nearly twenty years 
ago, but on account of disadvantageous cir-
cumstances preferred to resign. The con-
ference was held at Glenwood and Elder 
Joseph Horne, counselor to the president of 
the Stake, was present and the resignations 
were accepted and a new Board of officers 
elected, Christina Sorenson, president;
Mary Magleby and Sister Tueson, counsel-
ors; Mary Swindle, secretary, and Caroline 
Bowman, treasurer. Sisters Bean and 
Thurber are satisfied with their labors and 
feel they have the love and confidence of 
the sisters. It was a great surprise to the 
General Board as they knew nothing of the 
change of officers.


R. S. REPORT. 

SALT LAKE STAKE. 
	The quarterly Stake conference of the
Relief Society convened December 3, 1898,
in the 14th ward Assembly Hall, at 10 a.
m. Present on the stand were President
M. I. Horne, Counselors Annie T. Hyde
and Clara C. Cannon, Presidents Zina D.
H. Young and Bathsheba W. Smith of the
General Board;and also of the Stake Board;
Julia C. Howe and Lydia D. Alder, aids,
Maria W. Wilcox, corresponding secretary;
Augusta W. Grant, recording secretary.
	Meeting opened by singing “God moves
in a mysterious way.” Prayer by Sister
Julia C. Howe. Singing “The resurrec-
tion day,” in remembrance of Sister Sarah
M. Kimball whose death was announced by
Sister Horne. The roll was then called.
	President Horne wished the sisters to re-
member the meeting is held the first Satur-
day in the month each quarter. The name
of Sister Augusta W. Grant was placed be-
fore the conference, and she was sustained
unanimously as Stake secretary.
	Presidents of the country wards were
were called upon to report.
	President Horne was pleased to hear the
reports, From her visits to the different
associations she found them all in good con-
dition. Those who donate will be blessed,
but those who do the spiritual work, which
is very important, will have additional
blessings. Now that winter is coming on
we must look after the worthy poor, they
must be taken care of. Prayed that the
Lord would bless us all and especially those
who have come so far this morning in the
cold. Spoke of the death of Sister Sarah
M. Kimball, and her faithfulness and good
works in life.
	President M. W. Wilcox, 4th ward, Salt
Lake City, spoke of the work of the aged
sisters who try to help themselves. Have
made 150 yards of carpet.
	President Zina D. H. Young said, “This
organization is from the Lord. I am
thankful that I am able to be here today.”
Gave reminiscences of the days of Nauvoo,
and hoped the sisters here would never
have to go through such trials. Spoke of
Sister Kimball’s good works.
	President Bathsheba W. Smith thought
now that Sister Kimball was gone she was
the only one who had been present at the
first organization of the Relief Society.
Wanted to see the Relief Society wide
awake and ready to do their duty. Take
an interest in our neighbors, do good to all
and work in the Temple for the dead.
	Conference adjourned until 2 p. m. Sing-
ing “The God we adore.” Benediction by
President Sarah J. Cannon.
	Afternoon session 2 p. m. Singing, “O,
my Father.” Prayer by Sister E. L.
Webb. Singing “Where the voice of
friendship’s heard.’’ Minutes of the last
quarterly conference read and approved.
	Counselor Annie T. Hyde felt honored to

(Column 2)
see so many of the elderly sisters, those
who had spent their lives in good works. 
Exhibited the album presented to Sister
Horne on her 80th birthday by the Relief
Society, and said it was a very appropriate
and beautiful tribute to one worthy of all
we can do. All of our young people do not
appreciate the work that is done by the sis-
ters and the Relief Society. The presidents
should be alive to their work and not allow
interest to lag. All strive to create an in-
terest, invite some one to come and give
special subjects, a lecture, or what-
ever they can do. They are mother’s class-
es. "Sister Horne wishes me to say that
she feels that the Lord inspired the sisters 
to do what they did for her, to make her
eightieth birthday memorable, and she is
very grateful.” Spoke of Sister Kimball
and said she was the only woman so far as
she knew that had laid the corner stone for 
a building, the Relief Society hall in the
15th ward.
	Counselor Clara C. Cannon said she felt
very grateful to the Lord that she had been
raised from a bed of severe  sickness. Said
we cannot be grateful enough for His many
blessings, our lives should be such that we
will be living examples to the young peo- 
ple.	 Much depends upon the mothers,
they can teach their children not to associ-
ate or marry those outside of our Church.
The teachers can do much good by drop-
ping a word of counsel when they visit the
families, and mothers should gather their
families together when these teachers visit. 
It is not their only mission to collect con-
tributions for the poor, though that is a
great work; they should be able to expond
the scriptures. We should have something
instructive in all our society meetings,
reading or something besides reports, that
the young people may be attracted to the
meetings.
	Sister Horne raquested that the revela-
tion given to Emma Smith should be read 
by the secretary.	This is the 25th section
in the Doctrine and Covenants. President 
Horne made a few remarks on the same
subject and advised the sisters to read more
of these revelations, and take note of their
meaning, it will be a great benefit to them 
and they can see that the prophecies are be-
ing fulfilled-	The Prophet Joseph Smith
always had great respect and consideration
for the sisters.
	Sister E. Stevenson said when the war
was declared she felt that the way would
be opened and the Gospel carried to many 
people who had not heard it before. We
should be careful of our conversation be-
fore our children, improve our time and in-
form ourselves in the principles of the Gos-
pel. If we are tried remember that "Whom
the Lord loveth He chasteneth.”	Instil
faith into the hearts of your children, appre-
ciate the blessing and privilege of going
into the house of the Lord, and let us live
our religion, give great attention to our
children, the mission of motherhood is the
greatest on the earth.
	Sister L. D. Alder said, "I love the Gos-
pel, and I love all those who love the Gos-
pel.	Those who work and serve the Lord
by devoting themselves to their children are
doing a great work; but those who add to
this work by laboring in the Relief Society
need not neglect these duties." Paid a lov-
ing tribute to the memory of Sister Kimball.
Said let us make our choice of things for a
better world.	There should be no mourn-
ing when we leave this world. Great and


[Column 1]
glorious will be the reward of the faithful,
we are free to choose our lives, but must be
satisfied with the result.
	Sister Mary S. Clark of Farmington said
she could not remember when she did not
have a testimony of the Gospel. It seems
so consistent and reasonable. Felt the
wonderful influence of the good spirit in
this meeting, and felt paid for all the trials
she had passed through. She greatly
appreciated the changes that had taken
place and the many comforts that the Saints
have now. Bore a strong testimony and
told of many of the manifestations of the
goodness and power of God. Don’t let
little things influence us too much, but
think of the great things of the Gospel.
May we know our duty and strive to do it.
	Sister Horne stated that in some of the
the wards the ReliefSociety presidents speak
in the wards and we would try to make
this a rule in all the wards, to speak the
Sunday evening before conference. Please
remember presidents, that the first Satur-
day in March, always the Saturday before
fast day, every three months. Do not let
us be discouraged if the younger people
look upon us as being non-progressive, we
do our work quietly and do not herald our
labors by the sound of trumpets, but if we
continue faithfully to work in an earnest,
quiet way, we will be rewarded.
	Couference adjourned for three months,
first Saturday in March. Singing “How
firm a foundation.” Benediction by Presi-
dent Horne in the gift of tongues, inter-
preted by President Zina D. H. Young.
					A. W. GRANT, Sec.

			EMERY STAKE.
	Minutes of the Relief Society Conference,
of Emery State. Held at Huntington,
Oct. 31st, 1898. Annie U. Larsen presid-
ing. Meeting commencing 10 a. m. by
the choir singing, “Come, Oh Thou King
of Kings,’’ under the leadership of Profes-
sor Hardie. Prayer by Brother Hardie.
The choir sang, “The happy day is rolling
on.” President Annie U. Larson, spoke
at some length, gave a report of the Stake.
Said, she with her counselors, had made a
visit to all the branches since our last con-
ference had found the sisters anxious to
adhere to counsel. Many had and were
building halls and granaries and storing
up wheat for time of need. There was a
steadily growing interest in silk culture.
Many had planted trees and cuttings and
some had raised a few worms; she had on
exhibition some beautiful cocoons and a
twist of silk that had been raised in Emery
County; she was pleased that the sisters
were willing to profit by the advice of the
stake board. After which Huntington,
Cleveland, Castle Gate, Orangeville, Castle
Dale, and Ferron were represented. Each
president substantiating the report of
President Larson. Bishop Johnson,of Hun-
tington spoke to the sisters, a short time en-
couraged them to lay up grain and hoped
the brethren would aid them what they
could.
	Congregation sang, “We thank thee O
God for a Prophet.” Benediction by Brother
M. E. Johnson.
	Afternoon session: Choir sang “Great
is the Lord” prayer by Brother Charles
Pulsipher. Sang, "How will the Saints
rejoice to tell.” Annie Larsen announced
the visitors from Salt Lake. Elder L. John
Nuttall, Sisters Stevenson and Alder. The
President from Price, Sister Pace gave a

[Column 2]
report. After which Sister Stevenson ad-
dressed the conference. She said mother-
hood was the greatest duty of life, urged
the older sisters to give their experiences to
theyoung mothers, teach them how to care
forthe babies when sick, thought many
little ones were taken away through not
knowing how to properly care for them.
A mother’s first duty was the care of her
children, all others were secondary. The
sisters should teach house-keeping in the
society; felt that ignorance on the subject
was the cause of many divorce cases.
This should be steadily improved upon by
the careful teachings of the mothers. Our
daughters married our sons and it should be
the careful study of every mother that they
were in every way worthy of each other.
Teach them virtue and self denial. Sister
Lydia D. Alder spoke of the importance of
attending the Relief Society said that sisters
who labored in the society would be satis-
fied with the life they had spent. It was
necessary that mothers should attend the
Sabbath school as much as anything else,
by blessing we are blessed. Our children
may be careless and wayward, but if we do
our part. they will yet come back, God will
keep a watch care over them. Be careful
how we spend our lives. By careful
thought we progress We must not lose
sight of the fact that it is necessary to have
trials, if we staid in the same place there
would be no advancement. If it is hard
for us today do as well as we can for it may
be harder for us tomorrow.
	Elder L. John Nuttall spoke at some
length on the needs and necessities of the
Relief Society. Said that each president
should take a pride in her society and make
it the best, the Prophet Joseph said the duty
of this society was to save souls, gave
several ways how this could be done. It
was the duty of the sisters to learn the
Gospel that they might be able to convert
others, many women would be called upon
missions and it was necessary to inform our
selves that we could do credit to the church
to which we belonged. He read extracts
from the minutes of the first organization
and commented upon it. Meeting closed
by singing “Now let us rejoice in the day of
salvation.” Prayer by Bishop Robertson.
	An officers meeting was called just after
the afternoon session. Elder Nuttall there
explained many things that were of great
importance to the sisters.
	An evening session was held, the choir
sang "Behold the Savior comes,” prayer by
Elder C. R. Johnson. Sang “Ye wander-
ing nations.” Elder Nuttall occupied most
of the time in a very instructive and in-
teresting manner; spoke of the first or-
ganization of the Relief Society its aim and
object, how it had been a great help to the
church, it being the first organization of
women on the earth and that the Prophet
Joseph had said he had turned the key for
women, how many organizations of women
had been effected since. and the good we
could do providing we never lost the spirit
of the gospel, we should all declare to our-
selves that “As for me and my house I in-
tend to serve the Lord.’’
	Sister Estella Colard sang “My Mountain
Home.”
	Sister Stevenson spoke of the silk culture,
encouraged the young ladies to join the
Relief Society.
	President Annie U. Larson made a few
remarks and said we would meet again at
9:30 a. m. Saturday, as the children’s con- 
ference would have a portion of the forenoon

[Column 3]
Choir sang “Behold the Mountain of the
Lord.” Benediction by Elder H. A.
Fowler.
	Saturday, 9:30: Annie Larson presiding.
Choir sang “Sweet is the work my God my
King,” prayer by Elder Samuel Rowley.
Sang, “Are we yet alive.”
	Sister Alder spoke of the inventions
that are in our day. It was a day of ad-
vancement, if we were not careful we
would be left behind. Urged the sisters to
cultivate themselves; we had no time to
lose. We should brighten up our mind
and make ourselves useful; explained
clearly how much good the Exponent had
been to the sisters, was anxious that the
members of the Relief Society should sus-
tain it. Advised the sisters to make a
study of the Book of Mormon. It was
necessary for the president to study the
members and help them to cultivate the
talent that was in them: we should make
confidents of our daughters and draw them
nearer to us that they might in return
make confidents of us, give them some-
thing to do in the Relief Society.
	Elder Nuttall was the next speaker. He
endorsed the remarks of the former speaker,
spoke of the difference in early times and
now educationally, read some of the min-
utes of the Relief Society held in the days
of the Prophet Joseph, said the sisters had
the right to lay hands on the sick.
	Stake Counselor Pulispher was pleased
with the conference and very thankful the
visitors had met with us.
	Stake Gounselor Josie E. Childs pre-
sented the general and stake officers of the
society which were unanimously sustained.
President Annie Larsen said the next con-
ference would be at Ferron Sang “Joy to
the world” Benediction by Elder Thomas
Hardie.
					M. J. SHIPP, Sec.

			SANPETE STAKE. 
	Minutes of Relief Society Conference
held in Moroni Dec. 9th, 1898. Counselor
A. L. Cox presiding, singing “How firm a
foundation.” Opening prayer by Elder
James Jensen; singing, “Now let us re-
joice” etc. Minutes read and approved.
	Counselor Cox made opening remarks.
Was pleased to meet with the sisters in
conference, but she very much regretted
the absence of our beloved president Sister
M. A. Hyde, who is seriousy ill, hoped the
sisters would all remember her in their
prayers. Roll call showed a fair repre-
sentation considering the severity of the
weather.
	The different branches were represented.
	Counselor A. L. Cox was greatly pleased
and encouraged by the favorable reports
and especially the increased attendance.
Said it was not the destiny of the Latter-
day Saints to stand still and was glad to
hear of the advance made along the lines of
Relief Society work. Was also pleased to
hear of increased diligence in storing grain.
Said before another conference a new year
will be ushered in. Hoped the spirit of im-
provement would continue to be manifest
in all our labors. Conference adjourned
till 2 p. m. Singing “Praise to the Man”
etc. Benediction by Sister Christina
Thompson.
	Afternoon session: Singing “The Spirit
of God” etc. Prayer by Sister Jane Bench,
Singing, “For the strength of the hills”
etc, 
	Counselor A. L. Cox was much gratified 
that so many of the presidents had ex-


pressed their intention of building granaries
for themselves, as it showed a desire to have
their wheat properly taken care of and thus
encourage others to contribute grain for
storing. Made reference to the recently ap-
pointed Annual Day on March 17; urged
the necessity of a general observance of the
day and also the collection of the ten cent
donation. Requested the sisters to sustain
the “Exponent” in a society capacity and
so far as possible, as individuals. Also
spoke on silk, dwelt at some length on the
duties of teachers, and the necessity of
seeking divine aid in their labors.
	Counselor Abagail Shoemaker was much
pleased to hear of the increased diligence
manifested in storing grain. It is a mission
placed upon us which we should strive to
fulfill to the best of our ability. Was
thankful that the Prophet Joseph had been
impressed to organize the Relief Society,
but that we be diligent if we expect to re-
receive the blessings pronounced to the
faithful.
	Sister Shoemaker said that President
McAllister had expressed a wish that the
Relief Society make some carpet for the
Temple, which she was sure would be ac-
complished.
	Sister Cox made closing remarks. Spoke
of the necessity of cultivating the gift of
faith, and of the marvelous escape of the
Utah boys in the battles, at Manila, which
she considered largely due to the prayers of
the Saints in their behalf. 
	Conference adjourned for three months.
Singing, “We thank thee O, God for a
Prophet.” Benediction by A, L. Cox.
	ALVIRA L. COX, Cor Sec.


LADIES’ SEMI-MONTHLY MEETING

	Semi-monthly meeting held November,
26th, 1898, in the 14th ward hall, President
M. I. Horne presiding, opened by singing,
“Let those who would be Saints indeed;”
prayer Sister Brown; singing “Zion stands
with hills surrounded;” Minutes read and
approved. President M. I. Horne said,
“this is a proving day and a pruning day;
we have our liberty, our choice, our agency,
we can serve God or not; if we attend to
our prayers and our duties we shall not go
astray, so said Joseph Smith, “Satan has
great power in deceiving and leading the
people away.” Exhorted all to be careful
of seducing spirits. Spoke of the suffer-
ings of Paul the Apostle how he had been
tried, said she felt well and thanked all for
the love expressed by her sisters on her
birthday, she thanked the Lord that He
had blessed her in raising her children and
begged all young mothers to ask God con-
tinually for His support to help them fill
their missions as wife and mother. Spoke
of the good seed we might sow day by day,
if we live as God wished us too. An-
nounced that the first Saturday every three
months would be Relief Society conference
and next Saturday was the appointed day.
Sisters Peterson, Liddle, Waterfall, Mary
P. Silver, Jones, Barton and Irvine bore
testimony of God’s goodness to them and
said many good things, in appreciation of
the blessings of the Saints. Congregation
sang, "Arise O glorious Zion.”
	Counselor Bathsheba Smith spoke of the
pernicious habit of smoking, thought we
might influence boys we meet, to avoid the
use of the tobacco, felt parents should rule


with kindness, and love. Sisters Ellis,
Cooper and others each spoke a few
words and bore their testimony.
	President Horne said we must expect
persecution, our Savior told us this, but we
should all be thankful that the knowledge
of the Gospel cannot be taken from us.
Meeting adjourned for two weeks. Sang
Doxology, Benediction.
		JULIA S. WOOLLEY, Sec.

[Column 1]
RELIEF SOCIETY IN OMAHA.

	The members of the Omaha branch of
the Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day
Saints met Oct. 25, 1898, at 3 p. m., at the
home of Sister Clara M. Fredricksen, 1604
No. 29 St., Omaha, Nebraska, for the pur-
pose of organizing a Relief Society. There
were present Apostle Franklin D. Richards,
Sister Jane S. Richards, first counselor in
the general presidency of the Relief Society,
Emmeline B. Wells, general secretary,
Josephine Richards West, Bishop Peter
Barton and Ellen A. Barton of Kaysville,
Utah, Elder J. L. Hatch, president of
Omaha branch, Elders R. E. Yeates and
Daniel Mackay, Sisters Mary M. Miller,
Mary E. Blixt, Addie S. Stone, Ida A.
Peterson, Anna K. Gregerson, Caroline C.
E. Peterson, Mary M. Weeks, Clara M.
Fredricksen, Elizabeth Hover, Caroline S.
Tromler and Anna B. Olson.
	Meeting was called to order at 3 o’clock
p. m. by Elder J. L. Hatch. The hymn
which begins as follows was sung: “How
firm a foundation.” Prayer by Elder R. E
Yeates. The hymn which begins “Now
let us rejoice” was sung. The meeting
was then presided over by Apostle Richards.
	Sister Richards expressed her joy and
satisfaction in being present with us today.
Referred to the time when the society was
organized with but few members, in the
days of the Prophet Joseph Smith. Then
they endured many hardships, being sur-
rounded by mob violence; subsequently
finding a peaceful home in the valleys of
the mountains the society was reorganized
by President Brigham Young, and has
spread and grown until now a society is
found in almost every branch and ward of

[Column 2]
the Church, numbering in all about thirty
thousand sisters. Sister Richards said Sis-
ter Zina D. H. Young who now presides
over the society in all the world, would be
pleased to hear of an organization in Omaha
where they suffered so much years ago.
“Although few in numbers you will not re-
gret being organized or miss the time spent
in the performance of duties. Meet together
as often as possible, once a week or once in
two weeks. Your principal duty will be
to care for the poor and needy, to adminis-
ter to the wants of the sick. Follow the
example of the Savior who cared
for all that were in need. At-
tend to those in need of assistance whether
they are of the house of Israel or not.” A
vote was called showing that all the sisters
present were in favor of being organized.
	Bishop Barton then spoke. He was
pleased to be present on such an occasion
and endorsed the remarks of Sister Rich-
ards. As a Bishop in one of Zion’s wards
he could appreciate the work of the Relief
Society. He said, “The sisters can do
more for the sick than the brethren, and I
have told the sisters in the ward where I
live that we could not get along without
them.” Said he often attended their meet-
ings and rejoiced at seeing the good they
accomplished. Related the saying of James
how faith and works go together. Bless-
ings were pronounced upon the Omaha
sisters by Brother Barton, in taking up
their work as a Relief Society.
	Sister Ellen Barton then added her testi-
mony. She had felt strange in Omaha un-
til meeting with the Saints on Sunday and
enjoying with us the spirit of the Gospel.
She spoke of the love and strength of spirit
it imparted to the sisters to work unitedly,
and the good that could be accomplished by
their doing the will of the Father. Refer-
ence was made to the sewing meetings the
society have at home, and how the poor
were provided with quilts, etc. She asked
the Lord to bless the sisters, and said she
knew they would find the work a pleasure
and of great benefit.
	President Richards said his feelings had
been expressed by the previous speakers. It
was with pleasure and thanksgiving that
he met with the branch of the Church in
Omaha. The object of the organization
about to be effected was to do good in time
of sickness and misfortune. The Church is
not to support us, but we must work to
build up the Church. The Relief Society
was not organized or brought into existence
to make offices for some one to fill, but for
the perfecting of the people and for the re-
lief of the distressed. It is not only a mat-
ter of dimes and dollars, but to administer
with kind words and deeds to the afflicted
of all people, for we are all of one royal
family. If we live up to the covenants we
have made with God He will bless us as
we need and we will realize some benefit of
the spirit. One of the prophets has said we
realize the gifts after trials. Said the next
step would be to select officers. He named
the officers necessary and said we wanted
those whose heart and soul was in the
work, and those who could work unitedly.
A person is not fit to be in office until he is
willing to be a servant. He mentioned the
necessity of keeping strict accounts and rec-
ords of the proceedings. He asked God to
direct in the selection of officers.
	Sister Richards related some of her ex-
perience in regard to office and thought
that had it not been for the work it seemed

[Column 3]
her duty to do in the Relief Society she
would not be alive today. She said we
were not here for our good looks but to be-
come good. The sisters were then given
the privilege of selecting one of their num-
ber for a president. Sister Weeks nomina-
ted Sister Addie Stone but she declined.
Caroline Tromler named Mary M. Miller
for that position. Sister Miller expressed
willingness to do her duty. Mary E. Blixt
made the motion that Sister Miller be sus-
tained as president, which was seconded by
Mary M. Weeks. The question was then
voted upon in the affirmative by all pres-
sent. Sister Miller chose Mary E. Blixt
as her first and Addie S. Stone as her second
counselors these names were put before the
meeting in their proper order and were sus-
tained by unanimous vote Sister Clara M
Fredricksen and Mary M. Weeks were
named for the secretaryship but for accept-
able reasons each declined. Ida A. Peterson
was then nominated for secretary and was
elected by united affirmative vote.
	Caroline C. E. Peterson was chosen and
voted in as treasurer of the society. Annie
K. Gregerson was unanimously sustained
as assistant secretary.
	Sister Richards then made a few remarks,
instructing the officers to go ahead with the
work, the counselors to share the responsi-
bility with the president Thought it
would be a great help to the association to
take the “Exponent.” The teachers of the
society may be selected at the next meet-
ing. Asked the Lord to bless us all. Sis-
ter E. B. Wells regretted that she was not
present at the fore part of the meeting,
but some delay in obtaining directions
caused her to be late. She was pleased to
meet the Saints. Called attention to the
blessing the Omaha sisters enjoyed in hav-
ing an Apostle and a Bishop at this meeting.
She recalled the past when she taught
school here nearly fifty years ago. She
told how helpful the Relief Society had
been to the Church and said its power
would increase.
	The officers of the Omaha society were
then set apart to their respective callings
under the hands of President F. D. Richards,
Bishop Barton and Elder Hatch. All present
then sustained each other as members of
the society. Benediction was pronounced,
by President F. D. Richards.
					J. L. HATCH.
