WORDS THAT BLESS. 

TO SISTER MARY A FREEZE.

How oft as we journey on through life,
Does the heart grow weak from its toil and
	strife;
How oft we feel that the heavy load,
Is all too much for the long, long road! 
But a cheering voice in the stillness heard,
Sends strength through the ambient air,
Or a tender thought from a sister’s lips
Makes lighter the cross we bear.

How many eyes in the flight of years,
Are always moist with bitter tears,
Till the fountain of sorrow at last is dry,
And only the pent up soul can cry;
But an earthly angel whispers hope,
Though it seems but an idle dream,
And that hopeful whisper paves the way, 
Through which heaven’s light may stream.

How many discouraged ones there be
Who the sequel of life can so dimly see,
Who faint or falter at every turn,
Finding courage and faith so hard to learn!
To such a beautiful word is given,
A lesson of holy love,
And they who for earth will not brave the
	storm
Will try for the life above. 

There are many others whose stalwart forms
Are bowed and bent with life’s heavy storms;
Of toil they have always had their share,
They are weary now of the part they bear,
To the hearts of these tried, feeble ones,
There's a source of joy untold
In whispers of the deeds of worth
They have done in days of old.

Oh, these words of love! how much they do
To make us better—our hearts more true!
How much they help on the weary road!
How much they govern our last abode!
Yet oft in selfish hearts confined
Are the thoughts which thus might bless, 
And they never render the world more bright,
Nor make its sorrows less.

But the world of hope in which you live,
The words of comfort you often give,
The kindly letters your hand has penned 
To son or brother or distant friend,
Not only brighten the jeweled crown
Which you in heaven will wear,
But help to swell the throng who hope
To meet and love you there.

ALOFA.


[Column 1]
THE BOSTON TEA PARTY. 
	It seemed very fitting that the Massa-
chusettes state society, Daughters of the
Revolution, should observe the 125th anni-
versary of the “Tea Party day” at the old
Hancock Tavern in Corn court. For in
the very room where the plot was concocted
so many years ago the multitude of guests
was received by the state regent, Miss Sarah
E. Hunt.
	From 2 to 5 o’clock the narrow passage-
ways, the stairways and rooms were
thronged with people, all filled with the
spirit of ’73,if one could judge by their utter-
ances, and all proud of their revolutionary
ancestry.
	The large apartments of the olden time
have been divided up into smaller ones by
partitions, and in several, of these members
of the various Chapters throughout the
state served tea from tables daintily decor-
ated with yellow chrysanthemums and
lighted by buff candles with blue shades.
They wore their hair powdered and dressed
with high shell combs, and over their
shoulders wore kerchiefs in old time style.
	Souvenirs consisting of tiny boxes of tea
tied with ribbons of the continential buff
and blue were given to all the guests. The
ladies of the committee were Mrs. C. P.
Heath, of Lynn, and Miss Mary C. Sawyer
of Wellesley, who were greatly assisted in
their duties by Capt. Wadsworth of the
tavern.

	The room where the guests were received
is a queer old place, though the ancient
rafters are now hidden behind a ceiling of'
plaster. On one of the walls is the follow-
ing inscription: “In this room the Boston
tea party made their plans and dressed as
Mohawk Indians, and went to Griffin
(now Liverpool) wharf, where the ships
Beavor and Eleanor and Dartmouth lay,
and threw overboard 342 chests of tea, Dec.
16, 1773." On other parts of the wall are
quaint old paintings representing the differ-
ent scenes for which the house is famous.
The most remarkable of these decorations is
the old sign bearing a portrait of John Han-
cock, which for many years swung over the
courtyard. The tavern was called for him
in 1780, when he was made Governor.
This sign was blown down a number of
years ago, and is said to have killed a man
passing through the yard. The picture is
somewhat broken, but otherwise the figure
upon it shows quite plainly.
	History credits the old Hancock Tavern
with having been the temporary abiding
place of all the early statesmen and men of
note in the colony. The original house on
the spot was erected as early as 1631. Be-
sides the older worthies the present house
has sheltered Lafayette, Louis Philippe,
Talleyrand and many others.
	In the eventful years preceding the revo-
lution the tavern was a frequent meeting
place of the patriots. Not only was the
throwing over of the tea planned there,
but many other plots were made to discom-
fort the British and help the cause of the
colonies. At one time not so very long ago

[Column 2]
before historians and antiquarians had be-
gun to notice the place and to remember its
ancient prestige, it was familiarly known as
“Bite Tavern,” it is supposed because a
small lunch at a very reasonable sum could
be obtained there.

	On the afternoon of the tea party among
the many guests were quite a number
whose ancestors participated in the original
tea party. Among those was a Miss Treat,
whose grandmother, Helen de St. Pry, was
a god-daughter of Lafayette. She married
Lieut. Treat who was stationed at Castle
island from 1776 to 1798. Another guest
was young Edmund Sears who celebrated
his birthday by attending the reception, for
his ancestors had 125 years before helped to
throw the tea overboard. Other guests
whose ancestors participated in the historic
affair were Mrs. Walter Tower of Melrose,
a descendant of John Spurr; H. G. Hewes
of Braintree, descendant of George Robert
Twelves Hewes; Mrs. J. D. Ronimus, des-
cendant of James Starr, all of the “party”
and Mrs S. Agnes Sprague, whose ances-
tor was of the same notable company.
	Many of these told pleasing anecdotes of
their ancestors, which had been handed
down in the families, and of which they are
deservedly proud. The Fadette orchestra
played.
	There was no attempt at any formal
speechmaking. In fact it would have been
almost impossible to do so on account of the
crowded state of the rooms. Miss Hunt
did say to a little circle surrounding her 
that she believed in the patriotism of work-
ing for one’s country, especially along the
line, of good citizenship, and that next to
the work of the home and the church should
come that of the patriotic societies, which
strive to keep alive the memories of the his-
toric past to inspire the youth to noble ef-
forts and the highest type of life in the
future.
	There was no attempt at decorating the
ancient rooms but in one of them hung an
old lantern which hung for many
years outside the door of the old tavern
	—Boston Herald.


STATE HISTORICAL  SOCIETY.

	The State Historical Society held its
second annual meeting in the lecture hall
of the Templeton building, Jan. 16, Hon.
Franklin D. Richards, president of the soci-
ety presiding. The hall was well filled
and the program was instructive.
	Commencing Mrs. Browning rendered
“Sweet Bird of Spring,” accompanied on
the piano by Spencer Clawson Jr. Prayer
was offered by Hon. O. F. Whitney.
	Governor Wells was not able to be pres-
ent, but in the paper he contributed and
which was read by Hon. O. F. Whitney,
he expressed his sympathy with the work
of the society and pledged his support in
every possible way.
	President Richards delivered his annual
address, in which he reviewed the work of
kindred societies, offered suggestions to
members, and called attention to the great


field of usefulness before the Utah society.
	Dr. J. E. Talmage’s paper, The Material
of History, considered the subject from a
scientific standpoint, and took occasion to
suggest action on the part of the State
against vandalism, which had led to the de-
struction of many important relics of by-
gone ages.
	An address by Mme. Mila Tupper May-
nard upon the Culture Value of History
was able and interesting.
	Don C. Maguire dealt with the Pre-historic
Races of the Southwest, referring to the evi-
dence of such that had come under his per-
sonal observation throughout an area of
country extending from the Gulf of Cali-
fornia on the southwest, to the Snake River
on the north. In it he referred to explor-
ing work done by himself and Professor
Montgomery formerly of the State univer-
sity, among the mounds and caverns at
Paragoonah in Iron county, where they un-
earthed some twenty-five human skeletons,
of persons who undoubtedly had met death
by violence ages ago. Mr. Maguire said the
State was full of such evidence and he re-
garded the Historical Society as having a
great work before it.
	Benediction was pronounced by C. R.
Savage, after which the society elected
officers for the ensuing year, F. D. Richards
being re-elected president; Isabella Cameron
Brown, vice president; J. R. Letcher, re-
cording secretary; J. T. Hammond, cor-
responding secretary, L. S. Hills, treasurer;
Mrs. Antoinette B. Kinney, librarian.


WOMAN S EXPONENT


FANNY C. LOVELAND DEAD.

	Many of the friends and acquaintances of
Mrs. Fanny C. Loveland will be sorry to
hear of her death. She died Sunday morn-
ing, November 20, 1898, at Bountiful of
old age and general debility. She had been
confined to her bed about three months and
ailing all last summer. She was Carlos
Loveland’s mother, and sister to Lucina
Sessions, Samantha Willey, Rosoline Dustin,
and stepmother to Emarett Sessions.
	Deceased was born in the state of Ver-
mont on May 11, 1816, thus making her
eighty-two years of age. She was but a
mere girl when her father’s family joined
the Church and moved to Geauga county,
Ohio, to join the Saints there in 1836. At
the age of twenty-one in the year 1837 she
was married to Chester Loveland, who pre-
ceded her to the great beyond by about
fourteen years.
She passed through all the persecutions
of Kirtland, Ohio, Jackson county, Missouri,
and Nauvoo, and endured the hardships en-
countered crossing the plains, and arrived
in Utah with her husband in 1850, and lo-
cated in Bountiful. She buried a son four
years old while on her way across the plains.
	She went with her husband and others
who had been called to settle Carson Valley
where they remained from 1856 to 1857.
After her return she resided most of the
time in Bountiful with the exception of a
few years that she resided at Brigham City
and Calls Fort respectively.
	When the Relief Society was organized
at Brigham City August 28, 1866, she was
chosen second counselor to Sister Lenora
Morley. After Sister Morley’s death she
was appointed first counselor to Harriet
Snow, which position she held until the or-
ganization of Box Elder Stake.
	She was the mother of eight children,


four of whom are still living, three boys
and one girl.
	Funeral services were held at the Sessions
House, Tuesday afternoon at 4 o’clock.
The speakers were Elders C. R. Jones
Madsen, of the Salt Lake Temple, Joseph
L Holbrook, W. W. Willey, D. O. Willey,
Thomas Wadoups, Israel Call, Judson Tol-
man and John A. Waite. The remains were
taken to Calls Fort Wednesday morning,
where funeral services were held for the
benefit of friends and relatives who could
not be present at those held in Bountiful,
and the remains were laid to rest at that
place. Quite a number of relatives and
friends accompanied the remains to Calls Fort
Deceased has been a subscriber for the
“Exponent” from its first publication un-
til her death. She was an earnest worker
for the cause of truth, possessed an excel-
lent character and was especially noted for
being a “peace maker.” Her prepossess-
ing face wore a sweet smile which scattered
sunshine and happiness to all who mingled
with her.
			SAMANTHA SESSIONS.


IN MEMORIAM. 

SARAH FAULKNER.

	On Monday, Dec. 5, 1898, Sister Sarah
Faulkner passed over to the great beyond.
	Sister Faulkner was born in Chadwick,
Worcestershire, England, Feb. 6, 1830,
and was sixty-eight years old. She came
to Utah in 1853 and has labored very faith-
fully ever since, not only being an active
member in all her Church duties, but she
desired to aid in making our city attractive
as far as lay in her power, by beautifying
her surroundings.
The deceased was a teacher in the Relief
Society for many years and she did her
work faithfully in sun or shine. She was al-
ways charitable and kind to the needy. Mrs.
Faulkner was a very estimable lady and
leaves numerous friends to mourn her death
besides one son, her other five children
having gone before her. The cause of her
death was a malignant cancer, from which
she has been a sufferer the past two years. 
	ELLEN KELSEY, Sec.
Springville, Utah.


[Column 1]
LADIES SEMI-MONTHLY MEETING.

	The Ladies’ Semi-monthly meeting was
held in the 14th ward hall Saturday, Dec.
17, 1898, Counselor E. J. Stevenson presid-
ing in the absence of President Horne.
Singing, “For the strength of the hills we
bless thee.” Prayer by Counselor Bath-
sheba W. Smith. Singing, “Welcome
best of all good meetings.” Minutes read
and approved.
	A sister, name unknown, bore a faithful
testimony. Counselor E. Webb spoke of
the spirit of meekness and love and said we
should dedicate ourselves to the Lord in
the morning and evening, be careful and do
our duties.
	Sister Lydia D. Alder said this was the
first of the year when our hearts were
drawn out to one another, and our thoughts
are upon Him whose birth we celebrate.
She did not think we could realize the love
He bears for His children. He was an ex-
ample of humility and obedience. “Let us
love one another and help one another.”
Another sister spoke on honest tithing and
donations.

[Column 2]
	Sister Martha A. Morris who had just re-
turned from Europe spoke a few moments.
She said she had been to get genealogy,
but she had testified of the Gospel where-
ever opportunity offered. She had borne
her testimony to her relatives forty years
ago and she bore it again to them and told
them she would leave them without excuse.
	Sister Jane James rejoiced in living in
these days. Sister McMaster thanked God
that she had been permitted to come forth
in this generation. Thought it was our
business to secure God for ourselves and
not criticise others.
	Sister Jane A. Albrand spoke of the way
God cares for His children,. He preserves
them on land and sea. Sister Jane Powell
said she had walked many miles to preach
to her relatives but not one of them had
been so favored of the Lord as to accept
the Gospel but herself. Said she and her
daughter had been privileged to go into the
Temple and do the work for over one thou-
sand. 
	Sister Kiddle bore testimony to the power
of prayer and spoke of the great desire she
had to come to Zion, and how thankful she
was to her Heavenly Father she had been
permitted to do so.
	Sister B. W. Smith spoke of the great
wrong we did in criticising those placed
over us. Said her idea of a Latter-day
Saint was to be kind and not get angry, pay
your tithing, donations and Relief Society
donations, go to your meetings promptly,
be careful of your criticisms, watch and
pray and do good to all. Wished all a
Merry Christmas and a Happy New Year.
	Counselor Stevenson said since we had
last met Sister Sarah M. Kimball had passed
away, she was one of the board of these
Saturday meetings. She wished all a Mer-
ry Christmas and a Happy New Year and
invited all to bring friends enough with
them to fill the house next meeting. An
invitation was sent to all the Relief Society
presidents and counselors to attend the
closing exercises of Dr. Robert’s nursing
class.
	Sister Morris and congregation sang “O,
my Father.” Meeting adjourned for two
weeks. Benediction, Mrs. M. W. Wilcox.
			J. S. WOOLLEY, Sec.


U. W. P. C.

The U. W. P. C. met in the pleasant
rooms of Dr. Ellis R. Shipp, Friday eve-
ning, Dec. 30, 1898. After opening prayer
by Lydia D. Alder the secretary called the
roll, responses being given by quo-
tations on Christmas from favorite authors.
It was moved and seconded that all busi-
ness be dispensed with for the evening, ex-
cept the proposal of the name of Miss Nina
Winslow Eckart as a member of the club by
Mrs. E. B. Wells. Miss Eckart was unan-
imously elected a member.
	The following interesting and carefully
prepared program was rendered: Poem on
Christmas, Mrs. M. A. Freeze. Christmas
story, Mrs. E. B. Wells, also one by Mrs.
Ruth M. Fox. Christmas reminiscences
by Mrs. Maria Francis. Poem, Mrs. L. D.
Alder. Several beautiful musical selections
were rendered, after which the hostess and
her charming daughters served delicious re-
freshments. A short time was spent in
social converse after which the club ad-
journed to meet in the new year of 1899,
Jan. 31, at 7:30 in room 208, Templeton.
	AIMEE C. SHILLER, Rec. Sec.


WOMAN'S EXPONENT.


SARAH E. CARMICHAEL.

	As lofty mountains whose crests ever
glisten with eternal snow hear strange and
weird music not sung by the sleepers in the
valley over whom they tower in grandeur
and majesty, so with the poet who hears
music in the floating wind whisperings in
the forest; to whom the sea tells its wonder-
ful secrets, seeing glory in the sunset, feel-
ing peace in the moonlight and intuitively
understanding the melody of birds.
	Like the chords of a finely strung instru-
ment is his soul. A rough wind jars its
music; an unskilled hand produces discord
which the careless never heed, yet to the
mind so delicately attuned a rude shock is
ever the result. The poet is also a dream-
er. The fleecy clouds piled high up in the
heavens are castles to him, wherein dwell
the children of his fancy. Sometimes they
sail away in waters deep and blue; some-
times climbing the shining hills which but
reflect the glories of the City Golden; some-
times as swans sailing on and on over the
rippling waves of a great sea, till low down
the horizon they are lost to view where all
things seem to end.
	Such a beautiful mind was Sarah E.
Carmichael’s. Born in Long Island, but
early coming to Utah when only a few scat-
tered houses marked the site of its present
principal city. In the lowly walks of life,
education but limited, to her inspired soul
the Bible was an open book, its lore her de-
light, even as the sky so richly painted
with varying sunsets, reflecting over the
Great Salt Lake like the glory of burning
worlds hanging high over the mountains,
the changing panorama fading away, the
gorgeous amber and gold, to paler, softer
tints as the day-god sinks to rest where the
line of water and sky meets. A throb of dis-
appointment falls coldly on the heart that
sight can go no farther. A child of Nature
she read from her wonderful book, under-
standing her secret laws intuitively, know-
ingeven more than she taught her.
	She needed not to go to Switzerland to
see the majesty of God displayed in her
wondrous snow-clad mountains, her wild
scenery, her ravishing sunsets. No! as a
child she accompanied her parents in a pro-
saic way across the wild plains and rugged
mountains, that here her poetical soul might
see and feel the glories so famed in that far
of land. Like an imprisoned bird regaining
its freedom are her bursts of song; deep,
well nigh unutterable her sympathy; and
the spirit of prophecy falls heavy on her
heart, as the clouds of the future, drawn
slightly aside, the stern hand of Fate re-
veals a dark river on whose banks she cries
for a prayer, that her boat may be safely
guided over its broad bosom.
	As her years increased so was she shy
and deeply sensitive. Out of tune with
her surroundings, not harmonious with her
family, to whom she belonged only by
birth, this child of thought sighed for the
companionship that she had never known.
	At the age of eighteen she began sending
some of her verses to the Deseret News for
publication. At first the genuineness was
doubted; people wondered that so gifted a
writer lived among them, yet to them un-
known. Friends began to gather around
her, and she was sometimes spoken of as
the “Lily of the Valley.” Among others
her songs touched the heart of Dr. William-
son who was stationed here as an army sur-
geon, and from a feeling of interest

[Column 2]
bloomed the beautiful flower of love. They
plighted their vows at the altar in 1867,
against much kindly advice from the friends
of both.
	In 1866 while they were dreaming their
love dreams, some of the doctor’s friends
and admirers of the poetess collected and
published a few of her choicest poems, a
copy of which has been read by the writer.
	It was fondly hoped by her friends that
under these new and happy conditions her
songs would soar higher, grander, purer
and be as much more beautiful as heaven is
than earth.
	But alas! for dreams of fame or love!
This brilliant and gifted mind went out
even as a lamp to utter darkness. The
surging, dark river had engulphed it! And
oh, sad is the spectacle of a loved one living
yet dead.
	The sorrowing husband of a year accept-
ed with resignation the blank life stretching
out before him; his brightest hope could find
no fruition this side of the grave. And his
life became like a beautiful poem telling of
devotion, patience, long watching and soli-
tary prayer. All earthly power was brought
to aid in her restoration, but to no avail.
By his side he decided she should remain,
under his own care. So for twenty years,
and even till he was called to cross the
bright river of which she wrote, he was
true to his charge. Some of their dreams
were realized. A wedding trip to Ohio,
then a short visit to California, whose beau-
ties enchanted her and she wrote,

Cheer for the queen of the Western wave!
			California!
Where the sunbeams walk on a golden pave,
And the moonlight hides in a silver cave,
			California!
Where the day that comes to this land of ours.
Finds the brightest gems and the rarest flowers,
Where the lingering day when it must depart,
Leaves the last red pulse of a broken heart,
			California!
	This visit over then to their home his
thoughtful love had provided for her where
she still lives, never knowing that the great
love of a lifetime still burning on its altar
has gone forever from earth, leaving her a
widow who knows not of her great loss.
	His was a grand and noble nature. Lov-
ing little children dearly he made them his
friends, always speaking so kindly to them.
Thus I remember him in the Long Ago.
	One of her friends, Mary V. Young, who
also was my friend and has herself long
since crossed over the river, told me the sad
story, pointing out to me the house where
Miss Carmichael passed her girlhood days.
Often we spoke of her, musing over the
wreck of such a promising life. After her
husband’s death the last poem written by
his gifted wife was found pinned over the
heart whose weary beatings were soothed at
last.
	To know no loss, no pain, no hope, such
is her life. Yea, the ways of God are in-
scrutable and past finding out; but the light
of eternity will reveal the secrets, the de-
signs and the conditions of this life.
	I would that here she might sing a new
song; and I fancy that over on the other
shore she will change the words of the old
poem for new ones and say,

Down by the river,
The bright flowing river,
	That flows to eternity’s sea;
I kneel to the Giver,
To God the Good Giver
	Whose love is ever o’er me.

[Column 3]
Not cold is it, nor dark is it,
But glad as my heart it is—
	Swift flowing river sublime—
Many ships come over it,
Gladly sail over it— 
	This strange, sad river of time.

Now down by the riverside
The golden sand riverside
	Thank God for my life and me!
You have prayed for us, prayed for us
And solemnly said for us
	“God guides them safe over the sea.”

Calm is the rivertide
The bright, golden rivertide
	That shall give my lover to me.

O’er the wide river
The cold rolling river
	That ever flows on to the sea,
I shall greet my fond lover
My true fond lover,
	Who ever is waiting for me.

And over the river
The smooth flowing river
	Where my lover is waiting for me, 
We’ll praise the Good Giver,
Our God the Good Giver
	Through ages unknown of eternity.

	It is to be regretted that her book has
gone out of print. The Stolen Sunbeam,
The Wounded Bird, Faith, President
Lincoln’s Funeral, with California are
among her gems.
			LYDIA D. ALDER.
	December, 1898.


TRIENNIAL OF NATIONAL COUNCIL

	The National Council of Women of the
United States will hold its triennial in
Washington, D. C., at the Universalist
church, corner of G. and Tenth streets,
meetings commencing February 12 to 18,
1899. There will be ten public sessions
and two very important business meetings,
besides Sunday services.
	At this triennial of the National Council
new officers will be elected for the next
term of three years, and the subject of the
International Council in London, England.
which is to be held the last week in June
of this year, will be an important feature of
the work to be considered.
	The National Council of Women of the
United States is composed of the great organ-
izations of women in the United States and
naturally such organized membership makes
the council a powerful agent in any work it
undertakes for the good of the country.
	Ladies intending to visit Washington
during the winter should go down in time
to attend the meetings of the triennial of
the council.


Gov. Roosevelt has ridden rough-shod
over the susceptibilities of the New York
“Antis.” This is the first time the Governor
of New York has recommended woman suf-
frage in his message, but in Massachusetts
it has been a frequent occurrence. Secre-
tary of the Navy, Long, was Governor of
Massachusetts for several terms and always 
recommended woman suffrage in his mes-
sage to the Legislature. Among other Mass-
achusetts Governors who have done so
were Govs. Claflin, Washburn, Talbot,
Butler, Ames and Greenhalge. These
gubernatorial recommendations do not have
much influence on the action of the Legis-
lature but they call attention to the subject
and stir up much wholesome discussion.

SALT LAKE CITY JANUARY 15, 1899.


[Column 1]
WOMEN IN THE LEGISLATURE.

	The Legislature of Utah convened in the
City and County Building on Monday,
January 9. Its members are nearly all
Democrats, in fact all who have been elec-
ted from this county. It is a matter of un-
favorable comment here and elsewhere that
only one woman should have been elected
this term. Dr. Martha Hughes Cannon in
the Senate was elected two years ago and
was fortunately “a hold over.”
	It is strange and regrettable that in a
state where there are nearly or quite as
many women voters as men, that so few
were nominated. It proves how strong the
old traditions and prejudices are against wo-
men in public life.
	At any rate women do not congregate up-
on the corners of the streets and other con-
spicuous places to talk politics and bet on
elections or nominations. The two women
who are at present members, are both very
capable, conscientious and well educated,
and know something of Utah and its needs.
They are equal in intelligence to the men;
perhaps some members who have been in
several successive Legislatures may be a
little better versed in technicalities, but
in the opinion of the writer these ladies
know enough of Parliamentary usage to
preside over the House or the Senate and
would give more grace and as much dig-
nity to the office. Meanwhile we shall see
what comes to pass. The selection of a
United States Senator will be a problem
not easily solved where there are so many
candidates; and it maybe a little difficult to
choose from the number, five already and
“a dark horse” in the background, but
women as a rule have quick perception
and are gifted with intuition and will most
likely make as few mistakes as men (Heaven
preserve them from making more.) At
any rate the trial is being made in the suf-
frage states, though hardly a fair beginning
with so small a number. The women who
have the ballot should not hesitate to begin
at the beginning and attend the primary of
their own ward or district in sufficient num-
bers to make themselves a power and do
some of the nominating, and until they
have courage to do this the cause will not
have very great impetus.
	The “eternal womanly” so much praised
deters women from making very rapid
strides towards a reformation in politics.
As if it were not womanly and dignified in
any case to try and better the conditions of

[Column 2]
the city, county or state where one resides!
Faith without works when one is surround-
ed with dangers that threaten destruction,
is not likely to be very efficient.
	If a turbulent stream of muddy water
were leaping and raging in one’s onward
way one would not hesitate to strike out
boldly to prevent its further progress, in
whatever manner seemed possible. The
women of today who have agitated the 
rights of the ballot see dark clouds in
the horizon of the future, in fact the turbu-
lent stream of immorality is making tremen-
dous strides, shut one’s eyes to the fact as
one may. And all this work cannot be ac-
complished at the fireside, where the found-
ations are laid. Mothers teach virtue, hon-
esty, integrity and uprightness in the home;
the boy or youth goes out into society and
into the wide, wide world and what hap-
pens? Many broken-hearted mothers
know. It is not perhaps possible to have
an ideal civilization with all the adverse in-
fluences of the age to combat with, but it is
possible that better laws and their enforce-
ment might be beneficial, and certainly the
experiment is worth trying. Women have
ideas of matters in business and domestic
life that are helpful and acceptable to men,
why may they not have practical ideas of
government and law-making? And they
are not asking for the kingdom nor for the
whole commonwealth, only to share in the
struggles for right and partake of its
benefits.
	Women are told by “the lords of crea-
tion” to be domestic and submissive like
their grandmothers in the past, but they
need to be reminded there were women
in the past whose names are handed down
in history as illustrations, who rebelled
against the injustice of the laws and condi-
tions as their descendants do in the present
day.
	Read the early history of New England
and you find many expressions of determined
opposition to the existing laws. The moth-
ers of the country surely have some rights
that should be recognized more than they
have been in the past. Abagail Adams of
revolutionary fame, in writing to her hus-
band upon this subject “even threatened to
foment rebellion unless the rights of her
sex were secured, and declared women
wouid not hold themselves bound to obey
any laws in which they had no voice or rep-
resentation.”
	It is true women have influence in many
directions, and yet the ballot is more potent
still, for it means power. It is not expect-
ed that woman’s ballot alone can revolution-
ze the country, but the exercise of suffrage
in the hands of the best men and best wo-
men must of necessity produce a manifest
difference and a decided change a few gen-
erations hence, or perchance sooner; at any
rate it is pleasant to hope so. But it will
take moral courage to stand for the highest
ideals in government, though one cannot
but believe it helps even to work for better
conditions, though defeat and not success
may often be the result, eventually the
right will triumph if persistent warfare
against wrong doing is waged and main-
tained.
	The women of these intermountain states
are privileged as no other women are, and
appreciating this they should also accept
the responsibilities it implies, and carry the
burden if such it be, hopefully and march
on to victory—the triumph of right over
wrong, light over darkness better, condi-

[Column 3]
tions for the masses and a more enlightened
humanity.


TENDENCIES OF THE AGE.

	There never was a time in any age of the
world when there was such a tendency to
higher education and in every direction to-
wards progress and development along the
lines of activity, practical, mental, scienti-
fic and inventive in many, and indeed in al-
most all parts of the world, as at the present.
One must be very wide awake to keep in-
formed even on the lines that lie most in
one’s own practical way of thinking and
doing.
	There is less awakening in the spiritual
life of the people, the tendency being more
material than otherwise; the money ques-
tion has seemingly swallowed up much of
the religious nature of the world at large.
Wealth more than aught else is fast be-
coming, if it has not already, the most
potent influence in even religious circles.
Indeed money may be called the passion of
the age as well as the ruling power, and
“more’s the pity, and pity ’tis that it is so.”
	To be sure money or some exchange of
value or barter is necessary in this worka-
day world, yet when men and women have
this morbid craving for money, it seems to
absorb every wholesome element in one’s
nature and change the entire character.
	If a restraining influence could be
brought to bear upon character thro’ the
higher thought and education of which we
hear so much, that would tend to modifi-
cation in this direction, what a different
world this would be. Out of all the soci-
eties and organizations for information and
for equalizing conditions there seems no
combined force strong enough as yet to
overthrow the money power. Are the peo-
ple about to succumb to a materialism that
will tend to degrade and despoil the inheri-
tance of virtue and true nobility that has
been the proud boast of American civiliza-
tion?
	To guard against the spirit of extrava-
gance and luxurious living, even though
one has the wherewith to obtain it, is in-
deed commendable, especially if the means
is used to build up industries that will give
employment to the workers who are com-
pelled to live by wages, or salaries as the
case may be.
	Brigham Young’s ideas in this respect-
were almost ideal. To live simply and to
devote means to fostering industries that
would build up the community and lay a
foundation for generations yet to come or the
emigrants from other countries, that all
might have something to do.
	It is appalling to contemplate the future
of young people reared in indolence and
luxury, accustomed to the gratification of
every want, and without thought of any of
the sober realities of life; living for pleas-
ure and vanity. That ever work should be
considered degrading is a mistake. That
all are not adapted to the same sort of work
is true, and parents should not (in our
opinion) compel children to follow a pur-
suit that is particularly distasteful. But if
there could be something of a return to
agricultural pursuits in this Western land
it would be a blessing. Every farmer
might be a gentleman; subduing the earth 
is a commandment and there is yet much to
be done in this direction. What are our
boys to do when they are grown? is a
question often asked and seldom satis-


[Column 1]
factorily answered. Those who strike out
into new territory and take up new land
making homes by dint of hard labor and
grow up with the community become in
many instances not only founders of cities
and towns, but often if capable and reliable
are chosen to fill important positions of
trust and emolument.
	Speaking of farmers as gentlemen, a cir-
cumstance came under the notice of the
writer which illustrates the fact. Visiting
in New England at a country farm house a
census taker called one morning to make
inquiries for his report. Among other
questions was the one curtly put, “What is
your occupation?” “A gentleman farmer,”
was the reply. “A gentleman farmer!”
said the interlocuter as he surveyed the
man from head to foot, “can’t I give you
some other title?” “No sir,” was the
only answer. Evidently being satisfied he
would get no further information he left
the house.
	Afterwards Mr. H. explained that every
clerk or wage-earner was of the opinion
that men who tilled the ground or were
engaged in agricultural pursuits could not
be classed as gentlemen; and it gave me
new thoughts upon the subject. Mr. H.
had been a Prof. of mathematics, a civil
engineer, had even been a member of the
Legislature of his state, but preferred in
his later years to retire to his country farm
and discard all other pursuits. Neverthe-
less he still intended to be considered a
gentleman by such young men as the one
referred to, and a gentleman he was indeed
though he did more actual work on his own
farm than half a dozen hired men, for in
these days some of the laboring men have
all flocked to the cities and large towns. A
striking contrast to the New England of
half a century ago.
	It is a well known fact that the greatest
men America has produced have been
raised upon a farm and accustomed to the
hard work of the farmer’s son. To be a
land owner was the pride and ambition of
the early settlers of our country.
	Homes of their own for the common peo-
ple was the sentiment Brigham Young
tried to inculcate and practically to demon-
strate in these mountain vales during the
period of the early settlement here. Who
does not know that the home is the centre
from which springs the best in life and in
government. Not from hotels and board-
ing houses, nor from transient occupancy
of "houses for rent”—whether they are
spacious mansions or neat cottages or
miserable hovels. The home which is our
own is the ideal one, the children enshrine
it in their memory, “the old home,” and
“childhood’s home,” are subjects for the
poets. If every young man in the com-
munity would strive for a home of his own,
how much it would do towards a better and
more permanent standing in the welfare of
the state.
	The writer can never forget with what
laudable pride she spoke upon the subject
during her first visit to Washington twenty
years ago. And how the ladies of the Con-
vention listened to women owning their
own homes and paying their own taxes.
The president of the United States himself
listened carefully to the statement, and his
gentle and amiable wife, how interested
she felt in this simple fact. But, alas, how
different, now!
	Simplicity of living is a virtue. Not
miserliness, but true economy which so
few people in this age seem to practice.

[Column 2]
Plain living is healthful and happifying.
There is, there must be room somewhere
for everybody to live honestly and respec-
tably; better far to launch out into new
fields and begin at the foundation than to
fight ones way in the great cities along the
crowded thoroughfares, where there are
hundreds of applicant with whom to com-
pete.
	The tendency to “single blessedness,” so
miscalled, instead of family life, is on the
increase and has reached even to these val-
leys. It is injurious to manhood and wo-
manhood and contrary to the laws of nature
and of God. “Multiply and replenish the
earth” is the first commandment given to
man in the Holy Scriptures, and has never
been countermanded. The inclination to
postpone marriage until late in life or alto-
gether is a growing evil, and should be dep-
recated certainly among Latter-day Saints.
That girls are extravagant and require
liberal settlements beforehand is scarcely a
good excuse. What girls and women long
for most and look forward to is love. And
honorable marriage with true love is what
the girls have a right to expect, and that
makes up for lack of this world’s goods.
“Love in a cottage” is where contentment
abides much more securely and far more
generally than in a palace, the old-fash-
ioned poet “Cotton” writes,

Though fools spurn Hymen’s gentle powers,
We, who improved is golden hours,
	By sweet experience know,
That marriage, rightly understood,
Gives to the tender and the good,
	A paradise below.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


[Column 1]
EDITORIAL NOTES.

	We have been shown today the drawing
for a very artistic map of the the pioneer
route of the Mormon people journeying
from the city of Nauvoo to the valley of
the Great Basin. The work is to be litho-
graphed and placed upon the market. It
will be suitable for every home and for
public buildings. A pocket edition is also
to be prepared and issued.
	The agents of the “Exponent” in all the
several wards and Stakes of Zion, and also
individual subscribers indebted for the
paper are urgently requested to settle all
old accounts at the earliest date possible.
This becomes an absolute necessity on ac-
count of the change that takes place in the
management of the Deseret Evening News.
The “Exponent” will not be sent to any
subscriber after January 1899, unless pre-
paid. All delinquents will as soon as pos-
sible receive written notices of bills due, in
addition to the printed matter. 
	One of the notable features of the past
year, 1898, and one in which women are
greatly interested is the document of the
Czar of Russia toward a proposed treaty
for universal peace, and coming as it did
from the most autocratic of rulers it does
seem indicative of a world-wide movement
for the arbitration of all serious difficulties
among nations, and a general tendency to-
wards the amicable settlement of hostilities,
and finally the result may be a ceasation of
warfare altogether, for which good time
coming all who desire the true welfare of
humanity should be grateful.
	The clubs of Salt Lake City have com-
bined their efforts and have succeeded in
getting started a “Utah Free Traveling

[Column 2]
Library.” It is one of the best philan-
thropic, educational benefits that could
come to those living in isolated communi-
ties where good books are not obtainable.
All the clubs in the Federation are expected
to contribute their quota of money and of
books. The work is in the hands of a
committee of ladies, of which Mrs. Helen
D. Moore is chairman.
	Woman’s Work, is a Southern monthly
magazine published in Montgomery, Ala-
bama, business manager, Evelyn Fitz-
patrick. The journal aims to disseminate
the best ideas of club women. It is being
ably conducted and promises, if one can
judge from the few numbers that have been
issued, to fill a void in the South, where
few women have as yet entered into the
spirit of club work that is permeating the
whole country. We are grateful to Wo-
man’s Work for mentioning the “Exponent”
and in connection with it the silk industry
in Utah. May Woman’s Work become a
power for good in the land, and the women
of all sections join hands and co-operate for
the good of all, casting aside the geographi-
cal lines of distinction, until by custom they
shall become obliterated in mind.
	Mrs. Hannah J. Bailey of Winthrop
Centre, Maine, is en route to California and
will pay Salt Lake a visit on her return trip
in March. Mrs. Bailey is the treasurer of
the National Council of Women of the
United States, and (of course) one of the
General Board of that great society to
which the National W. R. S. and the Y.L.
N. M. I. A. are connected. Mrs. Zina D. H.
Young and Mrs. Elmina S. Taylor both
being vice presidents of the council by vir-
tue of their positions as presidents of these
National associations. Mrs Bailey is a wo-
man of great executive ability, and will
find a loving welcome in our city from the 
ladies who have such pleasant remembran-
ces of her genial manner and kindly face.
Mrs. Bailey manages a large manufactur-
ing business in Maine of which her husband
was proprietor previous to his decease a
few years ago; she is also superintendent
of the World’s W. C. T. U. and associated
in other organizations of public interest.
We trust her brief stay among us will be
a pleasant leaf in her wayside notes of her
journey from the coast.

	The Stake secretaries of the Relief Soci-
ety in all the several Stakes of Zion and of

where Stakes are not organized, are hereby

building, Salt Lake City, Utah.
	E. B. WELLS, General Secretary.


STATE SOCIETY D. AND R.

	A State Society of Utah of the Daughters
of the Revolution was organized on Wed-
nesday, November 16, 1898, in the office of 
the “Woman’s Exponent,” room 208,
Templeton building, in this city. The 
names of State officers elected were pub-
lished in our columes in the issue of Dec-
ember 1, 1898.
	On Friday December 16, the famous Bos-
ton Tea Party day, a meeting was held at
the residence of Mrs. Maria V. Dougall,


where a suitable and interesting program
for the occasion was successfully carried
out. Light refreshments were daintily
served to the D. R. and guests.
	Friday, January 6, there was a meeting
at the residence of Hon. Alice S. M. Horne,
member of the House of Representatives of
the State Legislature of Utah now in ses-
sion, but owing to adverse circumstances
the adoption of the Constitution was post-
poned, also the reading of the inaugural
address of the State Regent, Mrs. E. B. Wells.
	Monday, January 16, the D. R. Society
met in the Templeton and the regent gave
her address and the Constitution was unani-
mously adopted. A State Council was
appointed, and committees on Press and
Printing and Decoration.
	The members of the Council are Mrs.

to incorporate shortly under the laws of the
State.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


GOLDEN WEDDING ANNIVERSARY.

 “Marriage is the life-long miracle.
The self begetting wonder daily fresh.”

	On Friday evening, Dec. 30, 1898, a
grand programme and picnic party was
given in the ward hall to do honor to
Brother Richard R. and Jane C. Birkbeck
on the fiftieth anniversary of their wedding
day. It was an impromptu affair, but,
however, a great number of guests assem-
bled to enjoy themselves with the aged and
highly respected couple. Numerous selec-
tions were rendered, both musical and
otherwise, and after all had feasted sumptu-
ously a great deal was gathered up and dis-
tributed to the poor the next day. All
who took part proclaimed it one of the best
parties of the kind ever given in Cedar City.
Grandma Birkbeck, as she is known by all
the young people, although in her seventy-
seventh year was on nearly every floor, and
seemed to dance as sprightly as many a
woman scores of years her junior. During
the festivities a purse was made up and
something over twenty dollars was presen-
ted to the honored couple with the compli-
ments and best wishes of all present.
	Though never having enjoyed the great
and glorious blessing of children, yet they
are loved and respected by all who know
them and have caused many a ray of sun-
shine to penetrate where all seemed dark-
ness, and made hearts beat with renewed
hope and gladness where sadness reigned
supreme. Many are the homes that have
been made happy by their visits to the sick
room.
	Sister Birkbeck has filled various positions


in the Relief Society, and at present holds
the office of Stake treasurer.
Respectfully, 
	VIOLET URIE,
		R. S. Sec., Cedar City.

“O, the music and beauty oft lose their worth,
When one heart only joys in their smile;
But the union of hearts gives that pleasure its
	birth,
Which beams on the darkest and coldest of
	earth
Like the sun on her own chosen isle;
It gives to the fireside of winter, the light,
The glow and the glitter of spring—
O, sweet are the hours when the fond hearts
	unite,
As softly the years in their innocent flight
Glide away on a motionless wing.”

	The “Exponent” adds hearty congratu-
lations, and ardent wishes for the eternal
future of such a union. May all that truest
love could ask for this venerable couple be
theirs, of health, happiness, prosperity and
an abundance of love in this world, and
these with the hope in their souls of a
glorious resurrection of lives throughout
the endless ages of eternity is the desire of
the writer.—E. B. W.


[Column 1]
R. S. REPORT.

ST. JOSEPH STAKE.
	Believing our sisters would like to know
what the Relief Society sisters are doing in
far off Arizona, I will give you a short ac-
count.
	Our conference convened on Dec. 3, 1898,
President Elizabeth Layton presiding.
There were present of the priesthood
Apostle Abraham O. Woodruff, President
Andrew Kimball, Counselors William D.
Johnson and Charles M. Layton.
	The speakers touched on many impor-
tant subjects, viz. storing of grain, building
a Stake granary, as we have on hand 995
bushels of grain; on tithing, fast donations,
supporting the Church school, necessity of
attending to prayers, looking after the poor
and needy, comforting the widows and or-
phans.
	It was decided that on the evening of
Dec. 6, we have a Stake reunion in
the form of a dance and repast. There
were over three hundred present and all
partook of a sumptuous supper. Apostle
Abraham O. Woodruff and Elder Seymour
B. Young were present to add to the dig-
nity of the occasion. The dancing began
at seven p. m., and closed at twelve p. m.
The proceeds of the party which were
$65.00 were used to defray the expenses of
two sisters who were in Salt Lake City
taking a course in obstetrics under Dr. Ellis
R. Shipp.
	The health of the people in general is
good, though this has been the coldest
winter witnessed in the last fifteen years.
The weather at present is delightful.
		SELENA PHILLIPS, Stake Sec.
	Thatcher, Ariz., Jan. 7, 1899.
MURRAY BRANCH, S. C. WARD.
	The annual meeting of the Murray branch
of South Cottonwood ward Relief Society
was held in the Scandinavian hall, Oct. 28,
1898, President Margaret Carruth presiding.
	Meeting opened by singing “Now let us
rejoice,” etc. Prayer by Bishop J. S.
Rawlins. Singing “How firm a founda-
tion.” Minutes of the previous annual

[Column 2]
meeting read and approved  The annual
report was read and accepted.
	President M. I. Horne of Salt Lake
Stake spoke words of cheer and comfort to
the sisters and bore a strong testimony to
the truth of the Latter-day work; encour-
aged us also to be diligent in our duties,
look after the poor, comfort the sick, and
for the mothers to watch over their children
and teach them to be pure and virtuous.
Spoke on the signs of the times. Asked
the Lord to bless the sisters.
	Counselor Clara C. Cannon urged the
sisters to be faithful in attending their
meetings, said it was both a duty and a
pleasure, and to be punctual in meeting
at the time appointed, that the angels might
be waiting Spoke also upon humility and
kindness, to forgive each other. Said let
us try and bear our trials and be thankful
to the Lord that we have a place with His
people.
	President Pixton of Taylorsville was
glad that she was a member of the Church
of Jesus Christ and asked the Lord to bless
all the Relief Society. Told the sisters to
attend to their meetings and they would
get their reward.
	President Rawlins of the South Cotton-
wood ward Relief Society said she desired
to continue faithful in this great work, and
told the sisters to prepare for an eternal ex-
stence.
	Bishop Joseph S. Rawlins and Brother
Charles Holm thanked the president for in-
viting them to speak. In their remarks
they encouraged the sisters in their good
work, and stated that the Relief Society
was a great aid in assisting them in taking
care of the poor.
	President Carruth spoke of the newness
of the society, knew the sisters were good
and faithful in the society work. Thanked
the brethren and sisters for meeting with us.
	Singing “Guide us O, Thou Great Jeho-
vah.” Benediction by Brother Charles
Holm.
		MARGARET CARRUTH, Pres.
SANDWICH ISLANDS.
	Minutes of the Relief Society conference
of the Hawaiian mission held in the Laie
meeting house on Saturday morning, Oct.
8, 1898.
	Promptly at 9:30 the Hawaiian sisters,
together with the sisters from Zion, formed
in line and marched to the meeting house.
Meeting commenced at 10 a. m., President
Alice R. Woolley presiding. With her on
the stand were her assistants, Elizabeth C.
Williams, Jennie P. Musser, Ellen C. Cole,
Anna Kaulukou and Kapu Kou. Meeting
opened with singing “Joy to the world.”
Prayer by Luukia Pono. Singing on page
200 of the Hawaiian hymn book. Minutes
of the previous April conference read and
approved.
	President Alice R. Woolley spoke a few
words of welcome and encouragement, and
felt thankful to our Father in heaven for
His great blessings to us, that we were per-
mitted to meet together again under such
favorable circumstances, and hoped the
spirit of the Lord would be with us that we
may be able to receive the necessary instruc-
tions that would be for our best good at the
present time. Felt to rejoice greatly in the
work of God and urged all to be faithful
and seek diligently for the spirit of the
Lord that it may be our constant companion.
	Sister Kapo Kou spoke on the benefits of
the Relief Society and the purpose for
which it was instituted.


[Column 1]
	The Hawaiian society was then represen-
ted by one of its officers. “The Lord is my
light” was sung very nicely by the Misses
Adalade and Madaline Fernandez.
	Sister Kaulukou gave a favorable and in-
teresting report of the work done in com-
pany with Sister Garff on the island of
Maui, and spoke of the Relief Society as or-
ganized by Joseph Smith. An encourag-
ing report of Laie society was given by
Sister Nainoa.
	Sister Kekuku and daughter sang "O,
my Father,” with pleasing effect. Sister
Annie Kou reported her labors in company
with Sister Kapili on the island of Hawaii,
spoke of the great interest that was taken
in some of the branches of that island.
	Sister Jennie Mnsser encouraged all to
remain steadfast in the work of God, and to
appreciate His blessings to us here on earth.
	Alemai was represented by the president
of the branch. Conference adjourned until
one o’clock in the afternoon. Singing from
the Hawaiian hymn book. Benediction by
Sister Nakoi.
	Afternoon session: Singing by the
Hawaiian choir “Truth has risen.” Pray-
er by Sister Kamaka Eleakala. Singing
"Hail to the brightness,” etc. Favorable
reports were given from eight branches of
the society of this and the adjacent islands,
all were in a favorable condition.
	Kekuewa Nakuaau spoke on the restora-
tion of the Gospel of Jesus Christ. Sister
Minerva Fernandez gave a biographical
sketch of President Wilford Woodruff.
	Sister Cole said we had met to gain spirit-
ual strength. We all have much work to
perform. Woman’s mission here on earth
is a great one, her influence is great and
therefore all should try and exert an in-
fluence for good. Mothers should teach
their children the Gospel and set worthy
examples before them at all times. Her re-
marks were interpreted by Elder J. B.
Hooper.
	Sister Hattie Davis reported her labors in
connection with Sister Woolley on the is-
land of Kauai, the majority of the branches
of the society on that island were in a favor-
able condition.
	Sister Elizabeth C. Williams expressed
her pleasure in meeting in the Relief Soci-
ety conference, and exhorted all to search
the scriptures for light, for we cannot any
of us be saved in ignorance. Encouraged
the sisters to attend to their duties and put
themselves in a position that they can
claim the blessings that are promised unto
the faithful.
	The general officers of the Relief Society
of Zion and of the Hawaiian mission were
presented and unanimously sustained.
	President Samuel E. Woolley was pleased
with the good instructions that had been
given, and hoped we would make good use
of them in the future. Encouraged all to
continue in the good work.
	President Alice R. Woolley felt very
much gratified with the meetings today,
knew that the spirit of the Lord had been
with us for we had had a time of rejoicing
together. Spoke of her visit to the island
of Kauai, felt encouraged with the pro-
gress the sisters were making there. En-
couraged the young ladies to join the Re-
lief Society and assist in rolling on the
great work of God.
	Conference adjourned for six months.
Singing from the Hawaiian hymn book.
Benediction by Elder Lewis R. Jenkins.

	Thinking perhaps that a few words

[Column 2]
from the missionary sisters on these islands
would be of interest to the readers of the
“Exponent,” we will endeavor to give a
brief sketch of our labors.
	There are at present six sisters laboring
in the Hawaiian mission, and we are striv-
ing in our weak way to accomplish the
purpose for which we were sent. The Lord
has greatly blessed us while laboring in
these lands and we are thankful that we are
able to spend a portion of our time in His
vineyard, in striving to raise the people to a
higher plane of morality. Our labors con-
sist in taking care of the Mission Home,
working in the Relief Society, Sunday
School, Mutual Improvement Association,
Primary Association; also teaching the
children music. We are striving to ac-
quire the Hawaiian language and feel
grateful that the Lord has blessed us with a
portion of it. Peace and contentment is in
our midst and at the dawning of another
new year everything seems to be in a pros-
perous condition.
	Ever praying for the welfare of Zion and
the cause of truth we are,
	Your colaborers[-]
ELIZABETH C. WILLIAMS, Act. Sec.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


LETTER FROM SANTA ROSA.

	I have taken the “Exponent” from the
beginning of its existence, and I shall be
glad to know how much is due.
	By a letter received today I learn of the
death of Sister Staines, Brother Goddard
and Brother C. M. C[-]nnon. The latter I
did not know personally, but presume he
is a young man. The other two I can re-
joice to know that like our dear Sarah M.
Kimball they are freed from the suffering
of this mortal body.
	I seem to hear the echo of the Halleujah!
The hearty, joyous welcome! The shouts
of victory of the tried and true as they pass
one by one to the realms of eternal bliss.
	I sincerely trust that every one will put
hes shoulder to the wheel and save the
friend of woman, the dear “Exponent”
from shipwreck from the carelessness of its
officers, or the subordination of its crew.
	I have a large chest full of “Exponents”
which I hope to have bouud some day.
Of course there are many other choice selec-
tions and letters there, too, which I hope to
haul over and glean from if life holds out
and other cares do not accumulate.
	They may spoil me here, as grandma’s
are scarce and all hands are anxious to help
make my stay pleasant, and profitable to
themselves from the questions asked.
	I very much regret that there is not a
branch of our Church here, but I am
pleased to find friends whom I met long
ago who have a spirit of inquiry, and have
called to welcome me back again.
	President Woodruff must have been in-
spired when he presented Bancroft’s His-
story of Utah to the free library of Califor-
nia. It seems like an arrow of light
through the thick darkness, and has the
effect of dispelling the ignorance of many
people and their sympathies are aroused
for the injustices done our people, how far
this extends I do not know as all classes are
not in a condition to reach this knowledge;
but I find that those who are feel that this
is a reliable work.
	Trusting that you may receive timely
aid, I remain,
			Your loving sister, 
				LUCY W. KIMBALL


GONE TO REST.

	Sister Sarah Ann Sweet and Rawle died Dec.
12th, 1898, the cause of death being general
debili[-]y, she was born in Bristol, England July
20th, 1822. She was the mother of nine children,
three of whom survive her, she leaves a husband
eighty-four years old, one son and five daughters,
thirty-two grand-children and five great grand-
children to mourn her loss She was correspond-
ing secretary of the Morgan Stake Relief Society
twenty years, and secretary of South Morgan
Ward ninteen years; both offices she held at the
same time and filled them with honor. She died
firm in the Gospel with the hope of a glorious
resurrection.
				Your Sister in the Gospel,
							LYDIA RICH.
	MORGAN, Dec. 19th, 1898.
RESOLUTIONS OF RESPECT.
	Whereas our Father in Heaven has seen fit in
His Wisdom to call home the spirit of our worthy
and beloved sister Marie Olsen, who was born
August 9th, 1831, in Store, Lima, Veile, Amt. Den-
mark. We feel to give a few items of her life and
labors. Sister Olsen embraced the Gospel in the
old country, and emigrated to Utah in 1853, leav-
ing a good home and pleasant surroundings for
her religion. She passed through the hardships
incident to settlers in Utah at that early date,
settling first in Salt Lake City for a short time,
from there moving to Sanpete Co. where she
resided for many years, until the Castle Valley
Country began to be settled, when h[-]r husband
and herself were called to move to Castle Dale,
Emery Co., where she remained until death re-
lieved her of that dreadful disease, dropsy,
September 5th, 1898. She was President of the
Castle Dale Relief Society about four years.
	Resolved, that we the members of the Castle
Dale Relief Society hold in grateful remembrance
t[-]e aid she exercised in our Relief Society by
service contribution and counsel.
	Resolved, that we strive to hold fast to the
sacred covenants made with the Lord to the end,
and acknowledge his hand in all things as did
Sister Olsen.
	Resolved, that one copy of these resolutions be
sent to the bereaved husband, one be placed on
the Relief Society Record and one be sent to the
“Woman’s Exponent” for publication.
			CAROLINE LARSON, Prest.
			KATHINKA ANDERSON, Sec.


REORGANIZATION.

	The quarterly conference of the Relief
Society of Box Elder Stake convened in the
Tabernacle at Brigham City, Dec. 13, 1898,
President Susannah P. Booth presiding.
Singing “Know this, that every soul is
free.” Prayer by President Mary Jansen.
Singing “Sweet is the work my God, my
King.” Minutes were read and approved.
	Sister Theone Michelson reported the
Scandinavian society, said, “We can com-
fort and strengthen one another when we
possess the right spirit.” Sister Charlotte
Boden of the 2nd ward said, “We are all
laborers in the same cause. therefore, we
trust one another. I feel proud to be one
with such good sisters.”
	Sister Christina Jensen of Mantua said,
“We have buried one of our most faithful
sisters lately and one last winter, we miss
them so much. I think we couid not get a
better president than Sister Booth.” Sister
Mary Harding of Willard said, “Let every
mother instil in her daughter’s mind to live
a pure life, obedient to those placed over
her.
	Sister Mary Watkins of the 4th ward said,
“I cannot help feeling a little lonely today.
I miss our beloved president, Sister Olivia
Widerborg, but she is happy, my only de-
sire is to live to be worthy to meet my par-
ents and friends who have passed away.
	Sister Fanny Graehl said, “Although we
miss Sister Widerborg it was right she
should go to rest and Sister Booth is just
the right one in her place. I pray the sis-
ters who are called to preside over the


[Column 1]
Stake may have our faith and prayers that
they may be comforted and able to fill their
holy calling.” Sister Annie Wixom said,
"I like to uphold the officers for I know
they need it. I have belonged to the Re-
lief Society from the beginning. I was in-
timately acquainted with Sister Morley,
Aunt Harriet and Sister Widerborg. I
think there never were three better women.
I am pleased to mention their names and
speak of their good works.
	Sister Mary Jansen of the 3rd ward said,
Sister Widerborg has left us, but I think
she has always been trying to be a true,
faithful Latter-day Saint, and her counsel-
ors were true and faithful in their calling.
There is a great deal done in the Relief
Society; we marvel at it.”
	Sister Harper of Calls Fort said, “I hope
I may live so that there may be a place for
me where those who have left us are."
President Susannah P. Booth said, “I have
great sympathy for my sisters from the
settlements trying to come here at ten
o’clock. I know the Lord will not forsake
me if I do my part, and I know He will
help my counselors if we work in unison
and love. There is just as much responsi-
bility on the members as on the officers.
May we again associate with Sister Morley,
Aunt Harriet and Sister Widerborg, those
we have loved and labored with. I wish
those present to stay to luncheon provided
by the 3rd ward.”
	Singing “Now let us rejoice." Benedic-
tion by Bishop Jensen of Bear River City.
	Afternoon session After opening exer-
cises Sister Thorne of Three Mile said, “We
are trying to do all the good we can for the
poor. I ask the Lord to help us live hum-
ble and near to Him.” Sister Sadie Han-
son said, “I feel grateful and acknowledge
the hand of the Lord that I am able to
walk to this stand.” Spoke of her serious
illness and reported Beaver Dam ward.
	Bishop Jensen of Bear Lake City said,
“I feel it quite an honor to represent our
Relief Society. I have labored together
with the sisters for a little more than seven-
teen years. It has been a labor of love,
they have always been on hand voluntarily
to assist in building up the kingdom. We
have a president eighty-two years old."
Reported the ward and said, “These sisters
have not been called by President Clawson
nor President Snow, but directly by the
Lord, and I know it, and  if they will be
faithful they will succeed.”
	President Rudger Clawson said, “I am
not very well and if it was not the first
meeting after your reorganization and other
sundry reasons, I would not have been here
today.” Counseled the Relief Society to
hold short meetings. He then presented
the officers of the Stake Board as follows:
Susannah P. Booth, president; Emilia D.
Madsen and Louisa Peters, counselors;
Annie Wixom, recording secretary; Emilia
D. Madsen, corresponding secretary; Fanny
Graehl, treasurer; Eugenie S. Pierce, assis-
tant secretary. Vote was unanimous.
	President Clawson continued: “Sister
Widerborg was a woman of great faith, she
was always in line with the priesthood, she
was very successful in her labors. The sis-
ters have a little surprise for her counselors,
Sister Wright and Sister Reese, who have
labored so faithfully. We feel to bless
them, the Lord will not forsake them, the
sisters want them to know they are remem-
bered, we will remember them. I feel to
bless them in authority of the holy priest-
hood, in the name of God."

[Column 2]
	Brother Clawson could not stop blessing
Sister Wright and Sister Reese. Sister
Mary Wright and Sister Zilla Reese were
each presented with a handsome rocking
chair as a present from the sisters of Box
Elder in acknowlegement of their labors.
	Sister Mary E. Wright said, “This sur-
prise touches a tender cord, my sisters. I
feel well in the Gospel, we have enjoyed a
feast today, the spirit of the Lord has been
here. It is well with Sister Widerborg, al-
though I miss her.” Sister Zilla Reese
said, “My brethren and sisters I feel to ap-
preciate the kindness you have shown us to-
day. I do miss Sister Widerborg because
she was so kind to me. I feel to continue
in our good labor, and pray the Lord to
bless us each and everyone.”
	Sister Emilia D. Madsen was the next
speaker. She gave vent to her appreciation
of the Relief Society. Counselor Louisa
Peters said, “When I reflect on the sisters I
am called to succeed I feel to pray that I
may follow in their footsteps.
	President Kelley said, “I believe there is
a feeling of kindness in every heart. I be-
lieve you all endorse that which has been
done here, all who do raise your hand.”
(Unanimous) “I do not feel capable of ex-
pressing my feelings. I was acquainted
with Aunt Lenora Snow Morley[-] as noble
and sweet a woman as ever lived. Then
Aunt Harriet was so energetic and interest-
ed in the Relief Society, always working
for it. Then the Lord raised up another
in Olivia Widerborg, who was just as good
as the others. Now we have a new presi-
dency and I have no doubt that they are
just as able to carry out this labor. I am-
pleased that you have honored the former
counselors, that their labors are appreciated
and their hearts are made glad, their hus-
bands’ hearts are made glad in the spirit
world, Brother Wright, Brother Reese and
Brother Harvey Pierce are rejoicing to see
their wives honored.”
	President Watkins said, “I realize the
spirit of God is here, there is a feeling of
affection and love.” Spoke of the labors
of the Relief Society, holding up the hands
of the priesthood, storing up wheat for the
time of scarcity. "What a glorious work
you are engaged in my sisters! Who can
tell the result? In your labors of love seek
the counsel of those who are placed over
you and be ready to assist these new officers
as they are ready to assist the priesthood."
	Bishop Jensen said, “I have known these
sisters who have been elected for a long
time. This is a good association and a
great blessing to us, no one knows this bet-
ter than the Bishops. I was much pleased
to see what was done for the sisters, I ap-
preciate them, I know they have earned it.
I am glad we have so many good sisters.
May we be united in doing good.”
	President S. P. Booth said, “I do not
feel capable to represent the Stake, I hope
in the spring we will be able to visit all the
settlements. I think we cannot do too
much for those who have labored so long
and so faithfully. I know the Lord will
not forsake me if I do my part. I pray the
Lord to bless you all, my sisters, with the
spirit of your calling.”
	Congregation arose and sang “The spirit
of God like a fire is burning.” Benediction
by Brother Crawford.
EMILIA D. MADSEN, Cor. Sec.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


Better not tell your neighbor your
troubles unless you are willing to listen to his.


Mrs. Alice Parker Lesser attorney at law
of Boston, Mass., has been admitted by
Judge Putnam to practise in the U. S. Dis- 
trict and Circuit Courts; also in the U. S.
District Court of Appeals.
	Mrs. Elizabeth Cady Stanton and Miss
Susan B Anthony have addressed an open
letter to President McKinley, asking that
suffrage may be granted to the women of
Hawaii upon the same terms as the men.
	Miss Maud Claiborne of Glenwood, Utah,
has been elected to a primary position in
the Des Moines schools at an increased
salary, with promise of a further increase.
But the Glenwood board were not willing to
let her go. She was too good a teacher to
release. From the teacher’s standpoint we
are sorry for Miss Claiborne, since such op-
portunities do not come often; but she is to
be congratulated upon the evident appreci-
ation of her work both at home and abroad.
	Mr. Edward W. Bok, editor of the Ladies
Home Journal, says: “The platform woman
has never been a credit to, but ever a blot
upon American womanhood. I make this
emphatic statement from a personal knowl-
edge of the homes which these women
leave behind when they go to their meet-
ings.” It would be interesting to know
whether young Mr. Bok was ever enter-
tained in the home of Mrs. Julia Ward
Howe, Mrs. Mary A. Livermore, Mrs.
Lucy Stone, Frances E. Willard, Mrs.
Elizabeth Cady Stanton, Mrs. Ballington
Booth, Susan B. Anthony, or any of the
most distinguished “platform women” of
the United States?
