THE NEW YEAR.

Tho' clouds of disappointmen
Ma g[-]ther in life's sky,
Do not despair, the sun is there,
And soon will be so bright and fair,
And then those dearest hopes of thine
Like sunlight in thy path will shine;
For now the new year brightly dawns,
The old year with its fear is gone,
	And joy awaits thee.
Look not backward with regret
To the faded year—
See, the star of hope is bright,
It will guide thee to the light,
Better days are yet in store,
Tho’ the days that are no more
Like a tender flower lay,
Folded in the heart away,
	But joys awaits thee.
NINA WINSLOW ECKART.


GRANDMOTHER’S TREASURE.

It is not o’er flashing jewels in their velvet
	beds reposing,
Stiff brocades, or priceless laces worn in
	days of long ago
That poor grannie loves to linger, when the
	day is gently closing,
When the b eeze, with softer cadence mur-
	murs rhythmical and low.
And she reads no tender phrase o’er and o’er
	with failing vision
From the batch of yellow letters safely hid-
	den from the sun;
And her hands hold no love token of that by-
	gone time elysian
When the world grew fairer for her, and her
	heart was lost and won.
But she sits amid the shadows, all alone and
	happy-hearted,
In a strained and broken voice she croons a
	tender old refrain
That she sang with fuller compass in the
	days that are departed,
As she rocks the wooden cradle where her
	children all have lain.
And the babe that ne’er grew older in the
	years in which her brothers
And her sisters men and women grew,
	smiles up in infant glee
To the face above t[-]e cradle[-] and with laugh
	and shout the others,
Sundered now by eagues of ocean, seize
	her hands and climb her knee.


And that roughly-painted cradle by her lov-
	ing heart is treasured,
And its quilt of silk[-]n patchwork is with
	love and sorrow kept
Not the crowns of king or kaiser, not a store
	of gold unmeasured,
Could part grannie from the cradle where
	her babies all have slept!
M. Rock


[Column 1]
MINUTES FIRST RELIEF SOCIETY.

MASONIC HALL, NAUVOO, ILLINOIS.
Lodge Room, March 17, 1842.
	Minutes of the first organization of the
Relief Society as prepared by Miss Eliza R.
Snow, from the original minutes taken at
the meeting. A preliminary meeting was
held at the residence of Mrs. Sarah M.
Kimball, recently deceased, at which meet-
ing Miss Eliza R. Snow was present, and it
was decided at that time to consult Presi-
dent Joseph Smith about an organization:
accordingly the meeting was called by his
appointment.
	Elder John Taylor was called to the chair
and Elder Willard Richards appointed secre-
tary. Meeting commenced by singing
“The spirit of God like a fire is burning."
Prayer offered by President John Taylor.
	It was then moved by President Joseph

[Column 2]
Smith and seconded by Mrs. Cleveland that
a vote be taken to know if all present were
satisfied with each person present and will-
ing to acknowledge them in full fellowship
and admit them to the full privileges of the
institution about to be formed. Vote was
unanimous.
	President Joseph Smith addressed the
meeting to illustrate the objects of the soci-
ety; that the society might provoke the
brethren to good works in looking to the
wants of the poor, searching after objects of
charity etc., to assist by correcting the mor-
als and strengthening the virtues of the
community and save the Elders the trouble
of rebuking, etc. He proposed that the sis-
ters elect a presiding officer to preside over
them and let that presiding officer choose
two counselors; that he would ordain them
and let them preside over the society just as
the Presidency preside over the Church,
and if they needed his instruction, ask him
—he would give it from time to time. “If
any other officers are needed to carry out
the designs of the institution let them be
appointed and set apart. The minutes of
your meetings will be precedents for you to
act upon.” He then suggested the propri-
ety of electing a presidency to continue in
office during good behavior, or so long as
they should fill the office with dignity, etc.
	Motioned by Mrs. E. A. Whitney and
seconded by Mrs. S. Packard that Mrs.
Emma Smith be chosen president; passed
unanimously.
	On suggestion of President Joseph Smith
the president elect made choice of Mrs. Sar-
ah M. Cleveland and Mrs. Elizabeth Ann
Whitney for counselors.
	The ceremony of ordination was then per-
formed, when President Joseph Smith re-
sumed his remarks, giving instruction how
to govern themselves in their meetings,
what order to observe, how to regulate dis-
cussions, etc., etc., and concluded by say-
ing, “Do not injure the character of any
one; if members of the society shall conduct
themselves improperly deal with them and
keep all your doings within your own
bosoms, and hold all characters sacred.”
	It was then proposed that Elder Taylor
vacate the chair. President Emma Smith
took the chair with her counselors.
	Elder Taylor moved, seconded by Presi-
dent Joseph Smith, that the society go into
an investigation respecting what name it
should be called. Motion carried unani-
mously.
	President Joseph Smith and Elders John
Taylor and Willard Richards favored the
name Benevolent Society; Mrs. Cleveland
moved that it be Relief Society, and Mrs.
Emma Smith and Miss Eliza R Snow
argued in favor of Mrs. Cleveland’s choice.
The discussion was lengthy and interest-
ing but the decision of the majority was in
favor of Mrs. Cleveland’s choice, and the
name selected and voted upon was Female
Relief Society.
	(The prefix female was many years ago
discarded by popular vote throughout the
entire organization.)
	By unanimous vote Miss Eliza R. Snow
was elected secretary and Miss Phebe M.


Wheeler assistant secretary, and Miss
Elvira A. Cowles treasurer.
	President Joseph Smith said, “I now de-
clare this society organized with president
and counselors, according to Parliamentary
usages, and all who shall hereafter be ad-
mitted into this society must be free from
censure and received by vote.”
	Elder Taylor addressed the meeting say-
ing he was much gratified in seeing a meet-
ing of this kind in Nauvoo; his heart re-
joiced when he saw the most distinguished
characters stepping forth in a cause which
was calculated to bring into exercise every
virtue and give scope to the benevolent feel-
ings of the female heart. He rejoiced to
see this institution according to the laws of
heaven; his prayer was that the blessings
of God and the peace of heaven might rest
upon this institution henceforth.
	President Jos. Smith gave a five dollar gold
piece to commence a Relief Society fund
and said, “Whatever I do hereafter for
charity, I shall do through this society.”
	Sarah M. Kimball gave $1.00. Elizabeth
Ann Whitney 50 cts., Emma Smith $1.00,
Sarah M. Cleveland 12½cts., Willard Rich-
ards $1.00, Elder John Taylor $2.00.
	The names of the sisters present at this
meeting were Emma Smith, Martha Knight,
Elvira A. Cowles, Sarah M. Cleveland,
Phebe Ann Hawkes, Margaret A. Cook,
Desdemona Fulmer, Elizabeth Ann Whit-
ney, Sarah M. Kimball, Elizabeth Jones,
Leonora Taylor, Eliza R. Snow, Sophia
Packard, Bathsheba W. Smith, Sophia
Robinson, Philanda Herrick, Phebe M. Wheel-
er and Sophia R. Marks.
	After singing “Come let us rejoice in the
day of salvation” prayer was offered and
meeting adjourned for one week.


THANKS.

Editor Woman’s Exponent:
	It seems to me that Ada Patterson de-
serves the heartfelt thanks of the women of
Utah for the fair-minded and sensible way
in which she has explained the present em-
barrassing situation we are placed in by the
election of B. H. Roberts to Congress. I
think, too, we should acknowledge the
liberality of the Mecca, a Denver magazine,
in publishing Miss Patterson’s fair state-
ment concerning us.
	She gives us credit for at least standing
by our rights, and of maintaining such
heroism as should be sufficient to stop the
voice of unthinking people who would have
it appear that to the imbecility of the wo-
men of Utah may be charged the election
of the man who fiercely opposed their being
allowed the privilege of voting.
	Miss Patterson says: “The unembroid-
ered truth is that the women of Utah were
opposed to Mr. Roberts. A large majority
of the woman vote was against him. The
minority voted for him because they were
loyal to Democracy, rather than to Mr.
Roberts. * * *
	He opposed permitting women to vote.
* * He lost as every one knows; but the
women never forgave him. Had his elec-
tion depended upon the votes of the women,
he would have met a Balaklava on election
day."
	Thus our friend, Miss Patterson, has
nobly spoken out in behalf of the wo-
men of Utah. And as one of them I feel
to return her sincere thanks.
L. L. GREENE RICHARDS.


WOMANS EXPONENT


VIRGIN CITY, DIXIE.

Dear Exponent:
	Perhaps a few words from our little town
will be acceptable to the “Exponent.” The
past year has been a very dry one. In
August we had a very severe storm which
did but little good and a great deal of harm.
	The health of the people as a rule is pret-
ty good. Our Relief Society is doing a
good work taking care of the sick and look-
ing after the worthy poor and feeling after
the aged.
	On the 29th of December, 1897 and ’98
the Relief Society held what is called an
old people’s party. A committee being
appointed for the occasion all met at one
o’clock when tables were spread the length
of both sides of the hall with a feast of the
blessings of the earth. After dinner there
was a program rendered satisfactory to all.
In the evening [-]ll took part in the dance,
the aged having the preference, which made
the hearts of the older to feel glad, and
those of the younger to feel free and happy.
	On the morning of January 11, the peo-
ple awoke finding a happy surprise, the
snow being about three inches deep, the
first wet storm for the winter, which made
everybody rejoice.
	Hoping this will meet your approval and
wishing you a happy new year I remain,
Your sister in the Gospel,
EMILY A. STRATTON.


Mrs. F. S. Lee, Representative from Ar-
apahoe County, presided over the deliber-
ations of the Colorado House of Repre-
sentatives on the afternoon of January 11.


[Column 1]
IN MEMORIAM.
ABIGAIL GLOYDE HYDE.

	Abigail Gloyde Hyde was the daughter
of Charles Gloyde and Abigail Pratt. She
was born in Cunnington, Hampshire Co.,
Massachusetts, November 16, 1820; this
was also the birthplace and early home of
the poet William Cullen Bryant.
	Her parents were among the early fami-
lies of New England. Her grandfathers
Gloyde and Pratt both served their country
with honor in the Revolutionary war	She
spent her early years at home with her
parents and attending school. Her father
was engaged in the manufacture of woolen
goods, being the proprietor of a woolen
factory. At the age of eighteen Abigail
left school with a good common school edu-
cation and commenced work in the factory.
She had early been taught the habit of in-
dustry and was a constant worker through-
out life. She also had a veneration for
sacred things, but was quiet and unpreten-
tious in her disposition. She heard the
Gospel as revealed by the Prophet Joseph
in her early youth, and was strongly im-
pressed with the truth thereof.
	In 1840 Elder William Hyde performed
a mission in the Eastern states, and Janu-
ary 16 of that year baptized Abigail Gloyde
and two of her cousins, Sarah and Lucy
Pratt. Her mother subsequently joined
the Church. She had a constant desire to
gather with the Saints, but as her father
had not embraced the Gospel she deferred
her emigration until 1859, after her mother’s
death, when she resolved to start alone and
join with the Saints in the Rocky moun-
tains. Her cousin, Sarah Pratt, joined her
in the expedition, and on May 11, 1859,
these two young women left the homes of
their parents and all their pleasant sur-
roundings and started for the gathering
place of the Saints. Sister Abigail’s father
accompanied her fifty miles to Albany, New
York, where they joined a company of
seven hundred Saints presided over by
Elder George Q. Cannon, en route to Salt
Lake City.
	The company chartered a train and
journeyed to St. Joseph, Missouri. Here
they prepared for the ox team journey
across the plains. Sister Abigail having
been very industrious in the woolen factory
had earned and saved several hundred dol-
lars. With a portion of this she purchased
three yoke of oxen and two wagons. One
they loaded with goods the other they used
for riding and sleeping in. The women
might have driven their own team but one
of the brethren desired to do the work for
them in order to procure a ride for his wife.
They had a very pleasant ride over the
plains with which they were much de-
lighted, Sister Abigail walked most of the
way. They were ninety-six days traveling
from the Missouri river, arriving in Salt
Lake valley, October 11, 1859.
	For a month Sister Abigail and her
cousin made their home at Abraham R.
Wright’s, who had charge of the company
crossing the plains. Subsequently they
were taken to the home of Elder Hyde in
Lehi, who had baptized them.
	On January 1, 1860, Abigail and her
cousin were both married to William Hyde
and went to live in Lehi. Subsequently
they went with Brother Hyde to Cache
Valley and were among the first settlers of
Hyde Park, the place bearing the name of

[Column 2]
Elder Hyde and over which he was Bishop
until his death in 1874.
	The first summer they lived in wagons
and tents. Sister Hyde was very indus-
trious during these labors of settling a new
place and cheerfully adapted herself to the
situation. She was the first president of
the Relief Society in Hyde Park and filled
the position with credit and satisfaction for
a number of years, through her entire ad-
ministration. She brought from her labors
in the factory in Massachusetts over seven-
teen hundred dollars, which was very oppor-
tune for those hard times.
	Brother Hyde purchased a carding mill
in Logan and Sister Abigail being very
anxious to earn something to help herself
to a livelihood, and being the only one in
that section who understood carding, went
to Logan and worked in the mill for a
term of years. Subsequently she went to
work in the Co-operative Store in Hyde
Park, and proved herself in this capacity a
most efficient business woman and  was
scrupulously honest in all her transactions.
She continued in this employment for
several years after the death of her husband,
when the store went into other hands. Be-
ing left without employment she went to
Logan City and again took up her abode
six days in the week in the carding mill.
She continued in this labor several years.
	In the former years of her labors in the
carding mill when the business was so
flush that the mill had to run day and
night, Sister Hyde would run the mill from
midnight until noon, when she would be
released by an able bodied man: and thus
for a long time she did the work which was
laborious enough for a strong man, and
which few women could do.
	She was postmistress of Hyde Park for
twelve years, and while absent working in
the carding mill her cousin Sarah, who
lived in the same house, filled the place as
deputy. Her reports and transactions were
always correct, giving the best of satisfac-
tion to all concerned.
	During this busy life of constant indus-
try and hard labor she bore three children,
two sons and one daughter, all of whom
survive her and are active, faithful Latter-
day Saints. The eldest son is now the
Bishop of Hyde Park.
	During the past few months Sister Hyde
was afflicted with paralysis, and passed
peacefully away without a struggle at 1:35
a. m., Tuesday, January 10, 1899. Her
children were all present at her bedside
during her last moments. She leaves two
sons and a daughter, six grandsons and
three granddaughters to mourn her loss.
	Her funeral was held in Hyde Park at
noon, Saturday 14th inst., and was attended
by the family and many loving friends.
Consoling and instructive remarks were
made by Presidents S. M. Molen, Orson
Smith and Elder Matthias Cowley. The
grave was dedicated by Bishop William
Hyde of the 5th ward of Logan, and thus
was laid to rest the remains of one of the
noblest of women, honest, chaste, benevo-
lent, industrious and faithful to the trust
reposed in her throughout life, from child-
hood to the grave. She fell asleep in the
hope of a glorious resurrection. She will
receive a crown of eternal lives in the
resurrection of the just.
						M. F. C.

is unable to appreciate happiness.


WOMAN'S EXPONENT


A BEAUTIFUL RESPONSE. 

A very bright little army woman rose to
the occasion well a few days ago when she
was one of the guests at a small luncheon
given at one of the large hotels for some
distinguished New England people. It was
a large affair and in an impromptu manner
a number of toasts were given. In the
midst of it the little army woman with hor-
hor heard some one say:
	“We have among us the wife of an
officer in the regular army, and I think it
is only fitting that she should respond to
the toast ‘The Flag.’ ” The men applaud-
ed and the women smiled encouragement,
and trembling a little as she held up the
glass of wine, the officer’s wife spoke:
	“I belong to a class of women to whom
the flag is especially near and dear. All
day it waves over the roofs of our little
homes in the far frontier, and at sundown
the rustle of its folds, mingled with the
notes of ‘The Star Spangled Banner,’ is
the lullaby of our babies. We know that
it will be the winding sheet of those dear-
est to us. Since the word Santiago has been
written in our hearts I see yet more in this
flag—in its red, the blood of our martyrs;
in the white, the paleness of their ashen
faces: in the blue, the lips set with the
word ‘Freedom,’ and in the stars the soul
that will shine on forever.’’


[Column 1]
WOMAN’S EXPONENT. 

Salt Lake City, Utah,
			Sunday, Jan. 15, 1899.
Dear Friend:
	In my left hand I hold the last bright
little leaf that has fallen from your balmy
tree, while with my right hand I essay to
trace a few slight veins with the hope that
they may appear, with no unsalutary effect,
on your next leaf when it drops.
	Upon entering your beautiful city last
evening, knowing I had but twenty-four
hours to remain, and that all the daylight I
should spend here would be while the
sacred Sabbath was lulling her children in-
to quiet inactivity, a feeling of disappoint-
ment crept over me. But I had not long to
droop over my misfortunes, for I had scarce-
ly made myself comfortable at my hotel
when dinner was announced. Being hun-
gry I was glad to be seated at table with
the other guests, and not very anxious to
enter into conversation with my entirely
new neighbors until my appetite was some-
what abated.
	The first I remember of speaking was
when a young woman at my right offered
me pickles and I said I would prefer catch-
up. That broke the ice.
	“Are you from the North or the South?”
she asked.
	“From the East,” I answered. “And
yourself?”
	“I came up from the South to attend the
funeral. Suppose you are here for the
same purpose, are you not?”
	“Didn’t know there was to be a funeral,”
I answered. “Whose is it, and when does
it take place?”
	“You did not know that Brother George
Goddard’s funeral is to take place tomorrow
at 2 o’clock, in the Tabernacle! Ah, but
you are a stranger here then! Pardon me,
I had no thought but you were one of our
people.”
	We got on finely after that. My newly
found friend proved to be, I must believe,
a newly appointed guardian angel to con-

[Column 2]
duct me where I might find the door that
will be opened to all humble seekers after
light and truth, if they will but knock.
	She it was, that friend, that angel of
mine, who put into my hand this
First of January “Woman’s Exponent”
with its most wonderful tracings of
your magnificent organization, the Relief
Society, some of the marvelous works of
which are shown in the brief reports con-
tained in that paper.
	But she explained much to me regarding
your people in a way and manner that I
shall hardly find in books or papers, and
she took me with her to the Great Taber-
nacle to attend that most splendid funeral.
	I never can forget what I saw and heard
there, and I never want to. Henceforth I
would like to work for and with those same
Sunday School children, among whom that
grand chieftain of yours, General George
Goddard, has spent so much of his life.
	Your teachers of righteousness are the
most thrilling, soul-inspiring speakers I
have ever listened to, and I have heard the
most eminent and eloquent divines in dif-
ferent parts of the world.
	In making my adieux, I have now but to
say of your wonderful city and people, “The
half has never been told.” And I feel like
adding, “Your people shall be my people,
and your God my God.”
			M. RACHELLE MEHETTE.


Mrs. Mary A. Wright was elected to the
Idaho House of Representatives from
Kootenai County at the recent elections, in
addition to Mrs. Clara Campbell of Ada
County, and Mrs. Harriet Noble of Boise.

	Mrs. Ellen C. Johnson, superintendent of
the Sherborn prison for women, has been
appointed by Governor Wolcott as a dele-
gate from Massachusetts to the Convention
of the National Prison Association to be
held at New Orleans, Jan. 21 to 24. She
is announced to address the convention on
“Prisons for Women.’’


RED CROSS NURSES.

MARGHERlTA ARLINA HAMM.
	“Of the nurses who attended the sick and
wounded some were hired and received a
salary. A second class received no salary,
but had their expenses paid. A third class
paid their own expenses and traveled great
distances in going from their homes to the
camps in the South or in the West Indies.
A fourth class consisted of members of local
branches residing in the neighborhood of
the hospitals, who volunteered their ser-
vices for a day, week, or month, and who
did not wait for any special order from the
central authority. Besides nurses there
were distributors, inspectors, physicians,
clerks, guards, bookkeepers and others.
Altogether over 1,000 volunteers served
their country in the peaceful ranks of the
Red Cross army.
	“Of the amount of money raised and ex-
pended by the Red Cross it is very difficult
to get anything like accurate figures. A
few contributors and auxiliaries published
the value of the supplies they forwarded
either to the supply depots or to the camps,


but at least three-fourths kept undivulged
the value of their donations.
	“Mr. Richard J. Hinton, who gave this
division much study, and found that the
amount of money received at the New
York office between June 15 and August
26 aggregated over $235,000, an average
of $3,400 per day. The supplies received
during the same period came to about
$250,000, or $3,500 per day. * * *
	“The grand total of gifts from all parts of
the world, including supplies and trans-
portation, was not less than $3,000,000 and
probably not more than $4 000,000, al-
though the agents in different states say it
is very difficult to value the supplies.
	“No such munificence was ever known be-
fore in the history of the world. Though
the war is over practically the Red Cross
keeps up its good work and will, as far as
it can, bend its energies to ameliorating
the condition of the Cubans. In Porto
Rico little or nothing remains to be done,
and in the Philippines there will be no
great need of charitable action; but in the
luckless island of Cuba a very large body of
people will require assistance for two or
three years before they become prosperous
and self supporting. Not alone are the
fields ruined, but the homes, factories,
machinery and live-stock have been de-
stroyed.”


REFLECTIONS. 

BY NINA WINSLOW ECKART.
The snow lies on the frozen ground,
	The night is dark and still,
Save now and then the winds low moan
	Across some distant hill.
And i[-] a r[-]om a young man sits, 
His head is bowed in prayer,
He longs to be at home again
	Where life is free from care.

Around the room are tokens dear—
	A pic[-]ure on the wall,
Recalls to him his college days,
	His friends and classmates all. 
The room where four glad years were spent,
	In thought he sees once more,
His books and chair await him there,
	He sees the open door.

And thinks “Oh would that I could stand
	Upon that threshold bright,
And have those dear, familiar scenes
	To cheer me up tonight.”
And then like mi[-]t before his eyes
	Another vision sweeps,
And swiftly as the life blood flows, 
	Around his heart it creeps.

He hears again his mother’s voice,
	And on his fevered brow
Her gentle hand again is placed,
	(Oh, were she with him now!)
The tears unbidden fill his eyes, 
	Then dashing them away
He rises to his feet again,
	And now I hear him say:

“Ah, yes, my life is lonely here
	But I will not give way,
The sun behind the cloud is bright,
	Although the sky is gray. 
And when a fortune I have made,
	They’ll be so proud to know,
How I have worked and won my way,
	In all this world of woe.”

Yes, hopeful be, my friend, for Fate
	Is good to those who try,
Beneath the cold snow of despair,
	The Crocus blossoms lie,
And they will bud and bloom for you,
	Ere many days are done,
The clouds will pass from out your way,
	And the behold the sun.

SALT LAKE CITY, FEBRUARY 1, 1899.


[Column 1]
A WONDERFUL AGE.

	Truly this age is significant of a higher
order of things, a larger outlook soon to
manifest itself in the development of the
human race. Assuredly in all that relates
to progress and to culture even in aesthet-
ics the human family is not going back-
ward, but forward. Organization has had
much to do with this onward movement.
	To be sure war has not ceased altogether,
but efforts made and plans proposed for a
Peace Congress for all the powerful nations,
foretells vividly what we who are living to-
day may anticipate as near at hand. One
even now looks forward to the unexpected,
and might almost add the impossible; for
really what would have been claimed
as utterly impossible fifty years ago has
come to pass, and is almost classed as some-
thing of an i[-]cident instead of, as our fore-
fathers would have thought it, a positive
miracle or unwarrantable in fact.
	Not only the rapid advancement made in
the world of affairs, great and small, and
the common workaday world itself, but the
unprecedented growth of ideas; not neces-
sarily from the learned in the arts and
sciences, or direct lines of knowledge, but
a kind of spontaneous outcome as it were
from some fount of intelligence which had
been reposing through the ages and culmin-
ated at last in the nineteenth century, crys-
talizing certain truths for the uplifting of
mankind. The Lord of heaven and
earth has the over-ruling power, notwith-
standing the wisdom of the world and the
impracticability of many of the theories of
men, and He will care for His own, and
those that seek after the bread of life will
be entitled to the promises made to the
faithful.
	Women have in this present generation
taken hold of some of the problems of the
day and wrestled with them with the in-
tuitive spiritual strength that seems to be a
part of the feminine nature. Men in high
places who have hitherto been tenacious of
rights and of power are beginning to feel,
and in some measure to acknowledge the
superiority of woman’s judgment in many
important matters, and to appreciate her
help as a force working for good in nearly
all the varied lines of progress.
	It is a self-evident truth that women
stand on a higher plane morally and spirit-
ually than men, and why, then, may they
not mingle in governmental affairs, since
society and the true life of the individual
and the nation depends so much upon good,

[Column 2]
wholesome laws judiciously administered,
with a view to restraining evil and promot-
ing good in practical ways that should tend
to better standards in every day life.
	While mothers and sisters were content
with domesticity and never looked into the
outside world except as some man gave his
masculine view, the world wagged along;
but when woman awoke to a conscious-
ness of her own power she began to wonder
and to calculate and inquire what were her
rights. The key of knowledge was turned
for her, and men no longer had the same
absolute sway. Women began to do some
of their own thinking on moral questions,
and then social problems were discussed on
the moral side, and following that economic
measures and so on through the whole cate-
gory of human affairs. To woman has
been opened the great domain of the world,
and why not to the mothers of generations
of men? But ask as one will why and
wherefore the fact remains that woman is
now fairly launched into the arena of Hercu-
lean endeavor to make her way onward
and upward in the ascent of life to the high-
er plane and to lift up those weaker and
less able than herself, the women of less en-
lightened countries and the children; striv-
ing to make better the conditions and en-
vironments, removing obstructions that lie
in the pathway to excellence, and always
bearing inasmuch as is possible the burdens
of others, who are struggling to reach safer
ground.
	Combined efforts are multiplying, and
necessarily must increase the power against
tyranny and oppression of all kinds, and
where men and women unite for high pur-
poses, intelligently waging war against
wrong-doing, the victory so long looked for
must eventually be expected.
	But such a thing cannot be brought
about momentarily; howevheer leaven is
working and the prophecies of Joel con-
cerning the last days when the spirit of the
Lord would be poured out upon all flesh
are being fulfilled before the very eyes of
the generations of today and they know it
not. There is a power that is working for
righteousness in the earth though the in-
fluences that tend to evil are opposed.
	Whatever may be said of the age, every-
one must admit it is a wonderful age how-
ever one marks progress. History must so
record it. This nineteenth century now
drawing to a close is the most eventful one
since the days of the Savior and His Apos-
les in the first century of the Christian era.
	The peace project of the Czar of Russia
presages something more than has yet ap-
peared; various conjectures about his motives
are afloat, but the Lord is working among
the nations and the desire for peace which
is growing among highly enlightened peo-
ples speaks for itself in a voice not to be
misunderstood. Russia may be afraid of
other nations and consequently advise dis-
armament, but a higher power than the
Czar can be recognized in the treaty for
peace along which line some men and wo-
men, the Peacemakers, have been laboring
during the last decade with great diligence.
Summing up the great changes in the last
hundred years must bring evidence to all
who stop to think, and another age like
this will assuredly bring greater things
to pass in the same ratio.


We have asked repeatedly through the
paper and officially for the names of women
who were elected in November 1898, but
have not yet been able to get replies.


TRIENNIAL NATIONAL COUNCIL.

	A delegation of ladies will go from Utah
to attend the Triennial of the National
Council of Women to be held in Washing-
ton early this month, and will be there in
time for the preliminary or prefatory meet-
ing to be held Saturday evening, the 11th
of February, at the Arlington Hotel where
the Council will have its headquarters dur-
ing the time of the Triennial.
	On Sunday women ministers of
different denominations who are in attend-
ance at the Council will probably be invited
to speak in some of the churches in the city
as they usually are, or have been in the past.
	The National Woman’s Relief Society
and the Young Ladies National Mutual
Improvement Association are affiliated with
the National Council and will send dele-
gates, or rather, technically speaking, a
proxy for the president and one delegate
from each organization. The president of
each society affiliated is vice-president to
the Council proper, and all business that
pertains to the Council is referred that she
may act in reference to it, either in person
or by correspondence or telegram.
	Mrs. Zina D. H. Young and Mrs. Elmina
S. Taylor are by virtue of the office they
hold vice presidents. The presidents and
delegates are the only ones who have votes
except the general officers of the society,
and in their absence or non-attendance the
president is represented by proxy, and the
delegate by an alternate who vote in their
place.
	Patrons of the Council have no vote,
though they have all other privileges; they
may attend all business meetings, partici-
pate in discussions and have the privilege
of the floor, but cannot vote as they have
no constituency to represent. Annual con-
tributors are admitted to the same privi-
leges as patrons. To become a patron one
must be recommended as a person of influ-
ence and standing in society by some one to
whom she is known and who is herself
known to the Society. The fee for patrons
is one hundred dollars down, and for an-
nual contributors five dollars a year. There
are several ladies in this city who are annual
contributors, though very few patrons.
	The National Council is certainly a most
dignified body of superior women; they
hail from all parts of the United States
and Fraternal Delegates have come from
other countries to the Triennial at each of
its regular sessions and also to its business
session.
	In Washington in 1895, at the second
Triennial, the Countess of Aberdeen and
Lady Henry Somerset were both upon
the platform as speakers with many other
distinguished women writers and orators.
	Mrs. Ellen A. Richardson of Boston,
president of the George Washington Mem-
orial Association, made a magnificent
speech at the executive meeting in Omaha
in October at one of the public sessions,
also Mrs. Kate Waller Barrett, who repre-
sents the Florence Crittendon [-]ssociation.
It is to be regretted that more Utah women
cannot attend the Triennial, as it is most
educational in many ways.
	It was confidently expected at the session
of 1895 that the next Triennial would meet
in an upper and lower house as the Con-
gress of the United States does, but so
many things have since transpired to hinder
the progress which the Council expected to
make that the idea is given up for the pres-
ent, although not abandoned.


Mrs. Mary A. Dickinson, who was elected
president of the Council in 1895, and who
was certainly well qualified in every respect
for the position, after two years was com-
pelled to resign by order of her physician,
and after much persuasion and entreity
Mrs. May Wright Sewall, the former presi-
dent, was induced to accept the office for
the unexpired term. Mrs. Sewall is, and
was at that time, vice president of the Inter-
national Council, which is composed of
National Councils, as the National Council
is of National Societies or Associations.
Mrs. Sewall has been nominated for the
next president of the International Council,
and will without doubt be elected. The
present incumbent of the office is the
Countess of Aberdeen.
	A new president, therefore, will be elected
at the coming Triennial, and this will be a
very important feature of the Triennial.
Probably no woman in the United States is
better qualified (if as well) to hold such
a high position as Mrs. Sewall, but the ladies
must be content when they know one of
their own country women, an American,
has been sought for and honored with a
higher place, to preside over many collective
National Councils from several different
countries, speaking different languages,
some of them ladies of rank and title, yet
Mrs. Sewall is their acknowledged peer,
nevertheless.
	The members of the Council are proud
of the distinction Mrs. Sewall has justly ob-
tained, and fully appreciate the honor it is
to American women.
	We ardently hope Mrs. May Wright
Sewall will be the representative from the
National Council of Women to the Paris
Exposition, and Congress will be petitioned
to make this appointment, and with a mil-
lion of women one would think Congress
could scarcely refuse to give this organized
work a woman representative.
	Mrs. Bertha M. Palmer will probably be
appointed to represent the work of the wo-
men of the United States. Such an appoint-
ment for her has been applied for—but so
far there is no positive assurance of it, al-
though there is scarcely a doubt with re-
gard to Mrs Palmer’s appointment.
	The National Woman’s Relief Society
will probably be represented by Mrs. E. B.
Wells, proxy for president, and Mrs. Zina
Y. Card delegate, Mrs. Carrie S. Thomas,
alternate delegate. The Y. L. N. M. I. A.
by Mrs. Martha H. Tingey, proxy for
president, Mrs. Minnie J. Snow delegate,
and Miss Ann M. Cannon alternate to dele-
gate and member of Resolution Commit-
tee. Mrs Susa Y. Gates as speaker, sub-
ject, “Status of Hawaiian Women.” Mrs.
E.B. Wells’ subject will be “Good Citizen-
ship.”
	It is also expected that Mrs. Lucy B.
Young and Mrs. Elizabeth McCune will go,
as both are patrons of the National Council.

EDITORIAL NOTES.

	In Tooele County, Utah, a woman re-
corder and collector has been appointed.
In Box Elder County Miss Fanny Graehl,
who served last term as recorder, was again
elected; also in Millard County Mrs. Deli-
lah King Olsen was re-elected for this term.
	Mrs. Susa Young Gates of Provo, editor
of the Young Woman’s Journal, has been
appointed by Mrs. May Wright Sewall
Active Chairman of the Press Committee of
the National Council of Women of the
United States.
	The Utah Legislature is spending most
of its time the last three weeks balloting
for a Senator and making eloquent speeches.
The two women naturally drift into the
same line of action, notwithstanding they
are both strong-minded. The fact is there
should have been more women elected,
then their influence might have turned the
scale and perhaps less time used in making
choice of a suitable Senator.
	The agents of the “Exponent” in all the
several wards and Stakes of Zion, and also
individual subscribers indebted for the
paper are urgently requested to settle all
old accounts at the earliest date possible.
This becomes an absolute necessity on ac-
count of the change that takes place in the
management of the Deseret Evening News.
The “Exponent” will not be sent to any
subscriber after January 1899, unless pre-
paid. All delinquents will as soon as pos-
sible receive written notices of bills due, in
addition to tbe printed matter.
	Tuesday, January 31 was the anniversary
of the birthday of President Zina D. H.
Young, familiarly and lovingly known
among the sisters as “Aunt Zina,” and in
commemoration of the time members and
relatives of the Young family were invited
to meet at the residence of Mrs. Phebe Y.
Beatie to spend a few hours of the after-
noon, to pay their loving tribute
to this distinguished Mother in Israel,
whose whole life has been spent in doing
good. Everywhere her works praise her
and her name is spoken of lovingly by
young and old. May her useful and
beautiful life be prolonged until she has
finished all her heart desires to do, and her
name and her works of love and charity
never be forgotten by the generation in
which she has taken such an active part,
but be handed down in the annals of the
Church as one of its noblest heroines.
	On the same day occurs the birthday
anniversary of President Jane S. Richards
who was born two years later. Both
these sisters were born in Jefferson Co ,
New York State, and have been intimately
associated ever since the organization of
the Relief Society in Nauvoo. Mrs. Rich-
ards is at present sojourning for a brief time
it in Los Angelos, California; her husband,
President Franklin D. Richards, has gone
over quite recently for a visit. Their son,
Hon. C. C. Richards and wife are with
them, and no doubt they are enjoying the
happy occasion and celebrating the birth-
day with all due ceremony, gifts and trib-
utes of love in that land of flowers, fruits
and sunshine. The “Exponent” extends
loving congratulations to these famous wo-
men, who have assisted in laying the
foundation of one of the largest societies of
women organized in this country. May it
be their good fortune to see much accom-

[Column 2]
plished for the futherance of this charitable
and philanthropic work during the next
few years, a work which has so engrossed
their time and talents in the years that are
past. A work of practical relief, love and
sympathy carried on in the midst of this
people, the magnitude and extent of which
is little known among many even of the
members of the Church.


PERSEVERANCE AT CENTERVILLE.

	The Centerville Relief Society owned
two lots of its own and desired to build, as
they had no place of their own to meet in,
but on inquiring the cost they found it
would be more than they could afford, not
wishing to go into debt too much. On the
adjoining lot from theirs was a school house
which they were advised to purchase if it
could be bought on reasonable terms. A
committee was formed of the brethren to
look after it. They found they could buy
it for five hundred dollars. Through the
perseverance of the committee, and prayers
and help of the brethren and sisters who
responded nobly to the cause, they bought
it, so we now own two thirds of a block.
The school house was cleared and repaired,
its size is thirty by thirty-five feet, it cost
to repair and furnish it one hundred and 
five dollars.
	The hall was dedicated on the 1st of June,
1898. There were present President J.
Hess and Counselors Grant and Clark, of
the Davis Stake; the Bishopric of the ward,
A. B. Porter, Melvin H. Randall and John
J. Smith; Stake Presidency of the Relief 
Society, President Susan Grant and Coun-
selors Clark and Holt and R. S. Stake 
Secretary Phebe Sessions; also Mrs. E. D.
Roundy of Salt Lake City and other visitors
from the different wards. It was decided
that the Relief Society should fast in thank-
fulness to their Heavenly Father for His
goodness unto them.
	The meeting was called to order by
President Margaret Randall. Singing
“The spirit of God.” Prayer by Bishop
Aaron Porter. Singing, Invocation com-
posed by E. D. Roundy, after which Presi-
dent Randall gave a brief sketch of how we
bought our house, and said she knew the
Lord had greatly blessed us in opening our
way to obtain the means to pay for it.
	The dedicatory prayer was then offered
by our beloved president, John W. Hess.
Short addresses were made by the visiting
brethren and sisters, congratulating us up-
on our building and furnishings, and
prophesying that the way would be opened 
whereby we could pay the rest of the in-
debtedness. They also spoke of the magni-
tude of the Relief Society work and its aims
and purposes, and said it gave the sisters
great scope to use their talents and abilities
in relieving suffering humanity, and in
many ways could we do good; we could by
our walk and conversation in our daily life
teach the principles of virtue and truth and 
sow the seeds of faith in the hearts of the
young people, and encourage all to do right
by our own example as well as precept.
Prayed the Lord to ever bless the sisters
with a full realization of their duties and
give them strength to perform the same.
	A poem was then read dedicated to our
beloved president, Margaret Randall, in
which her faithfulness and integrity to the
Gospel and the Relief Society work in
general was portrayed, composed and read
by Mrs. E. D. Roundy, after which the


meeting was adjourned and cake and ice
cream were served to the assembly.
		MARY ROCKWOOD, Sec.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


SEMI-MONTHLY MEETING.

	The Ladies’ Semi-monthly meeting was
held Jan. 9, 1899, Counselor E. J. Stevenson
presiding. Singing “Come let us anew.”
Prayer by Sister Louisa Stevens. Singing
“All hail the new born year.” Minutes
read and approved.
	Counselor Stevenson told the congrega-
tion that President Horne had been very
sick but was improving. Spoke of the les-
sons we must learn in life and how valu-
able they would be when we had learned
them, pray for the help of the Lord. The
Prophet Joseph Smith said at one o’clock
was a good time to pray. Sister Kiddle
who had testified in our last meeting had
now been laid to rest. Spoke also of the
death of Sister Priscilla Staines, said let us
all be prepared to go.
	Sister Brown spoke of Temple work and
the peace it brings us, thought we should
look to God to heal our sick, etc. The
congregation sang “The Spirit of God.”
	Sister Mary P. Silver said, “We are the
most blessed people on the earth.” Ex-
horted all to attend to their duties and be
prepared to meet eternity.
	Sister Martha Morris sang “We’ll stand
the strain.” A sister, name unknown, of-
fered a humble prayer, and another sister
bore a strong testimony.
	Sister Barnes said she knew Joseph Smith
was a prophet of God. She became con-
verted to the Gospel by reading the Book
of Mormon. Through her trials and hard-
ships she had been brought nearer to God.
Another sister bore an excellent testimony.
	Sister Almeda Young spoke upon bearing
testimony when the Spirit of God moves,
and the necessity of our listening to those
who are placed over us, and of the integrity
of President M. I. Horne.
	The congregation sang “Arise my soul,
arise.” Sister Barton spoke of the com-
mandments given to Joseph Smith, and
said how blessed we should be if we would
keep them. She thought the rearing of the
youth was of the utmost importance and we
should lead in all things, attend to our
meetings, pay our tithing and never speak
against the priesthood.
	Sister Louise Stevenson said she thought
it was best to go with our children to their
meetings, teach them principles of truth.
	Sister Julia S. Woolley and Sister Mary
Morris bore testimonies of the Gospel and
the peace it brings. A sister in the congre-
gation spoke of her sons going to the late
war and how the Lord had preserved them.
Another sister bore testimony and urged all
to stand firm and unwavering to the truth.
Another sister told how her father had cast
her off because she joined the Latter-day
Saints.
	Sister Mary Morris thought those who
spoke against the principles of the Gospel
were prompted by the adversary. Congre-
gation sang “What was witnessed in the
heavens.”
	Counselor E. J. Stevenson said let us
keep humble and purify our souls so that
we can enjoy the spirit of the Lord. We
are a tried people, but go to our Heavenly
Father and he will never betray us.
	Meeting adjourned for two weeks. Sing-
ing “The God we adore.” Benediction by
Sister Lydia D. Alder.
				J. S. WOOLLEY, Sec.

THE SILK WORM AND ITS SILK

	When Noah was a little boy, about 150
or 200 years old, and hundreds of years be- 
fore he received Divine instructions to con-
struct the Ark, when not far to the west-
ward of Mount Ararat where the Ark rested
after the flood, and just across the Caspian
Sea, a little Chinese girl (she was about 14
years old) named Si-ling chi, the wife of the
Emperor Hoang-ti, was watching one day
early in the spring, with astonishment the
movements of some caterpillars that were
feeding upon the leaves of a mulberry tree.
Day by day she watched and day by day
the caterpillars grew rapidly.
	She noticed that as they moved their
heads from side to side that a liquid, gum-
my substance appeared to issue from their
mouths and soon change into a soft and
downy thread. She used to go every day
and watch the caterpillars working. Bye
and bye, when the cocoons were formed,
she had the curiosity to take one in her
hand and examine it. She found it about
the size and shape of a peanut; from it a
number of loose threads floated about.
Pulling one of them she found it was so
light it would float in the air, yet so tough
she could pull off quite a length of it. It
occured to her that if these cocoons could be
softened she could obtain a longer thread
of the silk and be able to use it. After
some study she soaked the cocoons in
warm water and began pulling on the
thread. To her delight it grew longer and
longer, and she wound it around her fingers
till the cocoon was all unwound, and then
she found she had a continuous thread
3,000 to 4,000 feet long. In her girlish
glee she turned to her waiting woman and
exclaimed, “Hoo-le-ma, (See;) Ming-hing
haynung,” (Look ! How beautiful!) The
silk when reeled was a bright golden color
and doubtless it is from this fact the Chi-
nese are so fond of yellow, it being the
royal color of their country.
	Si-ling-chi’s industry did not end here,
for she went several steps further and
taught the ladies of her palace to manu-
facture and embroider silk. Not only the
emperor, but Mandarins and others ap-
peared in public robed in fabrics of dazzling
hues.
	For many years the Chinese guarded
well the secret of the source of their im-
mense wealth, for raw silk was imported
all over Asia, India, Persia and Arabia.
There are two legends as to how the secret
got out. One is that some monks conveyed
the eggs in their staffs over the border; the
other is that the pantless little fellow Cupid
had a hand in it.
	The first known place outside of Asia
where silk was cultivated was the Island of
Cos. The cloth woven in this country
was of the thinnest possible texture and
transparent. Justinian secured a monopoly
of the trade. Under the care of the Greeks
the silk worm took kindly to its Western
home and their silken textures became
famous. At a later period the Saracens
maintained a mastery over the trade and
by them it was spread east and west.
	The eggs of the silk worm are deposited
by a moth known as the Bombyx mori,
which lays about 350 of them. After im-
pregnating the female the male dies. He
rarely survives more than four or five
hours. The female lives two or three
days to deposit the egg. When first de-
posited they are a beautiful bright hue, but
they soon turn color, becoming dull and

[Column 2]
dark. She never deposits the eggs in a
heap, that is to say her eggs are never laid
one top of the other. What her purpose is
in spreading them out I do not know. That
is a habit of which there is no intelligent
explanation. The male is a little fellow,
creamy in color and much livelier than his
mate. He flutters about while the female
remains still. Her brief life is spent with-
in a radius of six inches. They neither
eat nor drink, having no mouths. They
are born only for a reproductive purpose.
Having deposited her eggs the female fol-
lows the male into the Great Unknown.
The eggs hatch out the following spring.
Its product is dark and covered with fine
hair. One hundred will weigh a grain and
at maturity the same one hundred will
weigh 9,500 grains. The little worms are
fed on young mulberry leaves. They go
on feeding and growing rapidly for a few
days, then refuse food, for two days they
remain in a torpid state after which they
cast their skins, which do not seem to grow
apace with the rest of their bodies. Then
they go on feeding and growing when the
same thing occurs. In all they cast their
skins four times and go through five ages,
they have about thirty-one days of eating
and nine days of fasting. Then sets in a
brief era of unrest during which they
wander about moving their heads, which is
preliminary to the final act of their strange
and wonderful career. Having found a
suitable place they commence throwing
from their spinneret the airy filament
which they have been storing up since they
began feeding. The spinneret is a very
small orifice below the mouth. Around
and around the body the filament is wound,
the worm making the silk a tomb for itself.
To this purpose the rest of its life is steadi-
ly devoted. It makes sixty-five motions of
its head every minute, or a total of three
hundred thousand to spin its cocoon. Dur-
ing this time (a minute) the filament flows
from four to six inches from the spinneret.
Its labor done, the legs drop off and death
intervenes.
	In the cocoon, however, is the pupa, or
chrysalis with no chance of escape. How-
ever there is a way out. In ten or fifteen
days the moth is developed and it ejects
against the end of the cocoon a secretion
which softens the gum, and pushing some
of the threads aside and breaking others,
the moths issue to play their part in the re-
productive process already described. So it
goes on indefinitely. This is all that is
known or can be known of the silk worm.
It eats and works. The laws that govern
its being are arbitrary and inflexible. From
these laws it seems to have no desire to es-
cape Where the conditions are favorable
it goes right on about its business, carrying
out the designs of its Creator and finally
yielding up its brief life in obedience to a
mandate as irrevocable as our own fate,
whatever it may be when we are called to
account. By what so called instinct it is
governed I cannot say. I cannot answer
that any more than I can solve any one of
the numberless profound problems which
we see about us. Its purpose most proba-
bly, is to provide a home for the chrysalis,
and to do that it sacrifices its own life. The
perpetuation of its species seems to be its
only aim, and in carrying out this purpose
it plays into the hands of man.
	A singular thing about the spinning pro-
cess is that the thread is never tangled.
Row after row of it is completed, the worm
never beginning a new one, until the old


one is finished. Then man steps in and
after stifling the pupa, unwinds the cocoons
with a filature. The cocoons are at first
hard and unyielding. They are softened in
warm water with a little soap. The ends
are gathered up with a probe to which they
attach themselves while in the water, and
then the filature unwinds them and they
become raw silk.
THE COCOONS.
	The cocoons from which the moths are
allowed to escape are taken with the waste,
that is, the silk thrown out by the worm
before it commences its cocoon, and con-
verted into spun silk,and used in the manu-
facture of inferior goods. The method of
manufacture consists in cleaning and
macerating the raw material, after which it
is carded and made into thread somewhat
after the manner of cotton.
	Cocoons vary in length from 300 to
1,800 yards in one continuous thread which
is 10/213 of an inch in diameter composed
of two fibers. Sixty thousand cocoons,
which would be produced from 1 1/2 oz. of
eggs, would produce enough silk to girdle
the earth at the equator.
	Ten to twelve pounds of leaves con-
sumed by the worms produce one pound of
cocoons. Three and three quarters pounds
of cocoons yield one pound (16 ozs.) of raw
silk, and one pound of raw silk produces,
when degummed, 12 ozs. of pure silk, one
pound of which with the assistance of dye-
stuffs, make twenty yards of a heavy im-
ported gros-grain. Twelve thousand
pounds of raw silk, or the product of about
50,000,000 worms are used weekly by a
leading American firm in the manufacture
of spool silk alone. Over $100,000,000
were expended in the United States during
the year 1888 for silk. In colored silks
35,000 shades or tints can be dyed. Black
silk fabrics can be adulterated five hundred
per cent. Pure dye silks are almost inde-
structible, 16-oz. dye being the best for
service.
				JOHN DEAN.

R. S. REPORT.

BINGHAM STAKE.
	The quarterly conference of the Relief
Society of Bingham Stake convened at
Lewisville, December 17, 1898, President
E. J. Bennett presiding. Opened by sing-
ing “The morning breaks.” Prayer by 
Bishop Jardine. Singing “Now let us re-
joice.” President E. J Bennett made a
few opening remarks, minutes of the pre-
vious forenoon conference were read and 
approved.
	President James E. Steele explained to
the sisters the necessity of sending in
their charitable reports to the Bishops.
	President E. J. Bennett said we should
give what we have to give through the Re-
lief Society that we may have credit on the
books, and then forward our half yearly re-
ports to the Bishops that the sisters may
have credit for what they do, and when 
the teachers go around they should try and 
find out if there are any who are in need,
and those who may need assistance should
not be too proud to receive help. We should
be careful not to make our business too 
public so as to hurt any one’s feelings.
	Reports were then given as follows:
Lewisville, President Jardine; Menan,
Counselor Mary Stephens; Iona, Secretary


[Column 1]
Hall. Each society was reported as doing
a good work.
	President James E. Steele said he was
pleased to meet in Relief Society conference.
He always liked to be on time so as to hear
the petitions that go up to the Father and
partake of the spirit that we may be in pos-
session of it in our conference. It is neces-
sary that we should come together that the
people may be instructed and encouraged.
The Lord sees the needs of His people and
thus we have these privileges. He could
see in meeting with those who did not at-
tend these conferences and did not partake
of the spiritual food, that they were not so
energetic. It looked as though the re-
sponsibility of this work were resting upon
a few. An object in coming to meeting
should be to be fed spiritual food and in-
structed. The reward is to those who are
faithful all the day long. Spoke upon the
storing of grain; hoped the sisters in this
Stake would add two thousand more bush-
els this season to their grain. Urged the
sisters to use their surplus quilts in blessing
the poor and keeping them from suffering
with the cold. Said the more he became
acquainted with the organization the broad-
er and grander it was, prayed God to bless
us that we might enjoy this conference.
	President E. J. Bennett wished there had
been more present to hear the words of
President Steele and the spirit they were
given in, said we were making a record for
time and all eternity.
	Meeting then closed by singing “Lord
dismiss us.” Benediction.
	Afternoon session opened by singing
“The time is far spent.” Prayer was of-
fered by Patriarch O. Harmon. Singing,
“O, ye mountains high.” Minutes of the
afternoon session of the previous conference
were read and accepted, after which Sister
Kate Bennett was released as Stake treasur-
er of the Relief Society, and sustained and
set apart as assistant secretary, and Sister
Alice Boomer was released as assistant
treasurer, and sustained and set apart as
Stake treasurer to the Relief Society. Re-
ports were then called for and the Ammon
society was reported by President Richard-
son. She said meetings were not attended
so well of late on account of sickness. They
had built a granary, had 235 bushels of
grain.
	Counselor Dabell reported the Grant soci-
ety. Said they had paid out fifteen dollars
of late for charitable purposes.
	Labelle society was reported by Sister
Clifford, secretary; Rudy society, by Presi-
dent Clark.
	Emma C. Rushton, Stake counselor,
thought we had good reports in today,
but they could be improved. Noticed the
sisters who attended these meetings were
generally on hand, it was those that did not
attend to these duties that needed waking
up. Felt we had none too much time and
we should try and visit those who were
neglectful.
	Stake Secretary E. S. Brunt thought we
did not study sufficiently so as to be able to
instruct our children in the Gospel, let us
keep good books in the way of our children,
and not the light literature of to day. The
Lord desired to see our children saved and
He was sending fishers and hunters to
bring them in the path of their duty.
	President E. J. Bennett spoke of the
storing of grain, said we should store it for
a time of need and not to loan and specu-
late on, and the sisters should build good

[Column 2]
granaries and have them dedicated. Spoke
against raffling, instructed the teachers to
go around with a good spirit and have com-
forting words for the sisters. Recommended
lectures in our society from the Bible and
our Church works. Said that at our late
conference in Salt Lake City it was decided
that the 17th day of March should be the
annual day of the Relief Society, and ad-
vised the sisters to make it a good and
profitable day.
	The officers of the General Board, also
Stake officers were unanimously sustained.
Conference was then adjourned for three
months Singing, “An angel from on high.”
Benediction by Elder Benjamin Bennett.
				E. S. BRUNT, Sec.


[Column 1]
OBITUARY.

SKETCH OF MRS. C. K. PALMER.
	Catharine K. Palmer, wife of Mifflin L.
Palmer, passed away at 3:20 p. m., Janu-
ary 12, 1899. She was the daughter of
Abram and Catharine King Dolbey, and 
was born in Chester Co., Penn., on the 6th 
of April, 1817. She was the mother of
eight children, five of whom survive her;
they are Mrs. Rebecca W. Harper of Read-
ing, Penn., Mrs. Phoebe Shaffer and Mrs.
Eliza Frost of this city, Mr. A. Morris
Palmer of Taylorsville and Mrs. Bethula
Sharp of Vernon; and she was also the
mother of the late Mrs. Selinda D. Bring-
hurst, wife of Bishop W. A. Bringhurst of
Toquerville.
	Sister Palmer joined the Church of Jesus
Christ of Latter-day Saints about 1840, and 
with her husband and part of her family
came to Utah in 1861. She settled in the 
8th ward of Salt Lake City, and with the
exception of a few months has resided there
ever since. She joined the Relief Society
of that ward at its organization in 1866, and 
was appointed a teacher. Since then she
was treasurer and afterwards counselor, and
in 1893 she was appointed president of the
society, which office she held at the time of 
her death. She filled all these offices faith-

[Column 2]
fully and won the respect of all who knew
her.
	She was buried from the 8th ward meet-
ing house, which was beautifully draped
and trimmed with smilax, on Monday 16.
Her pall-bearers were six of her grandsons.
Every honor was paid her, the meeting
house was filled, the floral offerings were
very beautiful. One from the Relief Soci-
ety was a sheaf of wheat from Philadelphia
and a sickle.
	She was sick for many months and bore
her sufferings uncomplainingly. She knew
she could not recover and gave directions
concerning her funeral. She was a faith-
ful worker in the cause of truth and has
earned a rest from her labors of nearly
eighty-two years. She leaves many near
relatives in the Eastern states, one being a
twin sister.
							E. F.
LOUISA G. HENINGER.
	Sister Louisa G. Heninger was born in
Bland Co, Virginia, September 8, 1836.
She was the daughter of Francis Grose-
close and Mary I. Grosclose, that being her
mother’s maiden name also.
	Sister Louisa was of a gentle, mild and
patient disposition, exhibiting in her tender
years a veneration for God and sacred
things, possessing a prayerful heart and
desire to understand the principles taught
in the Scriptures of divine truth. With
such characteristics she was readily im-
pressed with the truth of the Gospel as re-
vealed to the Prophet Joseph Smith in these
last days. Elder Jedediah M. Grant intro-
duced the Gospel into that section in 1839
or 1840, and made many converts to the
truth Among them was Absalom Young
who became a very capable Elder in that
section of Virginia. Sister Louisa with
her father was baptized by Elder Absalom
Young, December 3, 1856, in Holsten river,
Bland Co., Virginia. She promptly secured
the spirit of the Gospel and took pleasure at
all times in hearing its principles expounded
and in doing all in her power to explain it
to others and remove prejudice from the
minds of the misinformed.
	She was married to James Preston Hen-
inger June 15, 1859, who also at an early
date had embraced the Gospel. They re-
mained in Virginia for some years and their
house was always the home of the Elders
from Zion. Sister Louisa and her husband
never felt that they could do enough to
make the servants of the Lord comfortable,
and to assist them in every way to prose-
cute their missionary labors.
	Their home was ever open to public
meetings when more capacious buildings
could not be procured. Many of the good
“Virginia folk” have listened at their
family fireside to the Gospel expounded by
Elders from Utah. Be it said to their credit
also, that they entertained the Baptist, the
Methodist, the Lutheran, and every class of
preacher that came to their neighborhood,
and gave them the best they had of the
comforts of life. By their sojourn in Vir-
ginia a permanent home was made for the
Elders for twenty-six years, during which
time many Elders found a welcome home
and many neighbors were brought to a
knowledge of the truth.
	Sister Heninger with her husband and
eight children and two children of her hus-
band’s by a former wife emigrated to Utah
in the month of November, 1881. They
settled in Eden, Weber Co., where they

[Column 3]
purchased a good farm and made them a
new home. They received their blessings
in the House of the Lord, and have been
constantly faithful to their duties. Brother
Heninger preceded his faithful wife to the
spirit world nine years and two months.
Sister Heninger was confined to her bed
three weeks, feeling sure that her time had
come and so stated to her family. Her only
desire to live was to work in the Temple
for her dead. Her son Harvey is now on a
mission to his native state, and is gathering
genealogical data. During her last illness
she was filled with the calm, quiet influence
of the Holy Spirit. Those who visited her
and administered unto her were all im-
pressed with the heavenly influence which
was with her.
	She passed peacefully to the world be-
yond without the sting of death, dying in
the Lord, November 24, 1898, surrounded
by all her children except one who is on a
mission to the Southern States. She was
the mother of eight children, two sons and
six daughters, every one of whom has em-
braced the Gospel and is living in the faith
thereof.
	At her funeral consoling remarks were
made by Bishop Pitkin of Millville, Cache
Co., and other Elders well acquainted with
the deceased. Her remains were laid quiet-
ly to rest in the Ogden cemetery, Sunday,
November 27, 1898. She died in the Lord,
she rests from her labors and her works do
follow her. Peace to her memory, she has
fought the good fight, kept the faith and
will receive a crown of eternal life. May
her children be worthy to meet her in the
morning of the First Resurrection.
						[COM.]
ELLA OLNEY HYDE.
	Ella Olney Hyde, wife of Ezra Hyde,
died at Hyde Park, Utah, January 10,
1899, after an illness of eight months. She
leaves a husband and one child, a father,
mother and other relatives to mourn her
loss.
						[COM.]


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


[Column 1]
COUNCIL OF WOMEN.

DATE OF MEETING AND TOPICS TO BE
DISCUSSED.
Members and Friends of the National
Council of Women of the United States:
	The Third Triennial of the National
Council will convene in Washington, D. C.,
February 13. The formal opening will be
preceded by a religious service on the
afternoon of February 12. A prefatory
business meeting will be held in the par-
lors of the Arlington Hotel on Satur-
day evening, February 11. Business
meetings will occupy the mornings of Mon-
day, February 13, and Saturday, February
18. The formal opening will occur on the
evening of Monday, February 13. The
mornings of the 14th-17th, inclusive, and
the evenings of the 13th-18th, inclusive,
will be occupied by public sessions. The
formal adjournment, which is fixed for the
evening of Saturday the 18th, will be fol-
lowed on the afternoon of Sunday the 19th
by a religious service. The meetings will be
held in the Universalist Church, corner of
Tenth and G Streets.
	The term now closing, like the one
which closed with the Triennial of 1895,
has covered a period of four years, instead
of three as contemplated by the constitu-
tion. These years have been fraught with
events the significance of which all men
and women, who, actuated by humanitarian
sentiments, are working in any line of re-
form, feel keenly.
	The program of the Triennial will include:
	First. Discussion and action upon inter-
ests and plans relating strictly to council
affairs.
	Second. Resumes of the achievements of
the closing term and presentation of the
work of the immediate future.
	Third. Discussion of great public inter-
ests from woman’s point of view, including
such topics as expansion, imperialism,
peace and international arbitration, the con-
dition of Hawaiian women, etc.
	One of the most interesting features of
the council’s work will be the presentation
of the progress of the council idea abroad,
and of plans now maturing for the second
quinquennial of the International Council
to be convened in London, England, June
27, 1899, where the National Council of
the United States must have an adequate
representation.
	Council headquarters will be the Arling-
ton Hotel. The railroad secretary (elected
by the executive board at Omaha,) Miss
Lucy E. Anthony, may be addressed at
1830 Diamond Street, Philadelphia, Pa.
	It is the earnest hope of the executive

[Column 2]
board of the National Council that the Third
Triennial will be conspicuous for the ade-
quate representation at its meetings of all
interests included within the council; for
careful preparation for the discussion of the
grave questions that confront all thought-
ful people living in these serious days, and
for the magnanimity and sympathy which
are the essence of the Council idea. May all
members and observers come to the Third
Triennial with the reverent desire to be led
by the light.
	On behalf of the executive:
		MAY WRIGHT SEWALL, Pres.
	633 North Pennslyvania St., Indianapolis,
Ind.
		LOUISA BARNUM ROBBINS, Cor. Sec.
	25 Broad St., Adrian, Michigan.

of every local suffrage association in the
State of New York should be sent to
Governor Theodore Roosevelt.” His address
is Hon. Theodore Roosevelt, Executive
Mansion, Albany, N. Y.—Ex.
	Governor Pingree of Michigan made this
recommendation in his message to the
Legislature: “I earnestly recommend that
such legislation be enacted as may be
necessary, requiring the appointment of at
least one woman on the advisory board,
and of at least one woman physician upon
the staff of each State institution, charitable
or corrective, having women or girls as in-
mates.”
