[Column 1]
KHARTOUM AND SIR HORATIO
HERBERT KITCHENER

	Away in far off Africa is a vast stretch of
country known as “The Soudan.” It lies en-
tirely within the tropics and reaches right
across the continent from the Atlantic Ocean
to the Red Sea. While there are, of course,
imaginary limits north and south, its actual
boundaries so far have not been really fixed.
	A large portion of Africa has come under
the “spheres of influence” of different
European nations. By sphere of influence
is meant that “large areas of land in Africa
are recognized as under the control of
European powers. * * * It designates the
region that may be occupied and developed
by the power for which it is named.”
	This great region of country, the Sou-
dan, is largely inhabited, not only by its
native tribes of the negro race but by wild
tribes of fanatical Arabs. For many years
a civilizing influence was brought to bear
upon this wild country, and at last in 1881
Soudan was under a civilized government.
To be sure there were many imperfections
in the administration of affairs, as of neces-
sity there must be under the circumstances.
But trade flourished. Khartoum, the capi-
tal, occupied an important position at the
junction of the White and the Blue Nile.
It became a business center for European
as well as Egyptian commercial firms.
Trade was facilitated by steamers plying
the river. Communication with the outer
world was maintained by post and by tele-
graph, and just when all seemed going well
a war cloud gathered and burst, ravaging
the country and spreading devastation.
	General Gordon, the famous English
soldier, had been Governor-General of the
Soudan for two years, 1877-1879, and dur-
ing that time he had stamped out the slave
trade. As a consequence the slave dealers
felt oppressed. In 1880 Gordon left the
Soudan to act as adviser of the Chinese
government in its relations with Russia.
It was during his absence the strife broke
out.
	We must now for a moment turn our
attention to Egypt. In 1876 the Khedive
of Egypt who had been working to get his
country conducted on modern principles,
found himself practically a bankrupt. He
had to have help from foreign nations.
French and English capitalists loaned large
sums of money, and England and France
sent experts to try and save something and
help “carry on the country.” In 1878 an
Englishman and a Frenchman were ap-
pointed to high offices in the government.
In April of the same year they were dis-
missed by the Khedive. Two months
later the Khedive was deposed by the

[Column 2]
Turkish government, and France and Eng-
land were again represented in the govern-
ment. “In 1882 Arabi Bey fomented an
uprising, the object of which was to restore
Egypt to the Egyptians.” With the help
of the British fleet and the British army they
were successful. “Then France refused to
take any part in carrying on the govern-
ment, so it devolved upon Great Britain.”
And thus France lost her hold upon Egypt.
	It was during this civil strife in Egypt
that the revolt began in the Soudan, July
1881, led by Mohammed Ahmed. The
Mohammedans are represented as being
superstitious, bigoted and fanatical. They
have long looked forward to a Mahdi, the
literal meaning of which is “guided or
directed one.” “According to Mohamme-
dan belief a spiritual and temporal ruler des-
tined to appear on the earth during the last
days. Some sects hold that the Mahdi has
appeared and in concealment awaits the
time of his manifestation. There have
been a number of pretended Mahdis, of
whom the last of importance was this
Mohammed Ahmed.”
	He was a man of the people, of obscure
birth or origin, but was, or pretended to be,
a descendent of the great prophet. He is
described as a great student with probably
a natural religious bent and possessed of in-
tense cunning. So it is “not difficult to im-
agine how easily he drifted into the lead of
one of the most fanatical movements, to
which Easterners are so prone.” This belief
of the Mohammedans is compared to the
Jewish belief in the coming of the Messiah.
	When the people of the Soudan were left
to themselves the enforced good behavior of
the rulers changed and the people felt op-
pressed and unjustly dealt with. The slave
traders were glad to be rid of Gordon’s rule.
The time was ripe for a revolt and a
leader awaiting his opportunity. 	
	This is the story of the beginning of the
siege by the Mahdi. The people inspired
by religious fervor or fanaticism fought as
only such people can. Attempt after at-
tempt was made to crush this revolt, but
the Mahdi came off ever victorious. His
victories inspired the Soudanese with the
greatest enthusiasm and religious zeal, and
they believed they had indeed the expected
Mahdi. The British did what they could
to help the Egyptians, but the flood was
not checked. Gordon had reached Khar-
toum with a command, but by the fall of
Beehu he was completely shut in. He re-
mained at his post hoping against hope, un-
able to accomplish his heart’s desire to
crush the Mahdi. In England the people
were roused to fever heat and a relief ex-
pedition was sent to Gordon. This expedi-
tion had to fight the most bitter fights to get
to its destination, and arrived, alas, just too
late! Gordon had been most foully slain,
and England had to mourn the loss of a
great leader and many brave men. Gor-
don’s personal characteristics made him one
of the celebrities of the world. His death
occurred January 26, 1885. A statue to
him has been erected in Trafelgar square,
London. Khartoum, of course, fell before
the enemy, the city being destroyed.


[Column 1]
	“Although the Mahdi himself died in
June 1885, the movement he had originated
suffered no check. * * * By the end of
that year Mahdism reigned supreme
throughout the Soudan.” And even the
southern part of Egypt fell under that
power. But soon dissensions arose among
the Arabs. The Khalifa, successor to the
Mahdi, was a western Arab and treated the
easterners with cruelty. Things continued
in a turmoil until the spring of 1898, when
General Sir Horatio Herbert Kitchener having.
been put in command, he at the head of a
powerful army composed of British soldiers
and troops of the Khedive, completely
overcame and subdued the fanatical rebels.
His victory was a most brilliant one and
caused the greatest rejoicing throughout the
British Empire, and indeed throughout the
civilized world.
	Like many another brave British leader
Kitchener is an Irishman by birth. It is
said that “he is a soldier by inheritance, by
training and by instinct.” He is forty-
eight years of age, tall, measuring full six
feet, and with the swarthy complexion that
would be but natural after years of exposure
to the tropical sun. Most of his service
has been in the far East, and he has ac-
quired a remarkable knowledge of Moham-
medan languages and tribes. This knowl-
edge has been of great service to him at
times. The following story is told as an
instance:
	“Two Arab date sellers were arrested as
suspected spies and confined in the guard
tent. Shortly after a third Arab prisoner
was hastily bundled into the tent. An
animated jabbering ensured among the
three and in a few minutes, much to the
astonishment, of the sentry, the latest ar-
rival drew aside the doorway and stepped
out, remarking, ‘All right, sentry, I’m
going to the General.’ It was Kitchener.
Again only a few minutes passed when an
orderly hurried up and a spade was handed
to the two Arabs, who were marched out-
side the lines, ordered to dig their graves,
and were shot. They were spies, and
Kitchener had detected them.”
	The glory of Kitchener’s successful and
splendid expedition consisted as much in
the way the soldiers were cared for under
adverse circumstances, as in the wonderful
fighting.
	Oudurman, a little north of Khartoum, is
the successor to that fallen city. General
Gordon expressed a preference for having
the capital at Oudurman, or even a little
farther north on the right bank of the Nile,
as the location of Khartoum was very un-
healthy and the cost of making it healthy
would be very great. It was at Oudurman
that the Khalefa’s army met with such ter-
rible slaughter. His men came bravely
forth to meet Kitchener’s Anglo-Egyptian
troops, but owing to the machine guns of
the English the massacre, was great. The
number of men of the Khalifa left dead on
the field has been variously estimated at
from five to twenty thousand.
	In the earlier stages of Kitchener’s ex-
pedition it was seriously feared that the
French were preparing to cut across from
their holdings in western Soudan to a point
on the Nile above Khartoum, at or about
Fashoda, for the sake of preventing the
English from going any further. “The
seriousness of such a proposition can only
be understood when one remembers the
peculiar nature of the Nile. If the French
were in full possession even at so remote a
point as Fashoda, they could at any time

[Column 2]
they chose so divert the water as to ruin
the whole of Egypt, which owes its life to
the periodical overflow and to irrigation.”
	This Fashoda affair threatened strained
relations for a time between England and
France. But the French now disclaim it
as anything but a private exploring party,
that there was no disposition in any quarter
to oppose the further advance of the Eng-
lish. And it is further stated that when
Kitchener arrived at Fashoda he found
Major Marchand there with only seven or
eight white men and a handful of native
Africans.
	It has been estimated that the victory of
the United States over Spain resulted in the
material improvement of 8,000,000 people in
the Phillipines, and 2,000,000 or more in
the West Indies.
	Kitchener’s splendid expedition brought
even greater succor and blessings to the
million of human beings in Eastern Soudan.
The fanatical movement of the Mahdi’s
resulted in the absolute closing up of one of
the most prosperous regions of the whole
African continent, a region at that time oc-
cupied by no less than 12,000,000 people.
This population was reduced to about one
half, agriculture and trade destroyed and
the country desolated. Happier and more
prosperous times may now be looked for.
There seems to be nothing in the Way
now of Cecil Rhodes great project of build-
ing a through railroad from Capetown to
Cairo.
	Kitchener is unmarried and will have no
married officers under him. He is a strict
disciplinarian, of impassive countenance.
And while his manners do not win the love
of his men, he has their profoundest respect
and sincerest admiration. He is described
as quiet but clever, and perhaps his severe
life and strict necessities have made him
apparently cold and stern. At all events
he is a great general and the pride of the
British people.
	After the victory in the Soudan, Kitchen-
er visited England accompanied by some of
his English and Egyptian officers. They
were received with unbounded enthusiasm,
being banqueted and toasted and in a
general way made to feel the great esteem
and appreciation in which they were held.
						E. S. W.


NIGHT WINDS.

The cold night wind is moaning
	And sighing amid the trees;
It flies o'er the wooded highland
	And sinks o’er the downy leas.
It catches the sails of the vessel
	That ploughs through the ocean wide;
And wafts its streamers landward
	With the strong and rolling tide.
But O, how it sobs at the window,
	And wails as it rushes by;
Like cries of the souls of the lost ones,
	Who trembled and feared to die.
Far away in the dreary distance
	I hear it sighing still,
And my heart but echoes t[-]e sadness
	That nothing of heart can fill.
And now it groans like the living
	When life’s faint spark has fled;
As they bow in deepest sorrow
	Around the newly dead.
And now it sounds like music
	From a harp of a thousand strings;
And then like sweetest melody
	That earthly chorus sings.
Now it sounds like a living thing
	That is haunted and full of pain;
And then it weeps for the doomed ones,
	Whose sins arise again.
And then it laughs in derision,
	And mocks at human woe;
Then rushing it reaches the mountains,
	Then over to valleys below.
And now like a saddening memory
	It calls up the troubled Past;
A harvest of hopes that were blighted,
	Too radiant and sunny to last.
Now again it cries like an infant,
	And now is mad with glee;—
O, God save the father fishermen
	Far out on the stormy sea.
This wind is playing with heartstrings,
	As it roams abroad tonight;
O, pray that the storm-tossed mariner
	May see the morning’s light.
Down by the open fireplace
	Sits Fear, so pallid and cold;
While the wind is rattling the window,
	In its dance of the demons bold.
But O, when it sobs like the human
	I think my heart will break!
Then out in the deepest darkness
	Steals a prayer for the wanderer's sake.
Again it sounds like an army
	Marching and tramping along;
Now the thunder of heavy cannon,
	Is heard with the victor’s song.


Then over the hills in the distance 
	The sound is lost and gone;
While brightening far to the eastward 
	Comes the morning pale and wan.
Now its moans are fast receding,
	As it softly goes to sleep;
In the rock-bound, foaming caveens
	Of the mighty ocean’s deep.
				LYDIA D. ALDER

WOMAN’S EXPONENT


[Column 1]
SISTER HORNE’S BIRTHDAY.
	Sunday, November 20, the 80th birth-
day of our beloved and respected Stake
president, Sister M. I. Horne. The day
is quite emblematic—the snow coming
down so pure and white is like what her
grand life has been. To me it was very
pleasing to see so large a congregation as-
sembled at the Assembly Hall yesterday
to celebrate the birth and do honor to one
who has done so much good, and to listen
to the remarks made by the different
speakers. 
	It will be forty-four years next month
since I first met Sister Horne in company
at the home of President John Taylor’s
father, many notable persons being present;
but when introduced to Sister Horne I was
forcibly struck with her motherly appear-
ance and kindly countenance.
	Having lived within a block from her
from that time until she moved from
the Fourteenth ward, I have had ample
opportunity to become acquainted with her.
To me she has been a model woman,
thoroughly domesticated, but modest and

[Column 2]
unassuming. I became identified with the
Relief Society at its reorganization after the
move South, Sister Horne being chosen
president, having previously been coun-
selor to Sister Phebe W. Woodruff. I well
remember how unassuming and gentle she
always seemed in conducting the affairs of
the society, yet firm in all her ways, but
never any arrogance so characteristic of
some. I do not forget how she would in-
tercede with the sisters to do their duty and
laid plans, and persevered in having the
society hall built there; that being com-
pleted paid for and dedicated, how she again
planned for the building of a granary.
President Young counseled each society to
build a hall and a granary as soon as prac-
tiable. She did not procrastinate but
called on the brethren to assist and they re-
sponded. That being accomplished, paid
for and dedicated, she then instructed the
society teachers each fall to gather donations
for wheat; and the motherly care she be-
stowed over that grain!
	I well remember on one occasion in talk-
ing to the sisters having so much to accom-
plish how she plead with them saying,
“Sisters, this is not my society, but our
society.” That went right home. I felt
that I was willing to do any thing in my
power to assist that noble woman; and
many more reminiscences I might mention.
Her family is an example to the community.
What a Heavenly blessing! but that wo-
man honored her husband, and her husband
honored her. I have always felt that this
was the secret of their success. While
raising her family she was content to stay
at home and attend to her first duty, and
not trust those God-given treasures to the
care of others. And now, behold the second
mission she has filled.
	My young sisters, let us emulate her ex-
ample, and let us lead our children to be-
gin the right way, and then continue doing
so. Let us teach our sons to honor their
wives, our daughters to honor their hus-
bands, that peace and content may abide in
the homes of the Saints.
	Oh, what a mission there is for each and
all of us if we will but fill it! May heaven’s
richest blessings rest upon Sister Horne and
all apertaining to her and may the
mementoes presented to her yesterday be-
token her sisters’ good feelings and be a
comfort to her; and may she live in peace
and happiness and continue her labors of
love until she is satisfied with life, is the
sincere desire of one who loves to honor her
and desires to testify to her goodness, hav-
ing known and labored with her so many
years.
						E. J. S.


A German biologist has calculated that
the human brain contains 300,000,000 nerve
cells, 5,000,000 of which die and are suc-
ceeded by new ones every day. At this
rate, assuming the correctness of the Ger-
man’s guess, we get an entirely new brain
every sixty days.—Medical Record.
	The statement by some of the daily press
that the Daughters of the Revolution ad-
mit collateral descendants of revolutionary
patriots is effectually disposed of by the
following extract from their constitution:
	Article III., Section I.—Qualifications.
Any woman shall be eligible to member-
ship in the Daughters of the Revolution
who is above the age of eighteen years, of
good character, and a lineal descendant of
an ancestor, who, etc.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


[Column 1]
VISIT TO CALIFORNIA.

Editor Woman’s Exponent:
	The souls of the Saints, especially the
sisters of the Relief Society throughout this
mission, have been made to rejoice by
reason of the fact that we have received a
visit from Sister Jane S. Richards of Ogden.
During the latter part of December Sister
Richards accompanied her son, Hon. C. C.
Richards, and wife who were seeking health
and rest and came to Los Angeles. While
there Sister Richards visited the Relief
Society of the branch of the Saints of that
city and had an opportunity of seeing a lit-
tle how they were getting along, and of in-
structing them along the line of Relief
Society work as it is carried on in Zion.
	We have a good society in Los Angeles.
Sister Woollacott, now visiting her daugh-
ter in Salt Lake City, is the president and
she has a good company of officers and
members considering the size and strength
of the branch. They are devoted to the
work of the Lord and are faithful workers
in the society. The president is a good
leader among women and by her wise and
careful management and the hearty support
rendered by the society they have always
got money in the treasury with which to
assist the needy. Sister Richards did much
to encourage and strengthen them in their
noble work.
	About the 24th of January President
Franklin D. Richards honored us by a
visit to this mission and arrived in San
Diego, Sister Richards having reached there
a few days before. On the 26th they to-
gether attended the Relief Society meeting
which convened in the house of Brother
Charles H. Hoag, the branch president,
and though they were few in number, yet
when President Jewel had conducted the
opening services and Sister Richards pro-
ceeded in that wondrously kind and plea-
sant way so peculiar to her to tell them of
the trials and afflictions through which the
Saints had to pass in the days of the Prophet
Joseph and the early days of Utah, of the
blessings of the Lord that had been show-
ered down upon them as a reward for their
obedience and devotion, and of the present
standard of usefulness they have attained
through their untiring labors in the great
work, the sisters listened with rapt atten-
tion the while, the love of God enkindled
in their hearts, filling their souls with light
and joy and causing a sense of deep appre-
ciation of the Gospel to fill them such as
they had never before experienced. The
spirit of the Almighty accompanied the
words of dear Sister Richards in a most re-
markable way as she dwelt upon the earlier
scenes of the Church. President Richards
spoke words of comfort and blessing to all.
	On the Sabbath besides attending regular
services in the hall Sister Richards met with
the Primary Association at the home of
Sister Gabrelson, and there after the exer-
cises of the little ones, which were indeed
most creditable both to the children and to
Sister Buehorn who has charge of them,
she talked to these little ones until she won
a place in their dear little hearts that she
will retain while they live.
	After spending about ten days in that re-
nowned but greatly over estimated health
resort, San Diego, the party consisting of
President F. D. Richards, Sister Jane S.
Richards and their son Charles and wife re-
turned to Los Angeles, the latter on their
way to Utah, while the president and wife

[Column 2]
remained and attended the meetings of the
Saints on the Sabbath at 245 1/2 South Spring
Street. This branch through the untiring
efforts of the Elders who have labored here
and the blessings of the Lord has grown
and increased in numbers and is our strong-
est branch in the mission, numbering about
one hundred and thirty members. There
is a good Relief Society, Mutual Improve-
ment Association and Sabbath School, all
in splendid condition.
	Brother and Sister Richards attended the
Sabbath School and the meeting that fol-
lowed, it being fast day, and mingled their
testimonies with those of the Saints, and
their soul-inspiring words were as precious
gems to the Saints, many of whom had
never before listened to the sound of the
voice of a living Apostle.
	When the visit at Los Angeles was ended
the party took train for San Francisco, and
the headquarters of the California mission
became the resting place of our beloved
Sister Jane S. Richards and her husband.
Be assured, dear editor, we all regarded
ourselves as highly honored in having the
privilege of entertaining such distinguished
persons as an Apostle of the Lord and his
precious life companion. This may sound
somewhat strange to some of the readers of
the “Exponent,” but if they had to spend
their time among the wicked and ungodly
in the midst of a mess of man made re-
ligions, the principal elements of which are
doubt and uncertainty, they would learn,
perhaps, as we have, to appreciate the
privilege of beholding a servant of the true
and living God, and of listening to the
words of life and salvation from his mouth.
	The Relief Society of the branch in this
city, of which Sister Rattenbery is the
president, convened on Thursday at 3 p. m.
at the mission house, and after some local
matters had been attended to Sister Rich-
ards addressed the meeting, and I would
that we could give a verbatim report of her
speech and that of President Richards who
followed, if for no other purpose than that
we might have them to refer to here, for
they sparkled with gems of thought crystal-
ized into words which as they fell upon the
ear caused the souls of all that heard them
to thrill with joy.
	A surprise party was projected by the
sisters so that the president and his wife
could see how the San Francisco Saints con-
ducted such gatherings. On Thursday
evening about 8 p. m. the mission house
was besieged by the attacking party which
soon gained an entrance and filled the house
to its utmost capacity. Over a hundred
were present. A program of songs, reci-
tations, etc., and then a speech from Presi-
dent Richards, smiling faces, happy hearts
and love abounded.
	Brother and Sister Richards visited the
homes of some of the Saints, Sister Collins
of this city, Sister Sumner of Oakland, and
then took their departure for Sacramento
where on Sunday the 12th they met with
the Sabbath School and forenoon meeting,
and then at 3 p m. with the Relief Society
at the home of Sister Smith, 405 15th St.
Sister Fanny H. Thackham, the president,
in an appropriate manner welcomed the
visitors from Zion. The sisters all testified
to their love of the Gospel, and of their de-
termination to remain steadfast to the end.
Sister Richards strewed pearls of pricely
value in her speech, among them such as
will be remembered by all who were pres-
ent for many a day, while President Rich-
ards spoke forth words of encouragement

[Column 3]
and blessing. In the evening at our regu-
lar hall President Richards was the speak-
er and he delivered a good sermon to Saints
and investigators who had gathered in to
hear the word of God. On the day follow-
ing President and Sister Richards took their
leave for their home in Ogden.
	We in this land of sunshine and flowers,
of drouth and earthquake, of Christian
churches and infidel congregations, of
gigantic ideas and small souled Christian
ministers, deeply appreciate a visit from the
tried and trusted leaders of the true Church
of Christ.
				HATTIE M. NYE.


HISTORY OF  HE S[-]LK WORM.

'Twas the 13th of May in 1898,
We are quite sure about the date,
For on that day the first worms hatched,
And on the 14th the second batch.
On the 15th no worms did gladden our sight[-]
But on the 16th a fine lot came out all right;
On the 17th only a very small lot,
But on the 18th they made it hot.
On the 19th of May our hatch was ended,
And with hopes and fears continually blended
Lest they should be too hot or too cold,
Our trials and troubles can scarce be told.
We were up every morn when the clock struck
	five,
For an early feed would make them thrive,
'Twas gather leaves here, and gather leaves
	there,
With never a moment to sit on a chair
At first on one row they nicely lay,
But on one row they would not stay,
But quickly spread to a dozen or more,
While our labors were increased fourscore.
But just when we thought we couldn't live,
For the constant cry of the worms was Give
Oh give me leaves, or you will see,
Not an ounce of silk will we make for thee.
On the 27th of June, on the end of a shelf,
We found what to us was better than pelf,
'Twas a single fibre, or silken thread,
This told the tale and ended the dread,
That had entered our souls, our work was
	done.
No longer now from sun to sun,
Must we gather leaves, but down on a chair
We can quietly sit, and stare and stare,
At the worms while they hide themselves from
	sight,
Rolled up in a ball so snug and tight.
This ends the story of the silk worm's life,
Composed of hope and fear, joy and strife.
M. V. N. HALL.


New and hygienically constructed tele-
phone stations have been provided in Vien-
na, and with them has come a reform in
the dress of the telephone girl. Experience
has proved that the dust lodging on the
clothes of the girls gets into the instruments
and interferes with the effectiveness of the
service. To avoid this the girls are now
required to wear a uniform during office
hours. On their arrival they doff their
street clothes, even their boots, and substi-
tute dresses provided by the government.
The dresses are made of stuff like that most
used for dust cloaks. They are dark in
color, and on the sleeves are yellow stripes,
in imitation of the national colors. Chicago
has adopted the same method to reduce
dust in the operating rooms.

SALT LAKE CITY, FEB. 15 & MAR. 1, 1899.


OFFICIAL NOTICE.

	At a special meeting of the Board of
Directors of the National Woman’s Relief
Society held March 7, in room 208 Temple-
ton building, (more than the required num-
ber for the transaction of business being
present), when the subject of ways and
means was being considered, a motion was
made by Mrs. Martha A. Cannon of Ogden
and seconded by Dr. Ellis R. Shipp of
Salt Lake City, that all money raised by
local branches of the Relief Society on the
17th of March over the ten cent member-
ship of each member enrolled in the society,
should be retained by the respective society.
Motion was put by President Zina D. H.
Young and carried unanimously,
	(Signed) ZINA D. H. YOUNG, President.
EMMELINE B WELLS Secretary.
	National Woman's Relief Society.


[Column 1]
THE TRIENNIAL.

SOME OF THE WOMEN, SUBJECTS,
DISCUSSIONS AND REPORTS.

	The women from Utah who went to
Washington in February to attend the
Triennial sessions of the National Council
of Women of the United States, have had
an interesting experience to say the least,
especially those who went as presidents
(proxies,) delegates and members of the
Resolution Committee. As every one knows
who has been connected with such great
national bodies, the hard work and really
most important is done in committees.
	Speech making is comparatively easy, more
especially so when prepared beforehand,
which the addresses certainly should be on
any given subject, and not written at odd
moments or on the way as some very busy
people are apt to do in the hurry that
comes from overwork. If one accustomed
to public duty might be permitted to sug-
gest the advice would be to take the ut-
most pains with the papers to be given as ad-
dresses to an audience of cultured people.
Random shots come in occasionally, but it is
the steady, deliberate firing that accom-
plishes the objects seriously undertaken.
	The Resolution Committee consisted of
one member from each organization belong-
ing to the Council and numbered twenty-
five persons, representing various shades of
opinion on many vital subjects, Rev. Anna
Garlin Spencer, Providence, R. I., chair-
man; Ann M. Cannon, Salt Lake City,
Utah, secretary.

[Column 2]
	The stormy weather was a hindrance to
the members arriving in time, although
eight of the Utah ladies arrived in time to
attend the business session Saturday eve-
ning, the 11th. The following day there
was a blinding snow storm which kept
pedestrians within doors and very few car-
riages appeared on the streets. The papers
published the names of all the ladies who
attended church, the weather was so in-
clement. Snow continued to fall during
the night and the wind increased in violence
and on Monday morning there were only a
few to attend the opening of the Council.
Business of importance, however, was
transacted and lunch served in the rooms
in the church at a nominal price. The
storm had been continuous thro’ the day
and the wind blew fiercely, and when the
time for adjournment came the query was
how to get to hotels or boarding places.
Carriages with drivers that dared venture
out were taken by those who could get to
them first, and some living only a short dis-
tance ventured to walk at great risk. Such
adventures as were told in the papers after-
wards had never before transpired in Wash-
ington. On the streets men with handker-
chiefs over their faces were buffeting the
snow and wind, their hats flying hither and
thither.
	Some of the subjects considered and
papers read were "Christianity as Related to
Sociology," dealing with the practical side of
religion, by Mary Perkins Davis, Ocean
Park, Maine. “The Possibilities of Wo-
man,” by Martha Horne Tingey of Salt
Lake City, Utah, a paper which showed
earnest thought and advanced ideas of life.
“The Social Conscience, in its Relation to
Industrial Problems,” by Mrs. Maud Nath-
an of New York City. It is one of the vital
questions of the present day and the speak-
er showed earnest feelings in her dealings
with the matter in question, and that she
was thoroughly acquainted with industrial
conditions, and dealt eloquently and heroic-
ally with actual facts. “The Tree of
Knowledge of Good and Evil," by Miss
Sadie American of Chicago was perhaps the
most unique as a whole of any paper pre-
sented. Miss American is very original in
her work and deals mainly with actual facts
in a positive manner. "The Retrogression
in Equal Rights,” by Susan B. Anthony,
who never fails to make strong points in
the most forcible manner, and who is al-
ways in touch with her audience and never
fails to create enthusiasm whatever views
people may entertain as to the subject mat-
ter of her address. “The Beginning of
Literature and Art in America,” by Mrs.
Maria Purdy Peck of Dubuque, Iowa, was
a charming address on a delightful theme.
St. Hilda of Whitby—“How Abbesses
were Educators and Civilizers,” by Mary
Newbury Adams, was one of the most
delightful and soulful papers given, the
speaker herself, a most charming woman;
but one hesitates to particularize where there
were so many capable, cultivated, intellec-
tual women. "Good Citizenship," by Mrs.
E. B. Wells of Utah. “Is the Church
Universal a Hindrance or a Help to the
Development of Womankind?” by Eliza-
beth B. Grannis of New York.
	The papers for discussion were of very
great interest, especially upon vital ques-
tions. One of the subjects was “Is Ex-
pansion for the Best Good of our Country?"
Affirmative, Judith Ellen Foster, District of
Columbia; Negative, Cornelia S. Robinson,
New York. The same evening “The Wo-

[Column 3]
men of Hawaii,” by Susa Young Gates, a
very ably written and exhaustive paper on
the conditions, customs and ethics of the
natives, and some expression of real life by
Hannah Kaepapa, the pretty custom of
the laie was given in an object lesson on
the platform by seven very handsome
wreaths being presented to the ladies of the
general officers by Mrs. Gates and the
native lady. Mrs. Sewall, the president,
Dr. Shaw, the vice president, and the
other officers gracefully acknowledging the
courtesy by wearing them the entire eve-
ning. “Equality, the most Potent Conserv-
ator of Human Rights,” by Ida M. Weaver
of Idaho. "Fraternal Life Benefit Societies
as Social Institutions,” Bina M. West,
Michigan.
	There was also a Symposium on "What
should be the Attitude of the Women of
the United States toward the Peace Confer-
ence proposed by the Czar?" This awak-
ened universal interest and was eloquently
emphasized and elaborated by Rev. Anna
Garlin Spencer of R. I., Carrie Thomas,
Alexander, Ill., Kate Waller Barrett, D.
C., and Rev. Anna Howard Shaw, Penn.
“The Relation Between the Council and
Peace and Arbitration," by Rev. Amanda
Deyo, Penn., was one of the most interest-
ing in the general discussion. “How can
Women Aid in Municipal Reform?”
These discussions were all very educational
and carried on sympathetically, each speak-
er having a certain number of minutes.
	Reports of organizations were also of
great interest and much appreciated, as
they showed the growth and progress of the
various associations during the period since
the last Triennial in 1895. These were
from the following societies: National
Free Baptist Woman’s Missionary Society,
Mary G. Osgood, New Hampshire: Uni-
versal Peace Union, Rev. Amanda Deyo;
Working Woman’s Home Association Dr.
Mary E. Green, Michigan; The Woman’s
National Press Association, Ellen S. Crom-
well, D. C.; Florence Crittenden Missions,
Kate Waller Barrett, D. C.; National Wo-
man’s Relief Society, Zina Young Card,
Utah; Wimodaughsis, Ada G. Dickerson,
D.C.; National Christian League for the
Promotion of Social Purity, Elizabeth
B Grannis, New York; National Woman’s
Christian Temperance Union, Lillian M.
N. Ste ens, Maine; Supreme Hive Ladies of
the Maccabees of the World, Lillian M.
Hollister, Michigan; Rathbone Sisters of
the World, Ida M. Weaver, Idaho; Nation-
al Mutual Improvement Association, Min-
nie J. Snow, Utah; National American
Woman’s Suffrage Association, Rachel
Foster Avery; National Council of Jewish
Women, Hannah G. Solomon, Ill.; Nation-
al Association of Women Stenographers,
Mrs. Maddox; Anti Vivisection Society,
Mrs. Lovell, Quincy, Ill.
	There were reports from local councils
and state councils affiliated, and fraternal
delegates introduced from organizations and
councils not yet affiliated; also reports of
standing committees which were quite as
interesting as those of society work. Com-
mittees on Education in Citizenship, by
Fannie Humphreys Gaffney, New York,
and Press Committee, Susa Young Gates,
Utah. On Social Peace and International
Arbitration, Hannah G. Solomon, Ill.
	The ideas, aims and grasp of knowledge
embodied in these addresses and reports and
the discussious which intervened, the in-
formation and instruction given, and the
educational facilities afforded, are far great-


er than one can possibly imagine who has
not been present in person, with mind and
soul susceptible to the uplifting influences
that flow from high intellectuality and in-
telligence of a superior order. The value
of the National Council cannot be estimated,
the movement of this great body of women
of diverse views and opinions, and a great
variety of dissimilarity of tastes and char-
acteristics coming together on the momen-
tous questions of the day and agreeing to
disagree, is an object lesson for the world.
	The letter of Mrs. Mary Lowe Dickinson,
the president during this last Triennial un-
til forced by her illness and the protestations
of her physician to resign, was one of the
striking features of the session, in its pathos
speaking heart to heart, and its expressions
of what the Council would have been to
her had she not from force of circumstances
been obliged to withdraw from the position.
	The address of the retiring president Mrs.
May Wright Sewall, bristled with original
ideas and suggestions, as also did her “Rec-
ommendations to the Cabinet.” One might
write columns upon this subject, but as it
will be published in full for circulation
among members of organizations belonging
to the Council, all who wish will have an
opportunity to study its contents.
	The resolutions adopted, which were the
chief work of its committee, and the plans
laid out for the future furtherance of the
Council's work during the next three years
we must leave for the next issue of the
paper, also much that is interesting in mat-
ters not practically essential to the Council
itself, as well as the blockades, blizzards,
delayed trains, and minor items.
	The two living presidents who have filled
terms of office were made Honorary Presi-
dents with a vote. They are Mary Lowe
Dickinson and May Wright Sewall.
	The new officers elected were, Prest.,
Fannie Humphreys of New York City;
Vice Prest., Maria Purdy Peck, Dubu-
que, Iowa; Cor. Sec., Mrs Kate Waller
Barrett, Washington, D. C.; Rec. Sec.,
Rev. Anna Garlin Spencer, R. I.; Asst.
Rec. Sec., Emmeline B. Wells, Salt Lake
City, Utah; Treasurer, Hannah G. Solo-
mon, Chicago.
	Members of the Cabinet are not yet ap-
pointed, nor any new standing committees.
The idea of grouping the societies under
certain departments has been advanced
and may be acted upon. The next busi-
ness session will be held in November of
the present year at Indianapolis, Indiana.
	Meantime the International Council in
London will hold its Quinquennial, and the
National Council of the United States will
have its quota of representation Mrs. May
Wright Sewall, the late president of the
Council will be installed as president of
the International Council. Miss Susan B.
Anthony and Dr. Anna H. Shaw are the
delegates from the National Council.


WOMAN'S EXPONENT. 109


EDITORIAL NOTES.

	The Stake secretaries of the Relief Soci-
ety in all the several Stakes of Zion and of
missions or branches of the Relief Society
where Stakes are not organized, are hereby
requested to send statistical and financial re-
ports for the entire year of 1898, to the
general secretary, room 208 Templeton
building, Salt Lake City, Utah. 
		E. B. WELLS, General Secretary.

	The Utah Legislature is drawing to a


close, and much of the valuable time has
been frittered away in voting for a Senator
to little or no purpose. It is very undigni-
fied and unsatisfactory to the people who
elected them to waste so much time in
complimentary and uncomplimentary speech
making, and delay serious matters which
should have been acted upon.
	President Jane S. Richards who recently
visited California gives a most interesting
account of the branches of the ReliefSociety
in that state. She speaks in the highest
terms of praise of the untiring efforts of the
sisters in their labors of love and the good
spirit enjoyed by them, in which she par-
ticipated. The pleasure they expressed in
her being present and speaking encourag-
ingly to them in their society meetings is
deeply felt and warmly appreciated by Sis-
ter Richards, whose love for the work of
the Relief Society stimulates her to under-
take much more than her delicate health
would warrant. To the writer in the office
of the “Exponent” she expressed the most
loving tenderness, interest and sympathy in
all the sisters in these branehes, and it is her
ardent desire to have them feel their good
works are appreciated in Zion.
	We must express our great satisfaction in
meeting with many dear old friends while
absent in the East, and among others Mrs.
Elizabeth Cady Stanton, who is more
beautiful than any picture of her could be
made, for she shows the eloquent beauty of
the soul within, and her soft white hair in
the loose puffs is like a halo above her
sweet, tender, motherly face. She con-
verses as pleasantly as ever and is as bril-
liant and witty as in her palmiest days.
We shall never forget in time or eternity
how grandly Mrs. Stanton and Miss Anth-
ony received the first Utah delegates, (Mrs.
Wells aud Mrs, Williams) in the Conven-
tion at Washington in January, 1879, and
we shall always honor these two noble wo-
men, the grandest types of American
womanhood. 
	Another dear old friend who plead our
cause most eloquently in those days long
ago, and whom we always make a point of
calling upon when we go to Washington,
D. C., is Mrs. Sara Andrews Spencer who
should never be forgotten by the Latter-
day Saints; she has been a staunch friend,
and manifested her valor in the hour of
greatest need, and when strong men were
less courageous in defense of the right.


REORGANIZATION.
	Minutes of the annual meeting of the 8th
ward Relief Society held February 4th,
1899, Vice President S. J. Chamberlain
presiding. The secretary called the roll,
after which the vice president stated that
the business of the meeting was to hear the
annual reports and the election of officers,
(on account of the death of the president,
Kathrine K. Palmer) for the ensuing two
years. The reports were read and approved.
Sarah J. Chamberlain was elected president;
Ann Cartwright, 1st vice president; Martha
A. Shelmerdine, 2nd vice president; Abby
A. Piggott, secretary; Elizabeth C. Frost,
treasurer, the vote in each case being
unanimous. Martha Morris was elected to
fill the vacancy in the board of trustees
caused by Martha A. Shelmerdine being
chosen vice president. Anna E. Hunter
and Louie Chamberlain were received as


members of the society. It was moved and
seconded that the minutes be accepted as
read; carried.
	ABBY A. PIGGOTT, Sec.


SAN FRANClSCO LETTER.

Dear Sister Wells:

	It may be of interest to some of the read-
ers of the “Exponent” to know that in far
off San Francisco there are a few Saints
who are striving assiduously for that goal
which the precious truths of the Gospel has
placed within the reach of suffering, strug-
gling humanity, viz., the salvation of the
soul.
	There are but few, comparatively speak-
ing, who have been gathered from the sin
and temptation existing in this great city,
the enormity of which none can conceive
except those who have lived here and per-
sonally observed the conditions existing
morally, spiritually and socially; and when
we consider that out of three hundred
thousand people the Saints number probab-
ly two hundred all told, we cannot but con-
clude that the good seeds which fell by the
wayside have, been almost entirely choked
out by the thrifty growth of the tares.
	* * *
	There are many who do not sense the
imminent danger which threatens them;
they are blind to the fact that there is a
future in which the immutable decree has
gone forth that“all men must be judged ac-
cording to the deeds done in the body,”
but are content to drift along with the tide,
ministering to the selfish, sensual appetites
which are generated and encouraged by
luxurious living, seeking only the pleasures
of today, uninfluenced by the possibility of
a tomorrow.
	To cast aside the every day routine of
worldly pleasures and become a follower of
Jesus Christ is a sacrifice but few can make,
albeit, those who have become Saints are
sincere, honest, earnest people.
	This branch of the Latter-day Saints is
in a prosperous condition and ably presided
over by President Woodward. Our meet-
ings are fairly attended, especially on Sun-
day evenings. Usually the congregation is
so large it is necessary to arrange extra
seats for the accomodation of all present.
The visitors are attentive, and as a rule
seem to be a class of people who come for
information and not to disturb.
	Sister Ratenbury, a lady possibly re-
membered by many of the old residents of
Salt Lake City for her ability to quote
Shakespeare, has the Relief Society in
charge, and not only the poor and needy
are ministered to by this organization but
also the Elders, whose clothes are reno-
vated and hosiery restored to a useful con-
dition by the sisters on their work days.
	The M. I. Associations are under the
control of Brother Collins and Sister Cum-
mings, formerly of Salt Lake City, and are
completely organized and fully equipped for
mutual improvement.
	The little ones bright and active, ready
and willing to assist, by their little efforts in
making their flourishing organization on an
equality with the ideal Primaries of Utah,
are presided over by Sister Woodward, wife
of President Woodward.
	Indeed the Saints are all zealous, earnest
workers, striving for the same grand prize
which ultimately is received by all those
who live faithfully and according to the
commandments of Him who rewardeth


liberally and upbraideth not. It is gratify-
ing beyond words to know that although
separated from the nucleus of Christ’s King-
dom the subjects thereof are loyal and faith-
ful, and whose impetus to right and proper
living is that grandest and most glorious of
rewards, eternal life.
				Your sister.
					RHODA C. NASH
	San Francisco, Feb. 27, 1899.

	SEMI-MONTHLY MEETING.

	The Ladies’ Semi-monthly meeting was
held in the 14th ward hall January 21,1899,
with Counselor E. J. Stevenson presiding.
Singing “Guide us O, Thou Great Jehovah.”
Prayer by Counselor Webb. Singing, “O
say what is truth.” Minutes read and ac-
cepted. Sister Annie T. Hyde stated that
President Horne was not able to be present
but that Sister Priscilla J. Riter had been
chosen to be superintendent of the nurses
who had been trained for Relief Society
work by Dr. Roberts, that President Horne
had sent a letter suggesting that a com-
mittee be appointed to solicit for bedding
etc., also to visit the drug stores and see
what discount they would give on drugs, or
anything that might be needed in their line.
Said, “Sister Riter will engage the nurse,
and she will go out under her direction and
report to her when through. Sister Riter
will visit the sick at least once.” Sister
Hyde advised the nurses to make them-
selves competent and desirable help.
	Sister Riter addressed the meeting a few
moments. Said she felt it a great responsi-
bility that had been placed upon her, and
also wished to have the support of the
nurses as she expected to give them hers.
Advised them to be united and forgiving.
Told them she did not want them to be
overburdened, but come and talk over with
her any dissatisfaction that might exist
while in her employ.
	Dr. Roberts hoped the strong bond of
love would exist between Sister Riter and
the nurses that existed between herself and
these students. Hoped all would start out
on a two years’ mission, and do anything
that was necessary for the comfort of the
sick, rise above annoyances and criticisms.
"It is understood the sick can have prefer-
ences and express them in regard to the
nurse, but no nurse is expected to go out
without being under the direction of Sister
Riter.”
	A sewing meeting was appointed for
February 3, in the Relief Society hall in
the 14th ward.
	Counselor Stevenson praised the Lord
that the sick could thus be provided with
nurses. She felt that the hand of the Lord
was in this work. Desired all to pray for
the recovery of President Horne.
	Meeting adjourned for two weeks.
Benediction by Sister Clara C. Cannon.

Minutes of Semi-monthly meeting held
February 11, 1899, in the 14th ward hall,
Counselor E. J. Stevenson presiding. Sing-
ing, “O, Jesus the Giver.” Prayer by
Sister E. Paul. Singing, “O, awake my
slumbering minstrel. Minutes read and
approved.
	Counselor Stevenson said she was sorry
President Horne was not able to come to-
day. Spoke of having visited the Relief
Society conference of Pocatello. Though
they did not have the advantages we have
they seemed as if they were advancing fast-
er than we were in some things. They

[Column 2]
were surrounded by some who were not of
our faith; had the privilege of converting
some. Advised mothers to. take the Juve-
nile and good literature, and exhorted them
to be humble and God fearing.
	Sister Barnes gave some good, motherly
advice. Sister Julia C. Howe said we met
to be strengthened by the spirit of God, we
were surrourided bymany temptations,
especially the young people, and it is neces-
sary to put safe guards around them. She
often wondered what she would do without
the Relief Society, thought it so necessary
that our young people should attend their
meetings, and also to interest the young
ladies in the Relief Society. Spoke of the
great responsibilities of mothers in rearing
their children.
	Sister Liddle realized that her greatest
trial had proved to be her greatest blessing.
Exhorted all to be patient and acknowledge
the hand of the Lord in all things. He is
angry with none except those who will not
acknowledge his hand in all things.
	Sister Rawlings bore a faithful testimony.
Congregation sang “All is well.”
	Sister L. D. Alder advised mothers to
pray and have prayers with their children.
Prayer is the corner stone, nothing will
make us happier than visiting our mar-
ried childrens’ homes and seeing them meet
around the family altar night and morning.
Said we must be careful and not do any-
thing that brings trial upon the missionaries
as anything and everything that is done in
Utah is exaggerated and misrepresented in
the papers and carried to different parts of
the earth.
	Sister Elizabeth Brown testified to the
power of prayer, made other good remarks.
	Sister Paul said words could not express
the gratitude of her heart for theGospel, she
was happy in her religion. Asked the sis-
ters to pray for their enemies and they
would not have power to do any harm.
	The congregation sang “Awake ye
defenders of Zion.” Other sisters bore
testimony and Sister Jane James said she
prayed for her enemies, for she knew they
must suffer.
	Sister Butterworth crossed the plains and
helped to drive cattle, but was happy
through it all, as she knew she was com-
ing to Zion. Sister Pond said she wanted
fo bear, a testimony of tithing. When her
husband was on a mission she paid her
tithing and the Lord had greatly blessed
her. Spoke of the inspiration of a Relief
Society president in Logan in regard to
Temple work.
	Counselor Elizabeth Stevenson spoke of
the experiences of the Saints, how it was
necessary we should have these ex-
periences. Told the Saints they must not
grieve too much over sorrows, it was not
pleasing to the Lord. Said these meetings
were organized for retrenchment, and let us
retrench in everything. When we have
sickness let us not call for doctors, but ask
the Lord to help us.
	Meeting adjourned for two weeks. Sing-
ing, Doxology, Benediction Annie T. Hyde.
				J.S. WOOLLEY, Sec.

IN MEMORIAM.
ELIZABETH TIMPSON.

	The death of Sister Elizabeth Timpson
which occurred December 31, 1898, re-
moves from our midst a faithful laborer of
righteousness. Always cheerful and lov-
ing she early won the love of both old and
young, and her departure from this life is
a loss keenly felt by all who knew her.
	She was born in Oundle, Northampton,
England, March 15, 1838. She was the
wife of Wm. B. Timpson, who preceded
her in death thirty-five years. She had be-
come a member of the Church previous to
the death of her husband, and in 1868 with
a family of five small children came to
Utah. She has resided in Salt Lake City
ever since and for many years was a
member of the Sixteenth ward.
	Always ready and anxious to do good
she was among the first to become associated
with the Relief Society of the ward, and
became a teacher of the society and held
that position until her death, never falter-
ing in the good cause. Her loss will be
deeply felt by her colaborers, for although
of late years she has been very weak phy-
sically, yet her presence was always in-
spiring. It is needless to speak of her
goodness, for everywhere her works testify
of it, and she will never be forgotten by
her friends.
	For many years she held a prominent
position in the Sunday School, also in the
Primary Association, being president of
that association at the time of her death.
She loved the children and they in turn
loved her and greatly miss her at their
meetings.
	Notwithstanding her many duties she
never neglected her home and her children.
Sister Timpson leaves four sons and one
daughter, all of whom are now married and
have families of their own.
	Last spring Sister Timpson suffered from
an attack of pneumonia which left her in a
very weak state, but without serious results
until one week before her death At this
time some very astonishing incidents oc-
curred which are related by one of her sons
as follows:
	“For some little time,” said he, “mother
had been staying at the house of my aunt,
her sister, in the Seventh ward. On Fri-
day last mother told her sister that she was
going home, as she thought she had visited
long enough. My aunt tried to persuade
her to remain, because she was fond of her
company, but mother came home, and on
Monday told her sister not to think her
action strange. 'I wanted to come home
and die in my own bed,’ explained mother.
	“On Wednesday last we were called to the
bedside and mother bade us all good bye,
wishing us a happy new year. At 12 o’clock
noon, she passed through the throes of
death and the spirit had flown. All the
members of the family were present, as
were a number of friends, and they will
bear out my statement. Mother had been
dead for over fifteen minutes and we were
about to arrange for the proper care of the
remains when mother opened her eyes and
said, ‘O, what a good sleep I have had! I
wish I could tell you all I have seen, but I
can’t. I will say, though, that I have
suffered the pain of death and my spirit
has been separated from my body. I saw
a great many things, but am forbidden to
tell you of them. Your father has not
come for me yet, so I will remain with you


a little, just a little longer, and  when he
does come for me I shall go with him and
shall not return to you. What I saw, I
saw in the spirit, and what I say now I say
in the flesh. 
	“We were very much impressed with
what mother had said, the more so because
of her absolute rationality while making
the strange statement.
	“A similiar occurrence came again on
Thursday at noon, and we are all left in
wonder as to what it all meant. There
are people outside of our family who wit-
nessed the strange and unusual occurrence
and who are as much astonished as we are.
When mother died at 3 o’clock this morn-
ing we thought perhaps it was a repetition
of the two previous incidents, and for that
reason we made no move whatever towards
arrangements for care of the remains until
9 o’clock, six hours after death. A state-
ment made by mother after her first return
to life was proved when death came this
morning. On Wednesday she said ‘I have
suffered the pains of death, but when your
father comes and I leave you, I will go
peacefully and without pain.'
	“And so it was. Death came as an
angel of peace and there was not even a
sigh at dissolution.” 
	Sister Timpson has done a great work,
led the life of a noble woman and we feel
that she has gone to a great reward.
	RACHEL ISAACS, Pres.
	ELLA SELANDER, Sec.


WOMAN'S EXPONENT.


OBITUARY.
			ELIZABETH WEILER.
	It becomes our painful duty to record the death
of our beloved president, Sister Elizabeth Weiler,
who departed this life January 10, 1899. She was
born January 25, 1819, making her the ripe age of
eighty years within fifteen days. She
was president of the Relief Society of the 3rd
ward for thirty-one years on the day of her funer-
al, which was held on the 13th day of January,
1899.
	Sister Weiler was beloved of all her associates
for her gentleness, her integrity, her love of right
and her staunch faith in the Gospel of Christ. She
was a friend to the poor and the widow, and she
exhibited great wisdom in all of her counsels.
	During the thirty-one years of her presidency
in connection with her sisters she worked in per-
fect harmony. We recognize the death of our be-
loved president as the loss of a faithful, energetic
worker, a friend to those in need and a comforter
to those in distress. We esteem her faithful and
untiring labors and devotion to the interest of
Zion worthy the emulation of every member of
the society.
	RESOLUTIONS OF RESPECT.
	Resolved, That we sympathize with the be-
reaved children, grandchildren and great grand-
children in the loss of a loving parent and a dear
friend.
	Resolved, That these resolutions be given to
her children, also that they be inscribed in the
records of the Relief Society, and that we pub-
lish them in the Woman’s Exponent
	ZURVIAH G. EARDLEY, Sec.


[Column 1]
THE BON MARCHE.
	What a fascination there is in the atmos-
phere of beautiful Paris! The gay butter-
fly life in the streets brilliant with life and
color.  The working classes—seldom with
hats or bonnets —live out of doors every
practicable moment, loitering aimlessly
about or chattering volubly together around
the tiny tables arranged on the broad walks
before the numerous restaurants. And the
streets are usually so beautifully clean, the
boulevards so wide and the parks so green.

[Column 2]
From the tops of the Arc de Triumph one
gets a view of fairyland.
	The Bon (good) Marche (market) is situ-
ated in the old portion of the city, the Latin
quarter, not far from the palace and beauti-
ful gardens of the Luxembourg, and while
there are other fine establishments of the
kind in Paris, the Bon Marche seems to 
hold the power of attracting the masses.
It draws all classes, from the peasant to the
princess of noble blood, and the fact that if
one should purchase under the stress of
strong temptation something which in one’s
cooler moments would prove unsatisfactory
or an unwise extravagance it could be re-
turned and the money refunded, has done
much in building up the enviable reputa-
tion of the house.
	The establishment of the Bon Marche,
specially constructed for a dry goods busi-
ness, is the largest, best organized and best
fitted up in the world. It contains all that
experience can suggest for convenience and
commodity, and is one of the most remark-
able sights of Paris. In 1863 Aristide
Bouciaut became sole proprietor where he
had entered ten years previously as partner.
He was certainly a man of the highest com-
mercial intelligence, but what contributed
to the success and to the immense increase
of his business was his spirit of enterprise,
the rectitude of his character and his great
benevolence. He not only founded a
powerful business house, he founded at the
same time a great humane work, a social
institution. He did not merely content
himself with the realization of profit, he ap-
plied himself above all to merit the confi-
dence of the public by universally acknowl-
edged integrity. He was supported by a
staff of superior employees, chosen with
care, governed paternally and organized
according to a just rule which accords ad-
vancement for services rendered, and per-
mits each one to aspire to the most im-
portant positions.
	The Provident fund founded by Aristide
Boucicaut, the Retiring Pension fund
founded by his wife and the Relief and
Pension fund premit all employees to await
with confidence the days of old age, while
many of them have purchased shares in the
business.
	The moral and intellectual condition has
not been neglected. Free evening classes
comprising the study of the English lan-
guage, vocal and instrumental music and
fencing have been instituted for their bene-
fit. Every year the most assiduous among
the pupils following the English classes are
sent to London at the expense of the house
for a period of six months to perfect them-
selves in the English language. A doctor
is engaged by the house and free consulta-
tions are given every morning at nine
o'clock. Women in confinement are
allowed one hundred francs (twenty dollars)
to defray the expense of a nurse.
	The Provident fund is supported by a
sum deducted annually from the profits of
the business. All employees in the house
five years become participators in its bene-
fits. Each member has an individual ac-
count opened in his or her name and is pro-
vided with a book indicating exactly the
amount to the credit of each one. This
amount is further increased by an annual in-
terest of four per cent on the individual
capital. Any lady clerk contracting mar-
riage, even though she leave the establish-
ment, is entitled to the full amount to her
credit. In 1876 the fund commenced with
a capital of 62,000 francs, which in 1895

[Column 3]
had increased to 2,286,215 francs, not in-
cluding the amount paid out to the various
participators.
	Madame Boucicaut after her husband’s
death, wishing to carry out his idea and to
provide for those who contribute to the suc-
cess of the Bon Marche, instituted the Re-
tiring Pension fund, endowing it with the
modest sum of 5,000,000 francs from her
personal fortune. Two hundred and thirty
old assistants have now actually retired and
are receiving pensions amounting to
216,000 francs per annum. It is a life pen-
sion and varies from 600 to 1500 francs per
annum.
	Employees called on for military service,
if on their return they can show an irre-
proachable military certificate, are re-
installed in their former positions.
	The picture and sculpture gallery is for 
the purpose of enabling artists to exhibit
their work free, thereby assisting many a
worthy person to enter into business re-
lation with the numerous patrons of the
Bon Marche. Copies are not accepted, only
the original works can be admitted. The
Bon Marche submits all offers made to the
artists, and demands neither hanging fee
nor pay of any kind.
	In the reading room (salon de lecture)
the French and leading foreign newspapers
are at the disposal of all visitors, as well as
abundant writing material, and every day
the immense table which extends from one
end of the long room to the other is sur-
rounded. Some are writing letters home,
some reading and others simply resting.
	Yes, and one needs a rest if one has tried
to enter at the main entrance about three
o'clock on a bright afternoon in the spring-
time as I did several times. The crush is
truly something awful. One is simply car-
ried along by a restless, surging tide of
femininity, and the noise, the buzz, the in-
cessant chatter, the per[-]ume, the tropical
garden of hats in a constantly moving pano-
rama, the magnificent display of dry goods
of every color and kind under the sun and
distributed about with a reckless prodigal-
ity that is exasperating to the woman of
limited means who for the time being de-
sires nothing so much as a pocketbook
equal in size to that of the Count of Monte
Cristo, is something to remember. What a
bewildering, artistically arranged mass of
everything calculated to attract, from the
poor working girl up, up to the ultra
fashionable woman, what woman could
leave the silk skirt department where they
are spread out before one by thousands, a
perfect rainbow of colors, beautifully made
and trimmed with ruffles, flounces and lace,
without a pang of regret.
	The entire building is mostly glass, for
the whole four sides are windows and the
roof is glass. Every window is a small
room in size and the goods so attractively
arranged as to be a poem in itself.
	The horses and delivery wagons are oh
the same gorgeous order. The horses are
sleek, fat and well groomed with harnesses
mounted with gold, the wagons red and
gold and black and gold, and there were
thirty-six wagons and about one hundred,
and fifty horses. Every morning about
eight o’clock they passed down the Rue de
Cherche Midi on their way from the stables
and it was a pleasing sight. I used to
watch for them and think how well they
were matched. Of course all did not leave
the stable in the early morning, the rest
were sent for as they were needed through-
out the day.


The Bon Marche differs from our dry
goods houses in that they have no cash girls
or boys. Every separate department has
its own desk and cashier and every time
a lady makes a purchase the clerk waiting on
her accompanies her to the desk where her
parcel is wrapped and she receives her
change, if she be so fortunate as to have
any coming to her.
	The crush in 1900 will doubtless be
greater than ever and the goods distributed
about with a recklessness never seen before,
therefore when we all go over to the Ex-
position let us do as Shakespeare so delight-
fully advises, “put money in our purses,"
for we shall be tempted as we never were
before, not only to purchase the where-
withal to enhance our natural attractiveness,
but in other departments such as bric-a-
brac, pictures, china, carpets and drapery,
table linen and drawn work and a thousand
and one other things too numerous to men-
tion.
	If I am lucky enough to put money into
my purse I’ll meet you all there and pilot
you to the very choicest spots in the whole
building.
S.L.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


MARGARET CHANLER AND ANNA
			BOULING.
	Margaret Livingston Chanler and Annie
Bouling, heroic women who served with-
out pay as nurses in Porto Rico during the
late war, have been recommended for that
rare honor, the thanks of Congress. They
landed in Ponce July 31, and the same day
began nursing American and Spanish sick.
Miss Chanler, from her own purse, hired a
house and established a hospital for
American officers, and as the number of
sick grew, hired another and another until
she had three hospitals in operation at her
own expense. In September she turned
these hospitals over to the government
trained nurses, having first provided for
their rental and running expenses.
	The two brave women then went to
Coamo, nursing the sick there, and later to
San Juan, and as soon as San Juan was
under the American flag started a hospital
there. General Miles says their conduct
was most commendable and praisworthy.
	“Such noble and generous acts performed
under circumstances of great embarrassment
and danger of contracting fatal disease prev-
alent at that time,” he says, “our govern-
ment should recognize, and I recommend
that appropriate thanks be presented these
two women, and in the case of Miss Chan-
ler on account of her contributing so large-
ly and generously from her own private
fund without the least hope of ever being
remunerated, I further recommend that the
matter be brought to the attention of Con-
gress for such action as it may deem advis-
able.”
	The suggestion is endorsed by Secretary
Alger, who recommended suitable action
by Congress. It is probable the two wo-
men will be given medals and the thanks
of Congress.


COLORADO GOES ON RECORD.
	Upon the 30th of January there was
passed in the Colorado assembly by a vote
of 45 to 3, and the following day in the
Senate by a vote of 30 to 1, the following
resolution:


Whereas, Equal suffrage has been in
operation in Colorado for five years, during
which time women have exercised the
privilege as generally as men, with the re-
sult that better candidates have been selec-
ted for office, methods of election have been
purified, the character of legislation im-
proved, civic intelligence increased and
womanhood developed to greater usefulness 
by political responsibility, therefore be it
	Resolved, By the House of Representa-
tives, the Senate concurring, that in view
of these results, the enfranchisement of wo-
men in every state and territory in the
American Union is recommended as a
measure tending to the advancement of a
higher and better social order.
That an authenticated copy of these reso-
lutions be forwarded by the governor of the
state to the legislature of every state and
territory, and that the press be requested to
call public attention to these resolutions.
	This resolution ought to have some in-
fluence upon the people and papers who
have persistently tried to keep in circula-
tion reports derogatory to the “Colorado
experiment.’’—Ex.


The Western movement against the em-
ployment of married women as school
teachers should also embrace the principle
that no bachelor teachers can be employed.
If you refuse to employ married women,
you discourage marriage. But refuse at
the same time to employ unmarried men
and you encourage marriage.—Ex.


U. W. P. C.
	The U. W. P. C. met in the "Ex-
ponent” office in the Templeton, January
31, 1899, President Ella W. Hyde in the
chair. Opening prayer was offered by Dr.
E. R. Shipp. Roll call responded to by
sentiments from George Crabbe.
	Under new business the subject of the
traveling library came up for consideration.
On motion of Mrs. E. B. Wells the club
voted to pay the annual dollar for the
library. President Hyde stated that any
book given by club members would be
gladly received by the library committee,
Mrs. Moore chairman.
	An interesting and charming paper on
the Bon Marche was given by Mrs. Lawler,
giving a life like account of that great
institution in the gay city of Paris. Anec-
dotes by some of the ladies and some dis-
cussion followed. An original poem,
Night Winds, by Mrs. L. D. Alder, was
next read. It was intensely suitable for
the wild and boisterous night. A very
meritorious paper on the Khartoum and
Sir Horatio Herbert Kitchener, the famous
Sidar of the Egyptian army, was read
with pleasing effect by Mrs. Lizzie S. Wil-
cox
	Mrs. Zina D. H. Young, president of the
National Woman’s Relief Society, and an
honorary member of the club, celebrates
her 78th birthday today. This is also the
76th birthday of Mrs. Jane S. Richards,
vice president of the N. W.. R. S., who is,
now visiting in Los Angelos. The club
extends best wishes to theso two veterans
in the cause of women, and wishes them
fair sailing over life’s uncertain sea.
	After some discussion an adjournment
was taken for one month. Dismissed by
Mrs. Doolan.
			L. D. ALDER, Sec, pro tem.


AMERICAN SILK. Commenting on the 
conditions governing the trade, the Silk
Association of America through its secre-
tary, has this to say: “Rising prices in the
raw-silk markets of the world have pro-
duced a decided rise in the prices of raw
silk in this market during the last thirty
days. This naturally necessitates a rise in
all silk products, and some of the branches
of the trade have formaly agreed in an
advance of prices. In this country at pres-
ent there are 27,000 power looms for broad
silk weaving,and 6,000 for ribbon weaving.
During the year there have been a number
of additional plants established in this
country by foreign firms and naturally
there has been some extension of facilities
by our older manufacturers. While addi-
tional facilities of manufacture is a gratify-
ing omen, it also carries with it an appre-
hension of facilities unduly increased. It
is hardly necessary to say that overpro-
duction is about as bad as a sluggish mar-
ket; in fact it conduces to it. The raw silk
supply on which our manufacturers depend
is indicated in the following figures.

IMPORTS IN UNITED STATES OF RAW SILK.
						Bales. 
1894.......................................54,924	$24,728,163.
1895.......................................66,989	30,595,991.
1896.......................................39,754	14,411,073.
1897.......................................72,496	30,636,009.
1898.......................................69,392	26,058,312.

RELICS.

“O! what shall we do with all of this stuff,”
	Said fair Estelle one day,
“Right here in this garret there is enough  
To fill one with dismay.
Sure in the new home there’ll be no room 
	For trash and rubbish like this.”
As I glanced around I knew my doom,
	So closed her lips with a kiss.
I smiled and quietly sent her away,
	For what would she think of tears,
That I knew would come while I should survey
	The hoarded treasures of years.
Yes, well may she ask what should be done,
	With dresses my grandmother wore,
Her o’d rocking chair and patchwork begun
	In the leisure hours of yore.

These scrapbags and bundles and old broken
		toys,
	Withered wreaths and baskets of flowers,
And the cradle where slept my girls, and my
		boys,
	Thorugh innocent babyhood hours.
This pile of old letters with histories rife,
	Ah! the burden of love they bear,
With tales of the brightest seasons of life,
	And of days bowed down with care.

This moccasin made for the fair first born,
	This shoe by the second one worn,
This christening robe with laces adorned,
	And bonnets all soiled and torn.
The relics of missions and travels abroad, 
	Books, chests and specimens, too,
Speaking now to my soul in voices aloud,
	Of scenes that have pass’d from my view.

This old fashioned bureau of native wood
		made,
	Is all that is left of the set
He gave me the morn of the day we were
		wed,
	With kisses I ne’er can forget.
Still here in its drawers are ribbons and lace
	Old jewels and gold-tinseled fans, 
That waved at grand balls with exquisite
		grace,
	While held in fair dimpled hands.

The identical waistcoat he wore for my sake,
	And this he wore that fair day—
Along with my gown of old fashioned make,
	Have long lain carefully away.
These precious mementoes of more precious
		days,
	To the flames must all be consigned!
Just so mortal joys will melt from our gaze,
	And souls must needs be resigned.


O, weak human heart we cling to this day,
	Make idols of earth’s fading things,
And follow the phantom that flitteth away,
	And pleasures that travel on wings.
O, help us our Father to took up to Thee,
	Seek joys that ever abide,
Those truths and that faith that never will flee,
	Whatever of ill may betide.
						ELLIS R. SHIPP.


HOW DO THEY COMPARE?

	Ida H. Harper recently published a very
convincing argument upon equal suffrage,
in which she instituted a comparison be-
tween the personnel of the anti-suffragists
and the suffragists. Alluding to the form-
er she says:
	“Almost without exception the former
appear in print under the husband’s name.
Their own individual names would convey
no meaning to the public. Scarcely one is
known for her own work in literature,
science, education, practical philanthropy,
business enterprise or any active effort
for the benefit of mankind. Opposed to
these place the names of the most promin-
ent of the pronounced advocates of suffrage
for women, Lucretia Mott, Elizabeth Cady
Stanton, Susan B. Anthony, Harriet Martin-
eau, Florence Nightingale, Frances Power
Cobbe, Lady Aberdeen, Lady Henry Somer-
set, Annie Besant, Lucy Stone, Harriet
Beecher Stowe, Louisa M. Alcott, Mary A.
Livermore, Julia Ward Howe, Clara Bar-
ton, Zerelda G. Wallace, Mary L. Booth,
Alice and Phoebe Cary, Mary Clemmer,
May Wright Sewall, Alice Freeman Palmer,
Sarah B. Cooper, Leonora Barry Lake,
Anna Dickinson, Anna L. Diggs, Grace
Greenwood, Harriet Hosmer, Lilian Whit-
ing, Bertha Honore Palmer, Jane L. Stan-
ford, Elizabeth Stuart Phelps, Frances E.
Willard and all the host of W. C. T. U.
leaders, Myra Bradwell and all the women
lawyers, Rev. Anna H. Shaw and all the
women ministers, Dr. Mary Putnam Jacobi,
and practically all the women physicians
and the vast majority of teachers and news-
paper women. 
	The list might be extended almost indifin-
itely and it would include nine-tenths of
the women who have made a name for
themselves and who are distinguished for
their own commanding ability. What a
travesty that a handful of women, eminent
only in social positions, should besiege the
legislatures to withhold the right of repre-
sentation from such as these! How much
worse than a travesty that they should
seek to deprive the wage earning women
of what would be their strongest weapon
for self-protection! All the opposition of
men for the last half century has not caused
so much discouragement to the women who
have fought the battles as this unprecedented
action on the part of their own sex. This
is not that they are retarding the progress
of the suffrage movement, for they are
having the opposite effect, but only that,
after all these years of opportunity, the wo-
men who should be leaders are laggards
and obstructionists.”


NOTES AND NEWS.

	The action taken at the recent Congress
of the Daughters of the American Revolu-
tion looking to an amalgamation of that
society with the Daughters of the Revolu-
tion, was entirely voluntary upon the part
of the D. A. R., and not with the sanction
or at the request of the Daughters of the
Revolution. There is no probability of a
“surrender” by the D. R. society to the D.
A. R’s.
	The General Society Daughters of the
Revolution constitutes all “original daugh-
ters,” women whose fathers served in the
War of the Revolution, honorary members
of the society, and presents them with a
handsomely engraved parchment certificate
of membership and a gold badge. Authen-
tic information of such daughters will be
gladly received at the headquarters of the
society, 156 Fifth Ave., New York City.
	The District of Columbia W. S. Associa-
tion, on motion of Dr. McNaughton, passed
unanimously a resolution of approval of
the disarmament conference called by the
Czar of Russia to meet in St. Petersburg in
May next, and asking the President of the
United States to appoint Mrs. Belva A.
Lockwood a delegate. Without any con-
cert of action a similar resolution was passed
by the Woman’s Industrial Relief League,
putting it upon the ground that the time
had arrived when the administration should
do something for women, and that Mrs.
Lockwood was by previous service pre-
eminently fit for the position.
	A lifelong suffragist has just been tender-
ly conducted through the portals of death
into the home of the soul, at the ripe age of
ninety three. Mrs. Sophronia Snow leaves
her impress on the age in the influence of
her daughters, two of whom live in Wash-
ington and two at the old home in Hamden
Corners, Maine. One of the latter is Mrs.
Jane H. Spofford, who was for so many
years intimately connected with the work
of the National Woman’s Suffrage Associ-
ation. It was with her that Mrs. Snow
passed her later years, and at this home not
long ago, she enjoyed visiting with Miss
Anthony, who was a guest there during
the suffrage convention at that place.
	Mrs. Alice Merrill Horne has introduced
an important educational bill in the Legis-
lature, providing for an increase of one
hundred per cent in the Normal scholar-
ship of the University. Its leading features
are as follows: The Normal school shall
be continued as a department of the Uni-
versity, for students of both sexes, and its
course of instruction shall include practice
in teaching and in pedagogy, may extend
through a period of four years, or until
graduation. Two hundred free scholars
may be maintained in the Normal school.
The holders of them shall be exempt from
the payment of all matriculation fees. Fifty
appointments to these scholarships, each for
a term of four years, may be made annually.
The bill was passed.
