[Column 1]
IN MEMORIAM.

MARGARET CHERRY.
	In memory of Mrs. Margaret Cherry,
who with her husband and six children
came to Utah in 1847 from Kentucky,
where she was born February 11, 1811.
They moved in the summer of 1848 to
Centerville, where she has lived ever since
until her death, which occurred October 18,
1898.
	Sister Cherry was a widow for thirty-
four years. When the Relief Society was
organized she became a member and officer
of that society and opened her house for
them to meet in. She afterwards gave a
small house to the society which they met
in for twenty years. As age and infirmities
came on her she was unable to leave her
home for a number of years. She was of a
peaceful disposition, a good neighbor and
endeavored to live her religion and keep
the golden rule. She died in full faith and
left a large posterity, of whom nearly all
are in the Church.
			MARY ROCKWOOD.
Centerville Relief Society.
KATE LAUGHAN LEISHMAN.
	We have been called upon to record the
death of one of our beloved sisters, Kate
Maughan Leishman, who departed this life at
her home in Wellsville, Cache Co., Utah,
Jan 15, 1899.
	Sister Leishman has been a faithful work-
er in our Relief Society, and has endeared
herself to the little band of sisters in this
branch by her sweet and gentle disposition,
and we her sisters offer these resolutions of
respect as a slight token of our love for her
who has been called to a higher sphere of
action.
RESOLUTIONS OF RESPECT.
	Resolved, That as God in His infinite
wisdom has seen fit to call home our dear
sister, Kate Maughan Leishman, we the
officers and members of the Baker City
branch of the Relief Society, express our
heartfelt sympathy for the bereaved hus-
band and family in their loss; also that they
have been greatly blessed with her sweet
and gentle influence in her home; and be it
further
	Resolved, That these resolutions be
placed upon our society records, one sent to
the bereaved family and one sent to the
Woman’s Exponent for publication.
			REBECCA NIBLEY, Pres.
			NELLIE W. ROUNDY, Sec.
A SAD BEREAVEMENT.
	Sister Elizabeth Pehrson, beloved daugh-
ter of Eric John and Anna S. Pehrson,
died January 15, 1899, after a lingering
weakness for some time, but was not con-
fined to her bed till one week prior to her
death, when she was attacked with an in-
termittent fever. Her weakness became
greater, though she took nourishment till
the last. She passed away without a strug-
gle Sunday morning between five and six
o’clock, in the arms of her beloved mother
and sister.
	Sister Pehrson was born and raised in
the Gospel and died a faithful Latter day
Saint, placing her religion above every
thing else. She was honest, virtuous, kind
and patient, never refusing any call that
was made upon her, therefore holding
many responsible positions at her death.

[Column 2]
	She was a teacher in the Sabbath School,
secretary in the Relief Society, second
counselor in the Y. L. M. I. A., and secre-
tary in the Y. M. and Y. L. Improvement
Association; also held the position of school
trustee and postmistress. She was born
April 28, 1873, at Vernon, Tooele Co., Utah.
	On Friday 20, her angel like form was
borne in a beautiful white casket decorated
with wreaths and blooming roses, to the
meeting house where the funeral services
were held. The stand was draped in white
and decorated with flowers, giving the
whole a beautiful appearance. The choir
sang “Guide Me to Thee.” Prayer was
offered by Brother David Bennion. Sing-
ing, “Wanted on the Other Side.” Consol-
ing remarks were made by Stake President
H. S. Gowan and our beloved Bishop J. C.
Sharp. The choir sang “Sister Thou
wert Mild and Lovely.” Benediction by
Neils Fredrickson.
	The remains were conveyed to the ceme-
tery in a light vehicle drawn by white
horses. The dedicatory prayer was offered
by President H. S. Gowan. The choir
sang “There is Sweet Rest in Heaven.”
The body was then carefully sheltered by
mother earth to rest in peace.
							C.
FRISTENCE MAGDALENE ERIKSEN.
	It is our painful duty to announce that
on December 4, 1898, occurred the death of
Sister Fristence Magdalene Eriksen from
heart failure.
	Deceased was born in Otostrup, Wolstrup
Sogn, Denmark, on the 11th of March 1857.
She was baptized into the Church of Jesus
Christ of Latter-day Saints in 1880 and
emigrated to Utah in 1881. She was a
member of the Newton ward, Cache Stake
of Zion. She died in full faith of the Gos-
pel and with the hope of a glorious resur-
rection. She was a member of the Relief
Society and always took an active part in it.
	The following resolutions were adopted
by the society in her memory:
RESOLUTIONS OF RESPECT.
	Whereas in the providence of God it
has pleased Him to call away this our be-
loved sister to a higher sphere, and we
reverence Him who holds the destinies of
men and who permits nothing to come to
pass but for a wise and glorious purpose; and
	Whereas at the time of her death she
was an active member of the Relief Society
and was held in high esteem by all who
knew her, for her noble qualities, therefore
be it
	Resolved, That we the members of the
Relief Society of Newton ward tender to
the bereaved family our heartfelt sympathy
and condolence for the sad loss they have
sustained of a faithful wife and a loving
sister; and be it further
	Resolved, That a copy of this preamble
and resolutions be recorded in the minutes
of the Relief Society, and a copy printed in
the Woman’s Exponent.
		ELIZABETH FUNK,
		MARY JENKINS,
		MAGDALENE PETERSON,
						Committee.
RESOLUTIONS OF RESPECT AND 
CONDOLENCE. 
	In loving respect to Sister Anna Lang-
hurst Layne, one of our pioneer women
and a faithful teacher in the Cardston Re-
lief Society, Canada.

[Column 3]
	She was born February 22, 1849, at
Stow on the Woods, Glostershire, England.
Died December 23, 1898, in Cardston after
an illness of two days, leaving a husband
and ten children to mourn her departure.
	Sister Layne came to Cardston with her
husband and family in the year 1887. She
was true to her religion and her covenants,
having embraced the higher order of mar-
riage for eternity she has now a claim to a
higher celestial glory. She was always an
energetic and faithful worker in the Relief
Society, a sympathetic and faithful nurse,
ever ready to assist the sick and the poor
and to cheer up the downcast; a loving
wife, a devoted mother, looking after her
children with a love unceasing. We feel
that her wise counsel will be heeded by her
children, (the youngest of whom is eight
years of age) that they will profit by her
worthy example.
	Whereas our Heavenly Father in His
infinite wisdom has seen fit to call home
our beloved sister, Anna Layne,
	Resolved, That in the death of Sister
Layne we are deprived of the society of a
loving sister and a dear friend, one who
was always alive to her duties and will be
greatly missed; so willing in the hour of
need, many sad hearts have been brightened
by her cheerful words of encouragement;
therefore, be it
	Resolved, That we sincerely sympathize
with those who are bereft and are bound by
the nearest and dearest ties, and we share
with them the hope of a happy reunion in
the bright hereafter. We pray that heaven’s
choicest blessings may attend the bereaved
family, and the spirit of the Lord be with
them to comfort and cheer them.
	Resolved, That a copy of these resolutions
be given to the bereaved family, one placed
on record and one sent to the Woman’s
Exponent for publication.
			SARAH B. DAINES, Pres.
			C. ELNORA WIGHT, Sec.
			LOTTIE BARTON, Rec. Sec.


U. W. P. C.

	The U. W. .P. C. met in the “Ex-
ponent” office February 28, 1899. The
president being absent the vice president,
Mrs. C. G. R. Wells presided. Opening
prayer by Dr. E. R. Shipp, after which the
secretary called the roll, responses being
given by quotations from the poet Moore.
Mrs. Francis proposed the name of Mrs.
Ida Cannon as a member. Mrs. Cannon
was unanimously elected. Mrs. L. D.
Alder read a communication from the
General Federation of Women’s Clubs,
Atlanta, Ga., and one from Chester, Penn.,
regarding organizing Federation traveling
clubs, and one from the Utah Federation re-
garding badges. The following interesting
program was then given:
	Poem by Dr. Ellis R. Shipp; Current
Events by Dr. Romania B Pratt; Tid Bits
by Mrs. H. V. Kimball, followed by dis-
cussion of members. Before the benediction
was offered all present joined in extending
well wishes and congratulations to Mrs. E.
B.Wells, this being the anniversary of her
birth, or nearly so, as she was born on the
29th. May she meet with us for many,
many years to come. 
		AIMEE C. SCHILLER, Sec.

WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


[Column 1]
MY WARNING.

Many a lesson our children teach us, 
	Did we con their quaint sayings through,
Many a loving reproof they give us—
	Doing ever the thing we do—
Showing how watchful we mothers should be,
What noble examples of purity.

’Twas in early spring many years agone,
	How well I remember the day,
The birds, just returning from warmer climes,
	Caroled forth such a joyous lay,  
All nature aroused from her winter’s sleep,
Rejoiced while I reveled in fancies dee[-].

“Mama oo’d better look out where oo step,
	I’se walked in oo’s tracks all de way,
And mine s’oes are as muddy as dey can be,
	But oo cant scold Nellie today,
’Cause oo’s are as muddy as mine don’t oo see? 
Oh mama who finked dat so naughty oo’d be.”

I paused in my reverie, baby’s voice
	Had called me back from my aerial flight,
And turning, I saw that I’d left the path
	And taken a wrong, instead of the right, 
And there we were in the mud ankle deep,
Just reward for neglecting the path to keep.

I snatched up my baby, and then retraced
	My steps till ’gain I’d reached the track,
Then hand in hand, homeward we took our
		“March,”
	Thankful at last when we were back, 
The muddy shoes removed, the tired head
Pillowed upon my breast, while this I read:

“Thy children’s children in thy steps shall walk,
	Thy latter days shall be thy best,
Apostles, prophets at thy board shall dine, 
	And thy posterity shall call thee blest,
While useful ’mong thy sisters thou shalt be, 
In deeds of mercy love and charity.”

“Thy children’s children in thy path shall
		walk,”
	The words seemed burning in my very soul
My life, its acts before my vision passed 
	And I in judgment sat upon the whole,
How small my spirit stature seemed that day,
How unlike His, “The Life, the Sight the
		Way.”

Long, long I sat accusing self of wrongs, 
	Which ere had trifling seemed to me,
But viewed today gigantic forms assumed,
	“The beam removed,” I plain could see
How oft by foolish follies I was led, 
How oft omitted duties, hard words said.

How oft controlled in anger ’stead of love,
	The “daily prayer,” a “thing forgot,” 
While oft “before my neighbor’s door I wept,”
	The while repining with my lot,
Forgetful that our Father’s watchful care 
Is “over all,” yet, each his cross must bear.

Humbly I bowed before the Lord in prayer, 
	Pleading for pardon, mercy, love,
Soon o’er my senses stole a holy calm, 
	Such as comes only from above, 
And lo! a voice seemed saying o’er and o’er:
	“Rise erring daughter, go and sin no
		more,

Henceforward follow whither I shall lead,
	I’ll keep thy steps so free from sin,
Thy children, following, shall see thy light,
	And they, with thee, the victory win,
But humble ye must walk, holding ‘the rod’
Braving life’s battles, trusting Me, thy God.”

The years have flown, and though my erring
		feet,
	In moments weak, have slid aside the track,
Anon the memory of that baby voice,
	In cadence sweet has called me back,
Struggling I seek for aid from Christ my head, 
	Led by a little child, as prophets said.

[Column 2]
Father, I daily plead, keep me from sin,
	Help me a shining light to be,
To those whom Thou hast placed within my
		care,
	That I may lead them back to Thee,
Back to the home from whence, so pure, they
	came,
This is the prayer I ask in Jesus’ name.
							NELLIE.


REORGANIZATION.

	Minutes of a special meeting called to
fill the vacancy made by the death of our
late president, Sarah M. Kimball, held in
the vestry of the ward meeting house,
March 7, 1899.
	There were present Stake President An-
gus M. Cannon, Relief Society Stake Presi-
dent M. I. Horne, and her counselors, An-
nie T. Hyde and Clara C. Cannon and
others, also Bishop Jos. Morgan and Coun-
selor Nephi Morris.
	Meeting was opened by singing “Oh
say what is truth.” Prayer by Nephi Mor-
ris. Continued by singing “Guide us O,
Thou Great Jehovah.” Sarah A. F. Price
was nominated and sustained to act as secre-
tary for the meeting.
	Bishop Jos. Morgan explained the ob-
ject of calling the meeting, to effect a re-
organization of the 15th ward Relief Soci-
ety, a vacancy being caused by the death of
our late president, Sarah M. Kimball. He de-
sired to be guided by divine power in what
was done today. Spoke of the efficient
assistance given by the Relief Society to
the Bishopric.
	President M. I Horne followed with a
few remarks, Spoke in high terms of the
ability of our late president, Sarah M. Kim-
ball. Referred to the first organization and
the instructions given at that time by the
prophet, Said that it was wonderful the
amount that was gathered in in small sums
contributed by individuals. Desired the
influence of the Holy Ghost to guide us. 
Said every virtuous woman ought to be-
long to the society.
	President Angus M. Cannon spoke of the
help given to the ward, Stake and Church
by the Relief Society organizations. Said
that all members should be virtuous and
that God would reveal sin to those who
were righteous.
	Sister Harriet A. Badger was then nom-
inated and unanimously sustained as presi-
dent of the 15th ward Rellef Society.
	Sister Badger accepted the office, and
said she would do the best she could to dis-
charge her duties faithfully. She chose
for her first and second counselors respective-
ly, Mary E. R. Gray and Janette S. Griggs.
Both were unanimously sustained. Sarah
A. F. Price was nominated for secretary and
Mary W. Gray for treasurer. Both were
unanimously sustained as such.
	The officers were then set apart by Presi-
dent Angus M. Cannon, Bishop Jos. Mor-
gan and Nephi Morris. Meeting was
closed by singing “Lord dismiss us with
Thy blessing.” Benediction by Sister
Julia C. Howe.
			SARAH A. F. PRICE, Sec.


THE. N. A. W. S. A. CONVENTION.

	Mrs. Emily B. Ketchum, the president of
the Susan B. Anthony Club of Grand Rapids,
Mich., is most actively engaged in making
preparations for the approaching Annual
Convention of the National American Wo-
man’s Suffrage Association to be held in
her city from April 27 to May 3, inclusive.
	Mrs. Ketchum has secured an admirable
entertainment committee with an active
chairman, Mrs. Allen C. Adsit, 94 Madison
Ave., and the Grand Rapids people propose
to entertain most royally the entire work-
ing force of the Convention, officers, speak-
ers and delegates.
	April and May promise delightful weath-
er in that part of the country, and we are
all looking forward to not only a good
working Convention, but a pleasant op-
portunity for social intercourse with a
gathering of women which ought to repre-
sent the entire country. A number of
states have already sent information that
their full quota of delegates will be present.
The South will be represented on the pro-
gram by three of its best known suffragists,
Laura Clay and Josephine K. Henry of
Kentucky, and Frances Griffin of Alabama.
	It is hoped that at least one of the wo-
men members of Legislatures in the enfran-
chised states will be present, and perhaps
such representatives will be secured from
two of these states.
	Among the speakers will be Elizabeth
Boynton Harbert, Illinois; Evelyn H. Bel-
den, Iowa; Anna Caulfield, Michigan; May
Wright Sewall, Indiana; Merrie H. Abbott,
Michigan; Rev. Anna Howard Shaw, Penn-
sylvania; Abigail Scott Duniway, Oregon;
Martha A. Root, Michigan; Carrie Chap-
man Catt, New York; Harriet Taylor Up-
ton, Member of the Warren, Ohio, School
Board; Flora Beadle Renkes, School Com-
missioner of Berry Co., Michigan; A.
Eugenie Paul, Superintendent of Street
Cleaning Department, First Ward, Chicago,
and Susan B. Anthony.
			RACHEL FOSTER AVERY.


At the quinquennial of the Woman’s In-
ternational Council in London next June,
one of the two large public sessions will be
given to a consideration of woman suffrage,
the other to the question of peace and arbi-
tration.


NEW ORGANIZATION.

	Minutes of the organization of the Relief
Society held in the 22nd ward branch, Dec.
8, 1898.
	Bishop Solomon stated that they deemed
it proper to organize a Relief Society in this


part of the 22nd ward, and for that reason
they had called this meeting prior to the
organization.
	Instructions and advice were then given
the sisters by Bishop Solomon and his
counselors, Arthur Winter and John Nebe-
ker. Bishop Solomon then gave the names
of the following sisters to act as officers:
Sister J. S. McKean, president; Elizabeth
Lawrence, first counselor; Nancy Shaw,
second counselor; Octavia H. Walgren,
secretary; Elvira Winberg, treasurer.
	The sisters then expressed their willing-
ness to fulfill their calling to the best of
their ability. Brother Solomon advised the
sisters to meet together as often as possible,
and to seek the spirit of the Lord at all times.
	The sisters were then set apart by the
Bishop and his counselors. Bishop Solo-
mon made a few remarks, exhorting the
sisters to be faithful at all times, and asked
the blessing of the Lord upon all. Bene-
diction was pronounced by Niels Nelson.
						O. H. W.

SALT LAKE CITY, MARCH 15, 1899.


ABOUT THE TRIENNIAL.

	The ladies who attended the sessions of
the Triennial of the National Council of Wo-
men of the United States in Washington, D.
C., in 1899, will probably never forget the
impression it has made and the benefits re-
ceived. To those who have never been pres-
ent the general uplifting cannot be too greatly
emphasized. The banding together of more
than a million women to influence the ac-
complishment of great purposes must neces-
sarily be a power in any given direction. It
is not so much individual as collective
power and the individual speeches made,
however brilliant, are not the predominating
influence. It is the united force of the whole
that gives such tremendous weight to the en-
tire effect. The propagation of the ideas em-
bodied in the platform of the Council express
forcibly in a few words what is expected, and
one can judge from that what the future will
be for all women who are willing to put forth


[Column 1]
a helping hand to advance this great forward
movement.
	The International Council, which takes
in all National Councils in all the countries
of the world is to hold its quinquennial ses-
sion next June in London. There the lead-
ing representatives of the National Council
will assemble to read papers, discuss subjects
and formulate plans for the general welfare of
women among all nations upon the globe.
Many crowned heads of kingdoms and em-
pires are becoming interested in, this Council
of nations, and will send representatives
duly commissioned as fraternal delegates,
where National Councils have not yet been
affiliated. To enlarge upon the facts here
set forth one might multiply pages illimitable,
but if women are alive to the awakening of
the times mentally and spiritually, they will
seek information and inspiration upon this
great upheaval of the higher activities of the
age. 
	The meetings of the Council this winter
and the business transacted have been of ex-
ceptional interest to the writer and for lack
of space even now we can only present the
following:
THE RESOLUTIONS REPORTED.
	Whereas the National Council of Women
cannot afford to lose from its Executive
Board a woman who has had the experience
of directing its policy as President; there-
fore be it
	Resolved, That a President of the Nation-
al Council, upon retiring from her office,
shall be made Honorary President of the
Council, with all powers and privileges, in-
cluding that of a vote upon the executive
committee; further be it
	Resolved, Should this resolution com-
mend itself to the judgment of the execu-
tive committee, that the names of Mary
Lowe Dickinson and May Wright Sewall,
The two living Presidents of the Council,
be immediately placed upon the official
staff, and that the constitution be so amended
as to include past Presidents as Honorary
Presidents within its limits; be it also
	Resolved, That no one except the Presi-
dent of the Council shall have either the
title or the privilege of an Honorary Presi-
dent.
	Resolved, We believe the state should
give to all children in schools under its con-
trol industrial and physical as well as
mental education.
	Resolved, That the National Council of
Women requests the government of the
United States how it may most largely
avail itself of the services of women in the
regulation of such matters pertaining to the
army and navy as the proper preparation of
food, sanitation, nursing, medical attendance,
etc. 
	Believing that the two strongest unifying
forces off organized effort are human brother-
hood and Divine Fatherhood, and rejoicing
in the expression of humanitarian princi-
ples in the manifold activities of American
women, represented by the National Coun-
cil of Women; therefore,
	Resolved, That this universal faith in the
Divine Fatherhood should be expressed by
either vocal or silent prayer at the opening
of all sessions of the Council.
	Resolved, That we protest against the
canteen system in our army and navy.
	Resolved, That we deprecate war, and
are in hearty sympathy with the purpose of
the conference called by the Czar of Russia

[Column 2]
for the gradual disarmament of the nations
of the world, believing that all difficulties
between civilized people may be settled
judicially by a high court of nations with-
out the sacrifice of life.
	Resolved, That the National Council of
Women of the United States send a com-
munication to President McKinley, re-
questing him to use his influence in en-
deavoring to put a stop to bull fights in
Cuba, on account of their extreme cruelty
and the demoralizing effect upon those who
witness them.
	Resolved, That in view of the wholesale
destruction and threatened extinction of
whole species of beautiful and useful song
birds for millinery purposes, and the conse-
quent rapid multiplication of insect pests,
and serious menace to the agricultural in-
terests of our country and the world, and
in view of the manifest inadequacy of mor-
al suasion to prevent the wearing of birds
and parts of birds on women’s bonnets, we
urge the passage of a United States law
prohibiting the sale by hunters or milliners
of any bird plumage obtained by the de-
struction, torture or injury of birds.
	Whereas the National Council of Women
of the United States stands for the highest
ideals of domestic and civic virtue, as well
as for the observance of law in all its de-
partments, both state and national, therefore
	Resolved, That no person should be al-
lowed to hold a place in any law making
body of the nation who is not a law abiding
citizen.
	Whereas the National Council of Women
of the United States is composed of many
organizations having diverse aims and
varied activities; and
	Whereas its own chief object is “the over-
throw of all forms of ignorance and injust-
ice” by the application of the Golden Rule
to society, custom and law; and
	Whereas this diversion in its constituency
and increasing number of its allied societies
tends more and more (under the present
form of procedure) toward the presentation
of resolutions and petitions too special in
character to be fully endorsed, and too great
in number to be properly discussed by the
Council as a whole; therefore
	Resolved, That this committee on reso-
lutions recommends that at succeeding ses-
sions of the National Council of Women of
the United States no resolution or petition
be presented except such as bear upon the
subjects, policy or work of the Council it-
self, in its internal administration or in its
relation as a body to other organizations,
or to some specific recommendation of the
Council in national or international enter-
prises or meetings.
SKETCH OF HAWAIIAN WOMEN.
President and Ladies of the National Coun-
		cil:
	I have come on behalf of my people, the
Hawaiian women, that they may be allowed
the same privileges as the women of America.
	Here I wish to say in the early days of
Hawaii the Hawaiian race were ignorant,
and like all other nationalities where civiliz-
ation had not reached, were savages. But
when the word of God was brought to us
in the year 1820 the Hawaiian race accepted
it and today they are equal in education,
intelligence and capability to the people of
the United States and all other nations of
the globe.

[Column 3]
	I have been present at some of your
meetings and find that the women of this
country are working for the advancement
of the human race, more especially for the
rights of women. If the right to vote is
accorded to the women of this country, I
hope and pray that the same privileges may
be granted to the Hawaiian women, and I
ask of the National Council of Women
whose noble souls prompted them to be
present at these meetings, that they will
give me all the aid in their power to for-
ward the fulfillment of my petition.
				HANNAH KAAEPA.


[Column 1]
MEMORABLE ANNIVERSARY.

	The 17th of March, anniversary of the
first organization of the Relief Society,
will be generally observed throughout the
organization in some way commemorative
of that auspicious occasion, and no doubt
the sisters will try to review in some way
the work accomplished during the fifty
seven years, though very few are living
now who were present then. Only one
that we can recall at this present moment,
that one is Sister Bathsheba W. Smith, who
was at that time not quite twenty years old.
	The assistant secretary appointed on that
occasion, Sister Phebe M. Wheeler, has
died during the last few months at Bounti-
ful at a great age; we have not yet received

[Column 2]
the notice with a sketch of her life, but ex-
pect to publish it in the “Exponent.” It
will certainly be of special interest.
	For the benefit of those not acquainted
with the circumstances of the first organiza-
tion and particulars, etc., we republished in
the February 1, 1899, number, the minutes
as prepared by Sister Eliza R. Snow, the
secretary appointed at that time, and who
carefully prepared them from the record
for publication in the first numbers of the
Woman’s Exponent, June 1, and 15, 1872.
We hope most sincerely that the sisters
will preserve these minutes and make them
a part of the record kept of the society in
their respective wards, that hereafter they
may have them for reference. If the sisters
would be more choice of the “Exponent,”
they might have information at hand on
many subjects when occasion came that
items were needed for use. Every society
should have it for the benefit of those who
cannot afford to take it, and every president
should have it for her own use, in order
that she may be posted on general society
matters, and because of any instructions
given through its columns from the presid-
ing board.
	The 17th of March marks an event of
great interest to the sisters and to all wo-
men, and we know why and how it is so
very important an epoch in the history of
the world. Since that time hundreds and
perhaps thousands of women’s organiza-
tions have sprung up on both continents, in
many countries and upon the islands of the
sea: some benevolent like our own, some
for reform, some literary, historical, genalog-
ical, scientific, political and religious.
	All the various associations and societies
have vast influence in the world towards
enlightenment, refinement and culture.
Many great reforms have been wrought
through the efforts of the women along
these lines, and what may we not expect in
the next half century of progressive work.
	Of the Relief Society great things will
certainly be expected, such as promoting
more substantially the silk industry and
other like enterprises, so as to employ a
large number of women and children, that
the young generation may not be allowed
to grow up in idleness, and be a tax upon
the community.
	More houses will be built suitable for
aged women so as to avoid paying rent, for
building material may be obtained even
without much cash, or at any rate with
economy in disposing of articles made at
working meetings; building material may
thus be procured and work on houses paid 
for. This has been done already in
some places where there has been judicious
management by those in charge of affairs.
	If the Relief Society stands, as it should,
first of woman’s organizations, there should
be a bureau of information with suitable
(not extravagant) headquarters where
strangers and women from all parts of the
world might converse with the women of
the society upon such matters as pertain to
the welfare of humanity, and get correct
views and opinions of the work in which
the women of the Latter-day Saints are en-
gaged. May that good time hasten on and
the sisters feel their labors are in the line
of progress, and the society become in real-
ity what has been expected by those who
laid the foundation of this organization, and
worked so faithfully until called to labor
in a higher sphere. In this connection we 
may mention reverently Sisters Eliza R.
Snow, Elizabeth Ann Whitney, Phebe

[Column 3]
Woodruff, Sarah M. Kimball, Mercy R.
Thompson, Marinda M. Hyde, Elizabeth
Howard and many others whose names will
stand in golden letters on the Roll of Honor.


OFFICIAL ANNOUNCEMENT. 

	During the early part of April there will 
be issued by the Deseret News a new 
Church work entitled “The Articles of
Faith,” the same being a series of lectures 
on the principal doctrines of the Church of 
Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints, by Dr. 
James E. Talmage. The lectures were
prepard by appointment of the First Presi-
dency, and the book will be published by 
the Church. It is intended for use as a 
text book in Church schools, Sunday
schools, Improvement associations, quorums 
of the Priesthood and other Church organi-
zations in which the study of Theology is 
pursued, and also for individual use among 
the members of the Church. The work has 
been approved by the First Presidency, and
I heartily commend it to the members of 
the Church.
				LORENZO SNOW.

DAUGHTERS OF THE REVOLUTION.

	At the annual meeting of the State Soci-
ety of the Daughters of the Revolution held
Friday, March 8, at 2 p. m. at the hand-
some residence of Mrs. Philo T. Farnsworth,
139 E. South Temple St., this city, a
large number of the members were present
and a few invited guests. The parlors were
elaborately draped and decorated for the oc-
casion with red, white and blue in all avail-
able places, and the dear old flag itself was
most conspicuous of all. Everything be-
tokened a gala occasion. The meeting was
held in the library opening out of the par-
lor where flags and colors were abundant.
Prayer was offered by Mrs. George A.
Smith, one of the most distinguished wo-
men in Utah. Piano music, “The Star
Spangled Banner,” by Miss Olea Shipp.
Minutes of meetings were read by the re-
cording secretary, Miss Julia Farnsworth,
also letters from the general secretary by
the corresponding secretary, Lucy W. Smith.
Music by Miss Shipp, “Red White and
Blue March.” A recess was taken and the
ladies repaired to the dining room, which
like the parlors had been draped with the
national colors. On the tables which were
artistically arranged were sweet-scented
flowers, snowy linen and dainty china, all
added to the delicious menu prepared for
the delectation of the guests. Mrs. Farns-
worth as hostess anticipated every wish and
the hour spent at the banquet was most de-
lightfully entertaining. Miss Farnsworth
was a charming toast mistress and displayed
great tactfulness in introducing each toast
and giving something original of the subject.
The veterans of the Revolution were not for-
gotten, nor were the mothers and “Our Flag”
was royally toasted.
	After the banquet, meeting was called to
order for the consideration of other matters
and Miss Shipp favored the company with a
song, La Separazione, (Rossine) prayer by
the chaplain, and an adjournment was taken
to the regular meeting, March 16, at 3 p.m.,
Templeton Building.
	All, both members and guests, expressed
themselves gratified with the success of the
first annual meeting.

DAVIS STAKE.

	Minutes of the Davis Stake Relief Soci-
ety conference held at East Bountiful Jan.
26, 1899, President Susan Grant presiding.
Meeting opened by singing “Glorious
things are sung of Zion.” Prayer by
Patriarch Judson Tolman. Singing, “How
great the joy that promised day.” Present
from Salt Lake City were Sisters Elizabeth
J. Stevenson and Dr. Ellis R. Shipp. 
	President Grant said, “I wish to welcome
President Lizzie Smith and the young ladies.
I am pleased to have them meet with us in
conference. 
	Minutes of forenoon session of previous
conference were read and approved. Re-
ports read and accepted.
	Sister Stevenson addressed the meeting.
She fully realized what a great privilege it
was to meet together. We sisters had had
this privilege only since the Prophet Joseph
Smith had organized the Relief Society.
It was the duty of every sister to attend
these meetings and prepare herself for a
higher probation. “When I hear of so 
many going into forbidden paths I wonder
if the mothers have done their duty.  It is
just as necessary for our sons to be virtuous
as for our daughters. We should take an
interest in our sons and daughters, make
home pleasant and provide suitable amuse-
ment for them. The spirit of God cannot
dwell in unholy places.”
	Patriarch John Kynaston said, “I do re-
joice in being here. I feel full of the spirit
of the Lord. What we have heard has
been dictated by the spirit of God. If you
put these things into practice the Lord will
bless you. I say unto these young sisters
you will be blessed in bearing the sons and 
daughters of God. The Lord wants this
people to become one, and prepare the way
for His coming.”
	President Lizzie Smith said, “It affords
me great pleasure to see so many of the
brethren present and to be with you. I
think it is nice to join our societies, as we
all expect to be Relief Society workers at
some time. I was not born when the
Prophet was killed but I think it made an
impression on me. I think children are
influenced very young, even before they
are born. If the mothers would go to the
Lord with their troubles instead of the
neighbors, it would be a great deal better.
My advice is for us to put into practice the
many good things we have heard. I ask the
Lord to bless us all.”
	Bishop Egan said, “I have been pleased
with what I have heard, I think it has
been dictated by the spirit of God. We
have different influences to contend with,
the good and the evil. We should be on
our guard so we will not give way to the
evil influences. It is very necessary and it
is our right, if we live for it, to have the
spirit of discernment.”
	President Susan Grant said, “I have been
very much pleased with the remarks. I am
sorry President Hess was not able to meet
with us, but he sent his blessing.”
	Meeting adjourned until 2 p. m. Sing-
ing “The spirit of God like a fire is burn-
ing.” Benediction by Brother Clark.
	Afternoon session opened by singing
“Praise to the man.” Prayer by Brother
Duarden. Singing, “Prayer is the soul’s
sincere desire.” Minutes of previous after-
noon session read and approved.
	President Grant said, “I am pleased to
see so many present, may the blessing of

[Column 2]
the Lord rest upon all who may speak.”
	Sister Shipp then addressed the meeting.
She said, “I desire an interest in your faith
and prayers, for I desire to talk more especi-
ally to the young ladies and encourage them
to attend these meetings, for they are a
blessing to all of us.” Spoke of love and
charity, said we should all try and cultivate
these qualities. The mothers have a great
influence over their children. A woman’s
geatest mission is to instruct her children
in the proper way. We should all try to
control our children with a spirit of love.
Spoke of our Savior and the spirit of for-
giveness. Said, “Let us try to follow His
example, seek to know and do our duty at
all times and be prepared for eternal life.”
	Patriarch Judson Tolman said, “I will
tell you, my sisters, you are numbered
among those who are reserved to redeem
the world. We can all see the great neces-
sity of doing right and living our religion.
I tell you not only the young men but the
young women will be called upon to go on
missions. I bless you, my sisters, and
your children.”
	Sister Emily Parish said, “I realize so
much is required of us, but we should ask
the Lord what is most necessary to be done.”
Sister Caddie Parish said, “Where much
is given much is required, and none of us
lack for opportunities of improvement. I
trust we will all follow the good examples
of our parents.”
	Counselor Hyrum Grant said, “I am
satisfied the spirit of the Lord is with us to-
day. The only desire I have is that I may
have a portion of it while I attempt to
speak. We should teach our children to
love and respect the authorities. Parents
should be very careful in speaking before
their children. I feel to say God bless this
and all other societies.”
	Sister Mary Clark said, “It is our duty to
ask the Lord for His blessing and what we
most need. I have been to the Lord for com-
fort and have been comforted. I feel to
bear my testimony to the Gospel.”
	Sister Stevenson said, “When our Patri-
archs speak of the degradation it makes me
feel like putting my shoulder to the wheel
and helping to regenerate the world. It is
the privilege and duty of the young ladies to
join these societies and prepare themselves
for eternal life.”
	President Susan Grant said, “I do not
think there is one in the house who has not
been greatly edified.” Spoke of the sis-
ters’ meeting on the 17th of March. “I
feel to ask the blessing of the Lord upon
all, I think we have had a day of rejocing.”
	Conference adjourned for three months to
be held at Centerville. Singing, “Guide
us O, Thou Great Jehovah.” Benediction
by Lewis Grant.
		SUSAN GRANT, Pres.
		PHEBE C. SESSIONS, Sec.


EDITORIAL NOTES.

	A new book called “The Primary Help-
er” has been completed under the direction
of the General Board of the Primary Associ-
ation, and is now for sale. It is intended
for the use of the children in their exercises
in the association, and for a help when get-
ting up entertainments. It contains both
prose and poetry, most of the articles are
original and written especially for the chil-
dren of Zion. Great care has been taken
to have nothing objectionable contained in
any of the pieces, and the associations and
Latter-day Saints generally should procure
copies for the use of the children and those
who labor among and with them. The
book is published by George Q. Cannon
& Sons Co., price per copy, 15c[-]s.
	A book on Bookkeeping by John W.
Whinyates of this city has been handed to
us, and we take pleasure in giving it a brief
notice in our columns, as it seems to rec-
ommend itself to the student as being clear
in method and easy in comprehension of
the subject dealt with. It contains speci-
mens of the books used in single and double
entry. In this age of hurry any practical
theory that will facilitate work, is certainly
a blessing, and it seems this little book
will be very helpful to those who transact
business, much, or little, as the case may be.
Women who are puzzled in keeping their
accounts and who cannot afford to take les-
sons will be able to learn all that is requi-
site without a teacher, from this small book
of 62 pages, price $1.00
	We have received from the Deseret Sun-
day School Union the pamphlet of the
“Proceedings of the First Sunday School
Convention, of the Church of Jesus Christ
of Latter-day Saints,” containing the en-
tire report as taken in short hand. It is
exceedingly interesting and will be a great
benefit to the organization, both for
the present and the future, and the address
of President George Q. Cannon, General
Superintendent, and of First Ass’t. Super-
intendent Elder George Goddard, will be
priceless in value to the Sunday School
Union, as also those of other speakers. The
convention was in every sense of the word
a perfect success, The teaching contained
in the book will be invaluable to teachers,
and in the form it is issued convenient for
reference. Price, 10cts., published by the
Deseret Sunday School Union, 408, and 409
Templeton Building, Salt Lake City, Utah.

For the “Exponent.”
A SKETCH OF THE JAREDITES.

	To a Latter-day Saint there is no more
interesting subject to study than the Book
of Mormon. The history of the doings and
wanderings of the aborigines of the Ameri-
can continent, or the roaming Indians of to-
day, the descendants of Father Lehi and
his associates, who left Jerusalem by the
Lord’s command 600 years B. C.
	Lehi and his little company, however,
were not the first colonists who landed in

[Column 2]
the western hemisphere. We read in the
Book of Mormon of a very ancient peo-
ple called the Jaredites, the founder of
which was Jared. He was supposed to be
one of the builders of the Tower of Babel,
and is presumed to be a descendant of Shem.
Little is told in the Book of Ether of the
private character of Jared, but he must
have been a great and good man and also
held the priesthood when God scattered
and confounded the languages of these pre-
sumptuous builders. Jared and his broth-
ers plead with Him that their language and
that of their friends might not be changed.
Their prayer was heard and the Lord
promised to lead them to a new land, choice
above all others, when He would make of
them a great and mighty people, and He
would go before them as their guide.
	In obedience to the command of the Lord
Jared with his brother and their families
and friends gathered their flocks, herds and
seeds of various kinds, and commenced their
journey in the wilderness as the Lord
might lead. They were going to a new
land far away from their eastern home,
across the ocean to a land uninhabited by
mortal man.
	They appear to have collected every
thing that could have been of any use to
them that they could carry. They took
hives of honey bees (known to them by the
name Deseret.)
	The valley into which the Lord first led
them they called Nimrod. Here they tar-
ried for a time, preparing for the long
journey that lay before them. Their wan-
derings were long and tedious, but at last
they reached the great ocean. The place
where they tarried they named Moriancu-
mer. Here they remained for four years,
at the end of which time the Lord visited
the brother of Jared (Mahonry Moriancumer)
in a cloud and chastened him and his breth-
ren, because they had grown careless and
neglected to call upon Him. Repentance
followed this reproof, and their sins were
forgiven them.
	While here the brother of Jared was com-
manded by the Lord to build eight barges.
This command he obeyed with the assis-
tance of the company. The vessels were
small and light in construction. When
they were completed Jared and his people
with their animals and all their belongings
went on board. After a stormy voyage of
three hundred and forty-four days they
reached their destination. It is supposed
they landed on the southern shore of the
Gulf of California.
	How thankful they were that they had
reached their new home! They gave
thanks to God for bringing them safely to
the land of promise.
	Their next duty was to prepare for the
future. They commenced to till the soil
and were greatly blessed in their labors.
They were a righteous people and began to
grow and increase in number and in wealth.
	In course of time Jared and his brother
grew old and died. Before their death the
people wished to be governed by a king
and Orihah, the youngest son of Jared, was
chosen. He reigned in righteousness and
wisdom, instructing the people in the ways
of the Lord. He lived to a very great age
and was succeeded by his son Kib, who in
his old age resigned his kingdom to his son
Shule. At one time during the reign of
Kib he was taken into captivity by his son
Corihor, and kept a prisoner for many years.
	Shule also reigned in righteousness
and was a great and mighty man, yet his


[Column 1]
Sisters Cynthia E. Packard and Sylvia
Griffin, counselors; Sister Alice Neves Dob-
son, secretary, and Sister Margaret A. Prea-
tor treasurer.
	We have but few members as our ward
is small, but all are interested and are ener-
getic workers so far as they are able. As
we are rather scattered on our farms, there
being no towns, some of the sisters living
farthest away having three miles to come,
our meetings are not held as often nor are
they as well attended as we would wish, and
it is a difficult matter for our teachers to
visit every month. As we hold our meet-
ings monthly we have very good meetings
when we do meet, and are always blest
with a goodly portion of the spirit of God.
The sisters are full of love and charity to-
ward each other, and try to follow the ex-
ample set them by their worthy president.
	Trusting these few lines will find room
in your excellent paper, I remain,
Your sister in the Gospel,
SYLVIA GRIFFIN.
ST. JOHN'S STAKE.
	A special conference of the Relief Society
of the St. John's Stake of Zion was held at
St. Johns, Arizona, Feb. 20, 1899. On
the stand were Dr. Karl G. Maeser, Elder
L. John Nuttall, President E. L. S. Udall,
her first counselor and some leading breth-
ren and sisters.
	After the usual opening exercises Presi-
dent Udall in her remarks said: "This is
not the usual time for holding our confer-
ence, but on account of Brother Nuttall
being here in the interest of the society we
have this privilege." Said she had visited
each society in the Stake since our last con-
ference.
	Elder L. John Nuttall said he had the
special mission of looking after the Relief
Society, given him while our beloved Presi-
dent Woodruff was living. "The property
rights of the society here are not in the
safe condition they are in and near Utah.
***The instructions I give you to day
will only be preparatory for the time when
I shall be permitted to visit you in your re-
spective wards, as the time now is too
limited. I have found in my travels that
the sisters do not understand the object of
the organization fully. The Prophet Joseph
on the first day of its organization said, 'It
is to provoke your husbands to good works,
to correct morals and strengthen virtue.'
My sisters do you realize there is anything
for you to do in this organization to cor-
rect these evil conditions? Now is the time
to put down iniquity, correct the morals
and strengthen the virtues of the children.
You can qualify yourselves to expound the
scriptures by giving lectures, reading, etc.
Take certain passages of scripture and
speak upon them. We are living in an ad-
vanced age and must keep up with the
times. Take care of your wheat, store it
up for a time of famine and pestilence, so
when this time comes, as it will come, the
grain you have stored away will increase
with you and the power of the adversary
will fail in this direction."
	Dr. Karl G. Maeser said, "I rise only to
bear testimony to the beautiful instructions
given by Brother Nuttall. If you will
abide by these instructions and put them
into practice you will indeed be saviors up-
on Mount Zion."
	President William Gibbons said we
should carry the instructions given here
this morning to our homes in the several
wards, that all the people might have the
benefit of them.

[Column 2]
	President E. L. S. Udall said, "We have
had a spiritual feast this morning. Confer-
ence will now be adjourned for six months."
	The choir sang and benediction was pro-
nounced by Brother Wm. Gibbons.
MARY E. B. FARR, Sec.
WEBER STAKE.
	Minutes of the quarterly conference of
the Relief Society of Weber Stake, held in
the 3rd ward meeting house, Ogden, March
9, 1899, President Jane S. Richards presid-
ing. Singing, "Come let us anew." Prayer
by Patriarch Thomas Morley. Continued
by singing "Come O, Thou King of kings."
Minutes of the December conference were
read and accepted.
	President Jane S. Richards hoped the
house would be crowded today, so much so
that all would realize the need of a larger
one, some had remarked that the sisters
were extravagant in wanting a house of
their own. Said she had seen many faces
since last meeting with us, but none were
so dear to her as the Saints of God. Spoke
of her visit to California and her meeting
with the Saints there, and of the holy,
peaceful influence that they enjoy in their
little meetings. It was indeed like a love
feast to drink in the sweet spirit that was
present, and to feel the firm hand shakes
and hear the fervent "God bless you" was
indeed a benediction. Many there who
long since left the fold were coming back to
us, which made her heart rejoice. She had
nothing but praise for the Saints of Cali-
fornia, such a spirit of unity was in their
midst. This same spirit was present with
us this morning, this was what we desired.
Some of the later words of our Lord and
Master were that we should "be one, as He
and His Father were one." Referred to
the Relief Society meeting held in the
Templeton, and the resolutions adopted
there on the observance of the anniversary
of the organization of the Relief Society,
and the annual membership fee to be col-
lected, and the need of the same.
	Brother D. H. Peery was pleased to meet
with the sisters, could endorse what sister
Richards had said in regard to the good in-
fluence that is felt among the missionaries
out in the world. Felt that the mission
of the sisters was great, but that they
would prove themselve equal to the duties;
they were more noble in purpose than were
the brethren. "Peace is almost leaving the
earth, men are losing faith in each other
and a spirit of malice and hatred is taking
the place of love. This is not right, we
should cultivate a spirit of charity and love.
We have come here to be hid in the cham-
bers of the Lord while His judgments are
abroad, and this should be a land of peace.
We are on the eve of the greatest events
that have ever transpired." Thought the
sisters should leave behind them a monu-
ment in the shape of a house.
	Sister Zina D. H. Young addressed the
sisters. Said to her the faces of the Saints
were the most beautiful picture she could
look upon. Her desire was to comfort and
bless the sisters. She had never had a
doubt of the divinity of this work, had re-
ceived a testimony when a child, and felt
herself the happiest person in the world.
"The Lord has been so merciful to us and
the Gospel is so grand and so glorious,
there is in it such an inspiration, a power,
a peace that the world knows nothing of.
It is a very foretaste of heaven, a preparing
for that change—the reign of peace—it is
so soul-satisfying that we have no room

[Column 3]
for hard feelings." In looking back to the
days of Kirtland and seeing the prophet and
the Temple, her soul was stirred and she
rejoiced more than ever in this grand and
glorious work. Urged the sisters to read
the Book of Mormon and the Doctrine and
Covenants. "Let your children see and
hear you read them and know that you en-
joy them, and you and they will be blessed."
Referred to her own mother and her read-
ing of the Book of Mormon just previous to
her death, and the calm resignation she ex-
hibited when reading the words, "I bid
you all farewell," knowing that it was her
own as well as the prophet's farewell to
earth. Said we should never speak disre-
spectfully of the priesthood, nor allow our
children to. We should feel that we have
no trouble, everything is beauty, grandeur
and harmony, even the peace that passeth
all understanding. The prophet told the
sisters they were to bless each other. "Let
us banish from our hearts everything that
is evil, and just as we prepare ourselves to
receive blessings, so they will increase.
May the Lord grant unto us the spirit of
inspiration, peace, joy and everything
glorious through Jesus."
	Brother Joseph Hall had greatly rejoiced
in the remarks of President Zina D. H.
Young, and knew that upon her rested the
spirit of the prophet and of the living God.
The sentiments she had uttered were grand
and enobling. Gave good instructions and
exhorted all to faithfulness.
	Sister Mary Lightner felt that the spirit
of the Lord was present. "We as Saints
should set good examples, we are as a
light set upon a hill. Men have testified
that there is a power among this people
that they cannot fathom. We should honor
the priesthood of the Lord, remembering
the fate of Aaron and his sister, beware of
our doings, speak no evil, but live to be
worthy of every blessing that the Lord has
in store for us." Told of a recent visit
with a Josephite, who tried to prove that
Joseph was a fallen prophet, how she was
able by the Holy Spirit to refute the same
and prove that he was what we claim, and
that his prophecies were daily being fulfilled.
Bore a powerful testimony to the divinity
of this work.
	Sister Emily Richards believed that in
confessing our faults we gained a blessing
that we could gain in no other way. Re-
ferred to her recent sickness and the time it
had given her for reflection. Wondered
what good deed she had ever done without
some selfish motive. These sick seasons
are precious moments to us, in which we
approach nearer to our Heavenly Father.
While in this mood Sister Zina had visited
her, bringing with her the spirit of health,
which is in her very touch. Knew the
spirit of the prophet rested upon her, and
that she was "blessed above women."
Referred to the reading of the Book of
Mormon and Doctrine and Covenants, and
the peace and joy such reading brings into
our lives. We should attend Relief Society
meetings and learn from our aged sisters
while they are still with us. Spoke of her
Washington experience and how the Lord
had aided her, that she had been able to
stem the tide of opposition that she had
met; also spoke of our sisters at the Wo-
man's Council, and the good they had
accomplished.
	Singing, Doxology. Benediction by
Bishop John Alred.
ACT. SEC.


[Column 1]
days were full of sorrow. He was succeeded
on the throne by the son of his old age,
Omer. Omer was a righteous but unfor-
tunate king. He lived to be exceedingly
old and two years before his death anoint-
ed his son Emer to reign in his stead.
	Emer was one of the best kings of his
race. In his reign the people prospered,
became owners of large herds of useful
animals and rich in manufactured goods.
Sixty-two years are numbered as his reign,
and he died full of years and honor. He
was succeeded by one of his sons, Corian-
lum, whom he had anointed four years
before his death.
	Coriantum was a righteous ruler, and
during his reign the people were blessed
and prospered and many large cities were
built. He lived until he was one hundred
and forty-two years old, and was succeeded
on the throne by his son Com.
	Com, the son of Coriantum, during his
reign the people increased greatly in num-
bers and spread widely over the land; but
they also grew in iniquity, and secret com-
binations that a few generations before had
almost caused the destruction of the race,
were revived. One of the leaders of these
bands was a son of Com’s named Heth.
This young man with others conspired
against the king, and Heth slew him with
his sword and reigned in his stead.
	Heth proved a cruel and vicious king,
and the people increased in wickedness.
The Lord sent many prophets among them
who called upon them to repent, declaring
if they did not a terrible famine should
come upon the land; but their words were
rejected and they were cruelly persecuted.
	The people still persisted in their iniquity
and the famine came as predicted, and
the horrors that attended can scarcely be
imagined. Disease followed and the peo-
ple rapidly perished. Then those who re-
mained began to repent and called upon the
Lord, and when they had sufficiently hum-
bled themselves the Lord sent the long
needed rain and the remnants of the race be-
gan to revive, then Shez, the only survivor
of the royal house, reigned over the few
that remained. Shez was a virtuous mon-
arch and taught his people righteousness,
and they were greatly prospered. He lived
to a great age and at his death was succeed-
ed by his youngest son, Riplakish.
	Riplakish greatly afflicted his people by
imposing on them heavy taxes, and when
they could not pay them cast them into
prison, where he compelled them to labor
to sustain him in his luxury and wicked-
ness. If any prisoner refused he was put
to death. For forty-two years the people
endured these oppressions, they then re-
belled, rose, and in their anger slew the
king and drove his descendants out of the
land,and what form of government followed
is not known, but after many years one of
his descendants, Morianton,established him-
self as king.
	Morianton was mild in disposition though
energetic, and during his reign the people
prospered and the nation grew exceedingly
rich. He lived to a very great age and
eight years before his death abdicated in
favor of his son Kim. Kim was a wicked
king and the Lord was so displeased with
him that he permitted his brother to rebel
against him and dethrone him, and held
him in captivity the remainder of his life.
	Levi, the son of Kim, when he was forty- 
two years old rose in rebellion and deposed
his uncle, who occupied the throne. He
was a righteous king and the people

[Column 2]
prospered under his reign, and at his death
was succeeded by his son Corom.
	Corom was a good king all his days, and
he died at a very advanced age, when his
son Kish ascended the throne. Nothing is
known of his character, but at his death
his son Lib succeeded him.
	Lib lived many years, was blessed with
a numerous posterity, and the sacred his-
torian Ether writes: "Never could be a
people more blessed than they, and more
prospered by the Lord.” They greatly ad-
vanced in the arts of civilization, they im-
proved in the manufacture of goods, agri-
culture and mining. When Lib died he
was succeeded by his son Hearthom.
	After Hearthom had reigned twenty-
four years the kingdom was wrested from
him and he was held in captivity the re-
mainder of his life. Nothing is known of
his character. For some time the kingdom
was ruled by usurpers.
	The next rightful heir to the throne was
Aaron, a son of Heth and a grandson of
Hearthom the king. He, with his grand-
father and father Heth and his son Amni-
gaddah, and his son Coriantum also, were
kept in captivity by the usurpers, but in
after years the kingdom was reconquered
and the dynasty restored.
	After a lengthy war Com, the son of
Coriantum, regained possession of the king-
dom. At this time it was divided, one
half being governed by Amghid, but after
a desolating war of years Com gained
power over the whole realm. While he
was king robber bands began to appear and
sought to destroy the kingdom. Com
fought these bands valiantly without suc-
cess, for the people generally were in sym-
pathy with them. Many prophets were
sent of the Lord who foretold the destruc-
tion of the race if they did not repent of
their wickedness. But they heeded not
the voice of warning, their words were re-
jected and the wicked Jaredites sought the
lives of the men of God. The king pro-
tected the prophets and while they were
with him they prophecied many things for
his comfort, and he was blessed of the Lord
the remainder of his days. He lived to a
good old age and was succeeded to the
throne by his son Shiblon.
	(Continued.)


WOMAN'S EXPONENT.


[Column 1]
NOTICE TO ALL THOSE INTER-
      ESTED IN SILK CULTURE IN
	   THE STATE OF UTAH.
	The Utah Silk Commission desires to
call attention to the fact that the time for
raising silk is again upon us, and we trust
that those who intend entering upon the
work this year will write to us at once for
eggs. Those who are taking up the work
for the first time will kindly state the num-
ber and size of the trees from which they
can obtain leaves; also the number of per-
sons who can give their time during the
latter period of the raising season, so that
we will be able to send the necessary
amount of eggs.
	We also desire to state that eggs can
be obtained from the commission free, and
we would prefer every one who is going
to raise the worms to have eggs from us as
we provide a quality which gives a much
better result than those raised by people
having little or no experience. We will
also send pamphlet of instructions free to
any one wishing information upon the sub-
ject.

[Column 2]
   We trust that many people throughout
the state have already planted numbers of
mulberry trees, and have been watchful
lest any of those which have grown are in
danger of being destroyed. We also wish
to say to those who have trees and do not
care to use them that they may do a great deal
towards establishing this important indus-
try by allowing others to use the leaves,
and we assure you that it will only benefit
the trees even if they are stripped several
times, they will soon become much more
thrifty, and this will also be a means of dis-
posing of the fruit which is sometimes an
annoyance especially when trees are planted
on the street. The best mulberry seed can
also be obtained from the commission.
	As a whole, the commission feels very
much encouraged with the results of last
year’s work, and trust it will be very much
increased this year, in all the departments
of work. Address all communications to
			MARGARET A. CAINE,
Secretary Utah Silk Commission, Salt
Lake City, Utah.
